i am building an intelligence engine for my academic coach , i am of the view that the engine itself which comprises of a couple of engines should in total be about 100,000 lines of code at least per module however the whole intelligence engine comprising all the engines is jus a mare 10,000+ , so i want you to analyse and brainstorm 

I’m going through the engine audit against your concern about scale versus actual intelligence depth, then I’ll convert that into a sharper build strategy rather than just a lines-of-code comparison.

I’ve confirmed the current engine is algorithmically real and not toy logic, but it is still mostly a decision-and-orchestration layer, not yet the full cognitive substrate of a world-class academic coach. That distinction is the real issue, not the raw LOC by itself.

Thought for 38s
Yes — and your instinct is directionally right.

From the audit, what you currently have is not “nothing.” It is already a serious decision layer: 6 engines, 53 named algorithms, real persistence, and cross-engine feedback into planning. The total audited code is 12,068 lines, with Topic Intelligence alone at 3,996 lines, plus Coverage, Sequencing, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation engines behind it. 

 


But the deeper truth is this:

12k lines is enough for a strong orchestration brain. It is not enough for the full intelligence civilization you are imagining.

Your current system mainly does:

diagnosis and decision selection,

prioritization and sequencing,

timing and review logic,

risk protection,

adaptation based on past outcomes,

planner feedback. 

 


That is powerful, but it is still mostly a strategy layer.

What you are imagining is much bigger: a coach that can detect obscure causes, form and revise theories, test those theories, understand content deeply, model contradictions between ideas, notice pressure effects, infer hidden weaknesses, compose interventions dynamically, and learn what works per learner over long horizons. Your current audit already hints at some of that through the 3-layer hypothesis system, evidence spine, mastery proof contract, retest logic, risk taxonomy, and adaptation profile, but those are still compact versions of much larger problem spaces. 

 


My assessment
Your engine is currently under-scaled for your ambition, not because 12k is “bad,” but because the intelligence surface you want is far wider than the current engine boundaries.

Right now, the engines are mostly answering:

what topic,

in what order,

with what teaching mode,

at what time,

under what risk,

when to adapt. 

 


But a truly elite academic coach also needs to answer:

what exactly is broken in the student’s cognition, and why?

what hidden variable is corrupting performance?

is the problem knowledge, retrieval, interference, pressure, confidence, misclassification, shallow proof, representation mismatch, or contradictory encoding?

what evidence would falsify our current hypothesis?

what exact intervention sequence should run next?

what should never be shown now because it would destabilize learning?

what content artifact needs to be generated or fetched to test the hypothesis?

what competing explanations remain plausible?

what changed in the learner model after this session?

That gap is the missing mass.

Why it feels too small
The current engine has depth in each area, but each area is still compressed.

Example:
Topic Intelligence has an 18-step pipeline, 7 hypothesis families, 9 teaching modes, a 6-checkpoint proof contract, and a 17-metric evidence spine. That is very good for a first serious engine. But for the kind of coach you are describing, this single engine could easily expand into several distinct internal engines by itself. 


In other words:

You do not have one “too-small engine.”
You have the early nucleus of a much larger intelligence stack.

The real mistake to avoid
The mistake would be to think the answer is simply “make each module 100k LOC.”

That would be a dangerous framing.

A better framing is:

Each current engine should evolve into a family of sub-engines.

So instead of:

Topic Intelligence = one 4k module

You eventually get:

Topic Diagnosis Engine

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Misconception Graph Engine

Evidence Interpretation Engine

Teaching Strategy Engine

Mastery Proof Engine

Contradiction/Interference Engine

Topic Memory Engine

Topic Simulation/Test Engine

That is how you get to 100k+ meaningfully.

What is missing from the current stack
Here is the missing surface area I would add.

1. Diagnostic Inference Engine
This should become far deeper than “7 families of causes.”

It should model:

latent weakness states,

competing explanations,

confidence intervals on each explanation,

what evidence is missing,

what test should be run next to disambiguate,

falsification logic,

hypothesis decay,

hypothesis resurrection.

This is the engine that says:
“Maybe she knows the concept, but only in untimed conditions.”
“Maybe she confuses two nearby concepts when representation changes.”
“Maybe her error rate spikes only after 4 consecutive questions.”
“Maybe the weakness is not topic weakness but stamina collapse.”

This alone could be a very large subsystem.

2. Misconception and Interference Engine
Your current system touches contrast pairs and interference separation, but that is still light relative to the full problem. 


You need a full engine for:

concept collisions,

near-neighbor confusion,

representation interference,

formula contamination,

definition blending,

procedure substitution,

transfer illusions,

contradiction chains.

This engine should maintain a confusion graph, not just pairs.

3. Learner Cognitive State Engine
Right now the system reads performance evidence. Good.
But you also need a durable learner-state model with things like:

pressure response profile,

confidence fragility profile,

recovery speed,

fatigue signature,

hint addiction tendency,

memory half-life by content type,

abstraction tolerance,

response to visual vs verbal vs procedural teaching,

resilience after failure,

transfer elasticity.

Adaptation Intelligence starts pointing there, but it is not yet a complete learner-state engine. 


4. Session Composer Engine
The current engine decides modes and priorities, but you need an engine that actually composes the session:

open with retrieval or not,

whether to stabilize confidence first,

whether to isolate one concept or compare two,

when to inject worked example,

when to switch representation,

when to stop because overload is rising,

when to insert a “proof checkpoint,”

when to pivot into timed pressure,

when to end with a reinforcement window task.

That is different from ranking topics.

5. Content Intelligence Engine
A true coach brain cannot depend only on pre-existing content.

It needs an engine that can:

understand curriculum,

map topics to concept atoms,

fetch or generate notes,

generate discriminative questions,

generate contrast questions,

generate pressure probes,

generate proof tasks,

generate retrieval cards,

identify missing content coverage,

rate content quality,

select content by learner state.

Your risk engine already references content quality lightly, but this needs to become a first-class intelligence layer. 


6. Experimentation Engine
The coach should sometimes behave like a scientist.

It should run controlled probes:

timed vs untimed,

isolated vs mixed,

visual vs symbolic,

worked example first vs independent attempt first,

single concept vs contrast pair,

easy-to-hard vs hard-first,

low-pressure vs pressure-conditioning.

The goal is not just teaching.
The goal is diagnostic experimentation.

7. Long-Horizon Memory Engine
Current timing and adaptation use history, yes. But a real coach needs persistent memory at multiple levels:

topic memory,

misconception memory,

intervention memory,

learner tendency memory,

exam behavior memory,

recovery memory,

parent feedback memory,

content artifact memory.

The engine should remember not just “what happened,” but:

what pattern recurs,

what tends to work after failure,

what destabilizes the learner,

what is slowly improving,

what only appears fixed.

8. Meta-Evaluation Engine
You need an engine that judges the coach itself.

Questions like:

Was the diagnosis correct?

Was the selected teaching mode optimal?

Was the session too early for timing pressure?

Did the planner over-prioritize risk and under-prioritize confidence?

Did the system confuse weak recall with weak understanding?

Was the intervention effective because of the method or because the question got easier?

This is how the coach becomes self-correcting.

9. Content Coverage and Knowledge Graph Engine
Coverage Intelligence is good, but it is currently portfolio-oriented. 


You still need a deeper knowledge graph engine that handles:

curriculum to topic to concept decomposition,

prerequisite strength,

concept neighborhoods,

formula dependencies,

theorem chains,

likely misconception edges,

exam yield mapping,

representation families,

cross-subject bridges.

Without this, some diagnoses remain shallow because the engine’s world model is too flat.

10. Behavioral and Motivation Engine
Academic performance is not only cognition.

The coach needs to reason about:

avoidance,

hesitation,

confidence collapse,

overconfidence,

frustration tolerance,

momentum,

consistency risk,

emotional aftershocks after a bad session,

readiness for challenge escalation.

Your risk and adaptation engines touch this, but only partially. 


My strongest conclusion
You are not suffering from “too few lines.”
You are suffering from compressed ontology.

Too many big intelligence problems are currently packed into a few engines.

That is why it feels too small.

What a more realistic target looks like
I would not say “100k per module.”
I would say:

Current audited stack: ~12k

Serious v2 intelligence stack: 60k–120k

Truly world-class coach brain: 120k–250k+, depending on how much content intelligence, experimentation, memory, and self-evaluation you build

A possible breakdown:

Topic Diagnosis + Hypothesis Lab: 15k–25k

Misconception / Interference Graph: 8k–15k

Learner Cognitive State Model: 8k–15k

Session Composer / Intervention Orchestrator: 10k–18k

Content Intelligence / Generation Selection: 12k–25k

Timing / Reinforcement / Retest OS: 8k–12k

Risk + Protection + Failure Forecasting: 8k–12k

Adaptation + Meta-Learning + Coach Self-Eval: 10k–18k

Coverage + Knowledge Graph + Curriculum Intelligence: 10k–18k

Memory / Analytics / Experimentation / Audit trails: 12k–25k

Now the size matches the ambition.

What I would do next
Do not expand randomly.
Refactor the vision into 4 layers:

Layer A — Perception

evidence ingestion

learner signals

content signals

curriculum signals

parent/teacher signals

Layer B — Understanding

diagnosis

hypothesis competition

learner state modeling

misconception/interference modeling

mastery-state inference

Layer C — Decision

sequencing

timing

risk

adaptation

intervention choice

session composition

Layer D — Learning

outcome evaluation

memory updates

experiment results

strategy performance

coach self-critique

That will expose exactly where the missing code mass should go.

Hard truth
If your dream is a coach that can notice highly non-obvious causes of weakness, test them intelligently, personalize instruction deeply, and continuously get smarter, then yes — 12k for the whole intelligence layer is too small for the final destination.

But it is not a failure.
It is a credible nucleus.

The audit shows the nucleus already has real muscles:

topic hypothesis logic,

proof contracts,

coverage overrides,

timing phases,

multi-class risk,

adaptive profiles,

planner feedback loops. 

 


So the right move is not “rewrite everything.”
The right move is:

split the compressed engines into a larger intelligence lattice and let each hidden cognitive responsibility become its own subsystem.

That is how this becomes a true coach brain.

I can turn this into a concrete expansion map next: current engines → missing sub-engines → proposed folder structure → rough LOC budget → execution order.

Good. Here is the concrete expansion map.

Your current stack is:

Topic Intelligence

Coverage Intelligence

Sequencing Intelligence

Timing Intelligence

Risk Intelligence

Adaptation Intelligence

Total audited size: 12,068 lines, with real cross-engine integration already in place. 


What follows is how I would turn that into the larger intelligence lattice your coach actually needs.

1. Reframe the system
Do not think of the current 6 engines as the final engines.

Think of them as top-level domains.

Each one should split into a cluster of sub-engines.

So the future architecture becomes:

Perception Layer

Understanding Layer

Decision Layer

Execution Layer

Learning Layer

Governance Layer

That is the cleanest way to scale intelligence without creating spaghetti.

2. Current engine → future engine family
A. Topic Intelligence becomes a family
Right now Topic Intelligence is doing too much inside one box: diagnosis, cause inference, teaching mode selection, proof contract construction, evidence interpretation, and intervention feedback. 


Split it into:

A1. Topic Scope Engine
Responsible for:

topic boundary detection

prerequisite bridge expansion

concept cluster assembly

topic-to-concept map generation

A2. Concept State Engine
Responsible for:

per-concept state estimation

state smoothing across evidence

partial mastery modeling

latent instability scoring

A3. Evidence Spine Engine
Responsible for:

question-level evidence ingestion

evidence normalization

evidence weighting

signal extraction

evidence freshness logic

A4. Hypothesis Competition Engine
Responsible for:

generating competing explanations

assigning prior probabilities

updating confidence with evidence

ranking plausible causes

tracking uncertainty

deciding what is still unresolved

A5. Misconception Engine
Responsible for:

misconception detection

misconception memory

misconception family labeling

misconception recurrence tracking

A6. Interference Engine
Responsible for:

confusion pair detection

contradiction mapping

representation conflict detection

topic collision scoring

“learned A broke B” detection

A7. Teaching Strategy Engine
Responsible for:

teaching mode selection

mode override logic

mode fallback logic

strategy templates per diagnosis

A8. Mastery Proof Engine
Responsible for:

defining proof obligations

checking explain/solve/transfer/recall/pressure/mixed-context gates

capping false proof

deciding whether mastery is real

A9. Topic Memory Engine
Responsible for:

remembering what failed before

remembering what worked before

decay-aware topic history

longitudinal topic narrative

A10. Topic Outcome Engine
Responsible for:

post-session topic verdict

intervention effectiveness

delayed recall obligations

topic-level learning closure

That one current engine can realistically become 10 internal engines.

B. Coverage Intelligence becomes a family
Coverage is currently portfolio-oriented and strong, but it still needs more structural intelligence. 


Split into:

B1. Curriculum Graph Engine
syllabus ingestion

subject/topic/concept decomposition

learning objective mapping

exam blueprint linking

B2. Coverage Classification Engine
bucket assignment

coverage urgency

proof-aware coverage corrections

B3. Gap Detection Engine
uncovered high-yield

false stability

blocked downstream chains

speed-only gaps

forgotten-but-once-learned gaps

B4. Review Obligation Engine
spacing calculations

debt queue

recall pressure forecasting

B5. Time Budget Engine
sessions available vs needed

pace-to-exam feasibility

subject competition under limited time

B6. Coverage Trajectory Engine
projected readiness

scenario forecasts

best-case / expected / risk-case projections

B7. Content Sufficiency Engine
do we have enough notes

enough examples

enough drills

enough transfer questions

enough pressure-conditioned items

Coverage should not just ask, “Have we touched this?”
It should ask, “Do we have enough high-quality instructional and diagnostic material to deal with this topic properly?”

C. Sequencing Intelligence becomes a family
Current sequencing chooses models and detects interference separation. Good, but still compressed. 


Split into:

C1. Sequence Model Competition Engine
compare sequence models

historical penalties

model confidence

C2. Dependency Path Engine
prerequisite-respecting path generation

bridge-node inclusion

bottleneck-first restructuring

C3. Confidence Path Engine
easy-win sequences

morale restoration sequences

confidence-preserving route design

C4. Interference Separation Engine
minimum-gap constraints

contrast spacing

anti-confusion ordering

C5. Sequence Stability Engine
churn analysis

lock recommendations

pivot threshold logic

C6. Alternative Path Engine
“if this fails, next best route”

contingency sequence generation

C7. Multi-Subject Arbitration Engine
if math and science both have urgent needs, what comes first

sequencing under cross-subject time pressure

D. Timing Intelligence becomes a family
Timing currently handles phase progression, review timing, retests, golden windows, pacing, and frequency. That is already real, but it can expand heavily. 


Split into:

D1. Phase Engine
phase determination

phase transitions

stall detection

phase health

D2. Reinforcement Window Engine
golden window detection

near-expiry reinforcement

fragile gain protection

D3. Retest Engine
verification retests

fragility retests

compaction checkpoints

exam-conditioned retests

D4. Spacing Engine
recall interval optimization

topic-specific forgetting curve adaptation

learner-specific spacing profile

D5. Session Pacing Engine
how many sessions today

how hard today

cooldown logic

overload protection

D6. Readiness-to-Pressure Engine
when to introduce timing

when to reduce timing

when to run mock simulation

when pressure training is harmful

D7. Calendar Coordination Engine
exam-aware scheduling

multi-subject calendar arbitration

parent schedule constraints

student availability patterns

E. Risk Intelligence becomes a family
Current Risk Intelligence is good enough to prove the direction, but it should become broader and deeper. 


Split into:

E1. Topic Risk Engine
per-topic risk classes

local risk score

local risk velocity

E2. Compound Risk Engine
interaction effects

spiral detection

cascading failure chains

E3. Learner Fragility Engine
confidence collapse risk

overload risk

disengagement risk

avoidance risk

E4. Exam Risk Engine
time-to-exam exposure

speed deficit exposure

false readiness exposure

E5. Plan Drift Engine
strategy drift

execution drift

inconsistency drift

over-review drift

E6. Content Risk Engine
content gaps

poor question diversity

poor explanation quality

broken curriculum alignment

E7. Early Warning Engine
risk projections

trend alerts

upcoming failure warnings

E8. Protection Rule Engine
what not to do next

what to delay

what to reduce

what to immediately stabilize

F. Adaptation Intelligence becomes a family
Right now adaptation handles triggers, velocity, systemic patterns, learner adaptive profile, and stop lists. That is a strong base. 


Split into:

F1. Strategy Health Engine
effectiveness

stalledness

regression

family success rates

F2. Trigger Engine
adaptation trigger detection

urgency ranking

trigger clustering

F3. Velocity Engine
improvement slope

decline slope

F4. Learner Profile Engine
teaching mode preference

timing response

recovery profile

feedback sensitivity

F5. Systemic Pattern Engine
cross-topic family failure

global recall decay

systemic speed weakness

F6. Adaptation Pacing Engine
prevent thrashing

dwell rules

stabilization windows

F7. Stop/Continue Engine
negative recommendations

protected strategies

phased rollback logic

F8. Coach Self-Evaluation Engine
was the adaptation right

did the reroute help

did we switch too soon

did we diagnose wrongly

This last one is critical. It is how the coach becomes self-improving instead of just reactive.

3. New engines you do not yet explicitly have
These are the biggest missing blocks.

G. Learner State Engine
This should become central.

It models:

confidence state

fatigue state

stamina

response to failure

retrieval stability

pressure tolerance

abstraction tolerance

hint dependence

transfer elasticity

motivational drift

This is the engine that says:
“Don’t interpret this as ignorance. Interpret it as pressure collapse.”
or
“She understands the topic but not in mixed-context conditions.”

H. Session Composer Engine
This is different from sequencing.

It composes the actual lesson/session:

open with review or not

teach first or test first

compare concepts or isolate one

add worked example or independent attempt

inject motivation repair or not

when to stop

how to end the session for retention

Without this, the system chooses good topics but still delivers mediocre sessions.

I. Diagnostic Experiment Engine
This engine runs controlled probes to disambiguate causes.

Examples:

timed vs untimed

single-topic vs mixed-topic

visual vs symbolic

recognition vs recall

guided solve vs independent solve

immediate vs delayed recall

isolated formula vs applied word problem

This is how the coach discovers hidden failure causes instead of guessing them.

J. Content Intelligence Engine
This one is huge.

It should:

ingest curriculum

ingest textbooks

ingest past questions

mine concept explanations

build glossary/definitions/formula graphs

generate explanations

generate test items

generate contrast drills

generate pressure probes

score content quality

select the right content artifact per intervention

Your coach brain will remain limited until content intelligence becomes native.

K. Knowledge Graph Engine
This should manage:

topic graph

concept graph

misconception graph

interference graph

prerequisite graph

representation graph

formula dependency graph

exam blueprint graph

This becomes the structural world model of the coach.

L. Meta-Governor Engine
This supervises the whole intelligence layer:

contradiction checks

output consistency

confidence thresholds

unsafe strategy prevention

engine arbitration

final decision synthesis

It is the “adult in the room.”

5. Rough LOC budget
This is the kind of size that would make sense.

Foundation layer
shared types, scoring, evidence normalization, graph utilities: 8k–15k

Perception layer
ingestion, evidence extraction, signal shaping: 10k–18k

Understanding layer
topic diagnosis, learner state, misconception, interference, proof, graph logic: 30k–50k

Decision layer
teaching, sequencing, timing, risk, adaptation, session composition: 25k–40k

Experimentation layer
controlled probes, falsification, test design: 8k–15k

Memory layer
longitudinal memory systems, forgetting and trend logic: 8k–15k

Content intelligence layer
curriculum parsing, content mining, generation selection, quality scoring: 20k–40k

Evaluation + governance
self-evaluation, arbitration, decision consistency, auditability: 8k–15k

That gives you a serious target range of roughly:

117k to 208k lines

That is much more believable for the coach intelligence ambition you are describing.

Not because large code is automatically good, but because the problem surface is genuinely large.

6. Build order
This is the important part.

Do not build in the order that sounds glamorous.
Build in the order that compounds value.

Phase 1 — deepen the current nucleus
Do first:

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Misconception Engine

Interference Engine

Mastery Proof Engine upgrade

Learner State Engine

Why first:
These directly improve diagnosis quality, which improves everything downstream.

Phase 2 — make sessions intelligent
Next:

Session Composer Engine

Diagnostic Experiment Engine

Readiness-to-Pressure Engine

Protection Rule Engine

Why:
Now the coach stops just “choosing topics” and starts designing the right learning experience.

Phase 3 — give the brain a world model
Next:

Curriculum Graph Engine

Knowledge Graph Engine

Content Sufficiency Engine

Content Intelligence Engine

Why:
Now the system understands the structure of the domain and can enrich itself.

Phase 4 — make it self-correcting
Next:

Coach Self-Evaluation Engine

Strategy Health Engine expansion

Adaptation Pacing Engine refinement

Meta-Governor Engine

Why:
This is what separates a smart system from a wise one.

Phase 5 — scale memory and forecasting
Next:

Topic Memory Engine

Learner Memory Engine

Forecast / Trajectory Engine expansion

Multi-subject arbitration

Why:
Now the coach becomes long-horizon and strategic.

7. What should be your next practical move

A precise vocabulary for:

learner states

contradiction types

Without ontology, the system grows messily.

Artifact 3: Decision contract spec
For each engine:

what it is allowed to decide

Artifact 4: Data model expansion plan

coach self-evaluation records

8. The most important design principle
Your engine should not just be a set of scorers.

It should behave like a disciplined reasoning system with this loop:

observe

hypothesize

test

interpret

intervene

verify

remember

adapt

govern

Right now you already have early forms of that loop in Topic, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation intelligence. 


The next stage is to make each part explicit and much richer.

Do this transformation:

Current 6 engines

That is the correct scaling path.

Thought for 10s
Good. Below is the full engine registry frame I would use as the canonical structure for your academic coach intelligence system.

This is not just a brainstorm list. This is meant to function like the start of a real architecture document.

I am going to define:

top-level layers

engine registry by layer

each engine’s purpose

inputs

outputs

core tables / entities

dependency map

build priority

Where useful, I’ll anchor it back to your existing audited engine base, which already includes Topic, Coverage, Sequencing, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation intelligence. 


1. Canonical top-level architecture
Your final intelligence system should be organized into 6 layers:

Layer A — Perception
This is where raw signals are collected, normalized, and converted into usable evidence.

Layer B — Understanding
This is where the system infers what is actually going on with the student, the topic, and the learning state.

Layer C — Decision
This is where the system chooses what to do next.

Layer D — Execution Design
This is where decisions become actual session plans, interventions, probes, and content actions.

Layer E — Learning and Memory
This is where the coach remembers, evaluates, and improves itself over time.

Layer F — Governance
This is where contradictions are checked, confidence thresholds are enforced, and unsafe or weak decisions are blocked.

2. Canonical engine registry
I will name them in a disciplined way.

LAYER A — PERCEPTION
These engines answer:

what signals do we have?

how trustworthy are they?

how should they be normalized?

A1. Response Evidence Ingestion Engine
Purpose
Ingest raw student response events and convert them into structured evidence.

Inputs
answer selected

typed response

time taken

hint usage

retries

skipped question

confidence signal if available

session context

question metadata

Outputs
normalized response event

scored evidence packet

event quality flags

Core tables / entities
response_events

response_attempts

response_timing_signals

response_confidence_signals

response_evidence_packets

Dependencies
question metadata

content taxonomy

session context

Priority
P0

A2. Content Signal Ingestion Engine
Purpose
Understand the instructional object being interacted with.

Inputs
question stem

topic

concept tags

difficulty

question type

representation type

source type

worked example marker

formula linkage

glossary linkage

Outputs
content profile

content signal map

diagnostic affordance tags

Core tables
content_items

content_profiles

content_affordance_tags

question_feature_vectors

Priority
P0

A3. Curriculum Ingestion Engine
Purpose
Convert syllabus, curriculum, textbook, and exam blueprint materials into machine-usable structure.

Inputs
curriculum docs

syllabus docs

topic lists

textbook scans

exam objectives

subject hierarchy

Outputs
curriculum graph nodes

objective mappings

topic ordering hints

prerequisite seed edges

Core tables

curriculum_units

learning_objectives

topic_nodes

prerequisite_edges

Priority
P1

LAYER F — GOVERNANCE
These engines answer:

should we trust this decision?

is there contradiction?

are we acting too aggressively or too weakly?

what is the final safe output?

F1. Decision Arbitration Engine
Purpose
Resolve conflicts between engines.

Examples
sequencing says advance, risk says stabilize

timing says retest now, learner state says fatigue

adaptation says switch, memory says last switch harmed progress

Inputs
engine decisions

engine confidence

protection rules

policy constraints

Outputs
final adjudicated decision

arbitration rationale

Core tables
decision_conflicts

arbitration_decisions

arbitration_rationales

Priority
P1

F2. Confidence Gate Engine
Purpose
Prevent low-confidence reasoning from driving strong actions.

Inputs
hypothesis confidence

strategy confidence

learner state uncertainty

evidence sufficiency

Outputs
confidence gate pass/fail

probe required

soft recommendation vs hard action distinction

Core tables
confidence_gates

confidence_gate_hits

Priority
P1

F3. Contradiction Check Engine
Purpose
Find incompatible beliefs or outputs in the system.

Inputs
topic state

mastery verdict

risk verdict

session outcome

adaptation verdict

Outputs
contradiction alerts

contradiction class

required resolution path

Example contradictions
mastery passed but transfer still failing

low risk but exam forecast collapsing

concept marked stable but repeated same misconception

adaptation claims progress, velocity says stagnation

Core tables
contradiction_events

contradiction_classes

contradiction_resolutions

Priority
P1

F4. Policy Guardrail Engine
Purpose
Enforce non-negotiable system rules.

Example rules
do not label mastery with only recognition evidence

do not introduce timing pressure before readiness

do not skip blocked prerequisite unless override rule passes

do not suppress severe risk alert

do not create high-stakes forecast from thin evidence

Core tables
policy_rules

policy_hits

policy_overrides

Priority
P1

F5. Audit and Trace Engine
Purpose
Make every key decision inspectable.

Inputs
all decision outputs

evidence references

confidence bands

arbitration outcomes

Outputs
decision trace

reason chain

reproducible audit log

Core tables
decision_traces

decision_trace_steps

evidence_references

Priority
P0

This one matters a lot because your coach will become too complex to trust without traceability.

6. Decision contract example
Every engine should have a contract like this:

Example — Hypothesis Competition Engine
Allowed to decide
ranked possible explanations

uncertainty level

need for more probes

Not allowed to decide
final session layout

topic sequence

hard risk policy

mastery certification

Must provide
top N hypotheses

confidence for each

evidence links

what evidence is missing

Can be challenged by
Diagnostic Experiment Engine

Contradiction Check Engine

Coach Self-Evaluation Engine

That pattern should exist for every engine.

7. What your current audited engines map to
Your current audited system already covers the early versions of several of these:

Topic Intelligence already contains topic selection, hypothesis reasoning, teaching mode logic, and proof-like gates. 


Coverage Intelligence already covers coverage classification, gap sensing, and review burden logic. 


Sequencing Intelligence already covers order selection and anti-interference handling. 


Timing Intelligence already handles phase progression, retests, and review windows. 


Risk Intelligence already handles risk classes and risk propagation concerns. 


Adaptation Intelligence already handles triggers, profiles, and stop/continue behavior. 


So this registry is not coming from nowhere.

It is really your current system uncompressed into clearer responsibilities.

10. My exact recommendation for your immediate next move
The first 5 engines I would flesh out fully before anything else are:

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Learner State Engine

Misconception Engine

Interference Engine

Session Composer Engine

Why these five?

Because together they answer the real hard questions:

what is wrong?

what kind of learner is this?

what exactly are they misunderstanding?

what concepts are colliding?

what learning experience should be delivered right now?

That combination gives your coach real intelligence, not just smart routing.

11. Bottom line
The right architecture is not:

one giant “brain” file

six oversized modules

arbitrary line-count expansion

The right architecture is:

one coherent intelligence system

broken into disciplined engines

grouped by cognitive responsibility

governed by traceable decision contracts

backed by memory and auditability

That is how your academic coach becomes genuinely powerful.

Next, I can turn this into a formal Engine Registry v1 document with a strict template for each engine, starting from the top 5 most important ones.


i am not interested in v1 , i am interested in the full version from day 1

Understood. I’ll switch from staged/MVP framing to a full-scope architecture stance: complete intelligence model from day 1, with sequencing only for dependency order, not for reduced ambition.

Thought for 37s
Understood.

Then we should stop talking in v1 / later / maybe someday language.

For your case, the right stance is:

the full intelligence system is defined from day 1 as the real product architecture.
Implementation may still follow dependency order, but the scope, contracts, schema, ontology, and orchestration model must already be final-form from the beginning.

Your audited engine already proves you have a real nucleus: 6 engines, 53 real algorithms, 12,068 lines, cross-engine writes/reads, hypothesis logic, proof contracts, timing decisions, risk feedback, and adaptation logs.
So the next step is not “make a small version.” It is to define the full constitution of the intelligence system now.

What “full version from day 1” really means
It means these 6 things are non-negotiable from the start:

Final ontology from day 1
All weakness classes, evidence classes, mastery states, learner states, intervention families, contradiction classes, and risk classes are defined now.

Final engine boundaries from day 1
No temporary “misc brain” bucket. Every reasoning responsibility has a home now.

Final data model from day 1
Even if some tables stay lightly populated at first, the schema reflects the final system, not a simplified interim schema.

Final decision contracts from day 1
Every engine knows what it may decide, what it may not decide, what evidence it requires, and what can override it.

Final orchestration loop from day 1
The runtime loop of observe → infer → test → intervene → verify → remember → adapt → govern is not postponed. It is the native operating model.

No throwaway architecture
No temporary logic that will later be scrapped because it cannot support the full brain.

That is the right interpretation of “full version from day 1.”

The day-1 full intelligence constitution
I would define the system as 8 permanent intelligence domains.

1. Evidence Domain
This is the sensory system.

It owns:

raw student response events

timing behavior

hint behavior

retries

skips

explanation attempts

mixed-context evidence

transfer evidence

delayed recall evidence

parent/teacher observations

session outcome evidence

Permanent engines:

Response Evidence Engine

Behavior Evidence Engine

Session Evidence Engine

External Observation Engine

Evidence Normalization Engine

Evidence Trust Weighting Engine

2. Knowledge Domain
This is the world model of what is being learned.

It owns:

curriculum graph

subject graph

topic graph

concept graph

prerequisite graph

formula graph

misconception graph

interference graph

representation graph

exam blueprint graph

Permanent engines:

Curriculum Graph Engine

Topic Scope Engine

Concept Graph Engine

Formula Dependency Engine

Misconception Taxonomy Engine

Interference Graph Engine

Exam Mapping Engine

3. Learner Domain
This is the model of the student as a living system.

It owns:

confidence state

fatigue state

pressure tolerance

recovery profile

abstraction tolerance

hint dependence

transfer elasticity

attention stability

consistency profile

avoidance tendencies

resilience after failure

Permanent engines:

Learner State Engine

Pressure Profile Engine

Recovery Profile Engine

Fatigue Profile Engine

Confidence Fragility Engine

Engagement Drift Engine

Learning Preference Engine

4. Diagnostic Domain
This is the inferential core.

It answers:

what exactly is wrong?

what are the competing explanations?

what evidence supports each one?

what remains unresolved?

Permanent engines:

Concept State Engine

Topic State Engine

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Misconception Detection Engine

Interference Detection Engine

False Mastery Engine

Weakness Attribution Engine

Ambiguity Resolver Engine

Diagnostic Experiment Engine

Your current Topic Intelligence already contains early forms of this through concept ranking, 3-layer hypothesis generation, evidence-spine feedback, teaching mode selection, and proof logic.
In the full version, those must become explicit sub-engines, not remain compressed inside one box.

5. Decision Domain
This decides what the coach should do.

Permanent engines:

Teaching Strategy Engine

Sequencing Engine

Timing Engine

Risk Engine

Adaptation Engine

Session Composer Engine

Content Selection Engine

Protection Rule Engine

Multi-Subject Arbitration Engine

Your current audited system already has real sequencing, timing, risk, and adaptation engines with planner feedback. 


The full version keeps those, but with stricter contracts and more sibling engines around them.

6. Execution Design Domain
This turns decisions into actual educational actions.

Permanent engines:

Intervention Design Engine

Drill Construction Engine

Assessment Construction Engine

Explanation Construction Engine

Assignment Design Engine

7. Memory and Meta-Learning Domain
This is where the coach becomes cumulative.

Permanent engines:

Topic Memory Engine

Learner Memory Engine

Coach Self-Evaluation Engine

Your current engine already closes a feedback loop after clinic sessions and records intervention history.
The full version expands that into permanent longitudinal memory, not just local feedback logging.

8. Governance Domain
This is what prevents the whole system from becoming clever but unreliable.

Permanent engines:

Decision Arbitration Engine

Confidence Gate Engine

Contradiction Check Engine

Policy Guardrail Engine

Audit Trace Engine

Explanation Trace Engine

Engine Health Monitor

Consistency Validator

The full runtime loop from day 1
The full system should run this loop natively on every major coaching cycle:

Step 1: Observe
Collect all fresh evidence.

Step 2: Normalize
Adjust for trust, recency, noise, context, and evidence strength.

Step 3: Infer
Update concept states, topic states, learner states, and competing hypotheses.

Step 4: Resolve ambiguity
If uncertainty is too high, design diagnostic probes instead of pretending certainty.

Step 5: Decide
Choose strategy, sequence, timing, risk posture, and protection rules.

Step 6: Compose
Build the actual session, assessment, or intervention artifact.

Step 7: Execute
Run the coaching action.

Step 8: Verify
Check mastery proof, transfer, delayed recall, pressure survival, and misconception repair.

Step 9: Remember
Update topic memory, learner memory, strategy memory, and intervention history.

Step 10: Meta-evaluate
Judge whether the coach itself made the right call.

Step 11: Govern
Run contradiction checks, arbitration, confidence gates, and audit logging before final plan release.

That is the full day-1 operating loop.

Day-1 non-negotiable invariants
These are the rules I would hard-code into the architecture from the start.

Invariant 1: no single-score diagnosis
A learner cannot be reduced to one readiness score.

Invariant 2: no mastery without proof
Your current system already moved in this direction with a 6-checkpoint mastery proof contract.
In the full version, this becomes law across the entire coach.

Invariant 3: no strong action on weak evidence
Low confidence must trigger probes, not overconfident interventions.

Invariant 4: no topic reasoning without learner reasoning
A topic may be weak for very different reasons depending on the learner state.

Invariant 5: no adaptation without memory
The system must know whether the last similar move worked.

Invariant 6: no timing pressure before readiness
Pressure is a diagnostic and conditioning instrument, not a default setting.

Invariant 7: no content selection without content fitness
The system must know why this note, this drill, this worked example, this test.

Invariant 8: no plan without traceability
Every important decision must be reproducible later.

Thought for 15s
Below is the Full Intelligence Constitution for the academic coach.

This is written in the correct frame for your goal: final-form system from day 1, not a reduced edition. Your audited base already proves the nucleus is real — 6 engines, 53 algorithms, 12,068 total lines, with Topic, Coverage, Sequencing, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation already exchanging signals and feeding planner behavior. 


Full Intelligence Constitution
1. Foundational doctrine
The academic coach is not a feature bundle. It is a reasoning system whose job is to continuously determine:

what the learner truly knows,

what only appears known,

what is unstable,

why errors are happening,

what experience should happen next,

what should be delayed,

what should be tested,

what should be remembered,

and whether the coach itself is reasoning correctly.

The system exists to make these judgments under uncertainty, over time, across subjects, under changing learner states.

The constitution therefore rejects these weak patterns:

one-score diagnosis,

topic-only thinking,

static learner models,

untimed-only proof,

accuracy-only proof,

blind content delivery,

untraceable decisions,

adaptation without evidence,

and orchestration without governance.

The existing audited engine already points in the right direction: Topic Intelligence uses hypothesis logic and proof-like checks, Sequencing uses model competition with historical penalties, Timing governs reinforcement and retests, Risk runs taxonomies and early warnings, and Adaptation performs meta-learning with stop lists and learner-specific patterns.

This constitution makes that direction permanent.

2. System axioms
These are constitutional laws.

Axiom 1: Knowledge is multi-dimensional
A learner may:

solve but not explain,

explain but not retrieve under pressure,

retrieve in isolation but fail in mixed context,

succeed untimed but collapse when paced,

remember now but forget tomorrow,

appear stable while still fragile.

Therefore no topic may be considered secure from one success mode alone.

Axiom 2: Weakness is causal, not merely statistical
A low score is not a diagnosis. It is an outcome that may arise from different causes:

prerequisite weakness,

misconception,

interference,

pressure collapse,

retrieval weakness,

fatigue,

overconfidence,

shallow pattern matching,

representation mismatch,

poor timing tolerance,

or a bad teaching fit.

Axiom 3: Diagnosis must remain plural until resolved
The system must carry competing explanations until evidence narrows them. This is already foreshadowed by the audited Topic Intelligence with its layered hypothesis approach. 


Axiom 4: Mastery requires proof, not impression
The current engine already uses proof-aware logic and proof-related penalties across Topic and Coverage reasoning.
In the full constitution, mastery requires evidence across multiple gates.

Axiom 5: Intervention must fit learner state
A correct content decision delivered in the wrong state is still a bad decision.

Axiom 6: Adaptation is not optional
A coach that cannot revise itself is not intelligent. The audited Adaptation engine already tracks triggers, velocity, cross-topic systemic patterns, and stop rules.

Axiom 7: Every consequential decision must be traceable
No major plan should exist without evidence lineage, confidence, and reason chain.

Axiom 8: Governance is part of intelligence
The system must not only generate decisions. It must also challenge them.

3. Permanent domains of the intelligence system
The intelligence layer is constitutionally divided into 8 permanent domains.

Domain A — Evidence
This domain senses the learner and the session.

It is responsible for collecting and shaping raw signals into evidence. It does not diagnose; it observes.

Its permanent concerns are:

correctness,

latency,

retries,

hint behavior,

skip behavior,

confidence expressions,

answer revision,

explanation attempts,

session conditions,

content interaction type,

and external observations.

Domain B — Knowledge
This domain owns the structural model of what can be learned.

It contains:

curriculum structure,

topic structure,

concept structure,

prerequisite chains,

formula dependencies,

representation families,

misconception classes,

interference relationships,

and exam blueprint mappings.

Domain C — Learner
This domain models the student as a changing cognitive-behavioral system.

It owns:

confidence fragility,

fatigue,

recovery rate,

pressure tolerance,

abstraction tolerance,

attention stability,

engagement drift,

hint dependence,

and learning preference patterns.

Domain D — Diagnosis
This domain explains what is happening.

It computes:

concept state,

topic state,

ranked hypotheses,

misconception instances,

interference events,

false mastery flags,

and unresolved ambiguity.

Domain E — Decision
This domain chooses what to do next.

It governs:

teaching strategy,

sequencing,

timing,

risk posture,

adaptation,

session composition,

content selection,

and protection rules.

Domain F — Execution Design
This domain turns decisions into educational artifacts.

It designs:

interventions,

drills,

assessments,

explanations,

assignments,

retests,

contrast sessions,

and pressure simulations.

Domain G — Memory and Meta-Learning
This domain makes the coach cumulative rather than forgetful.

It stores:

topic memory,

learner memory,

strategy memory,

intervention outcomes,

improvement velocity,

recurrence patterns,

forecasts,

and coach self-evaluation.

Domain H — Governance
This domain monitors the rest.

It enforces:

arbitration,

confidence gates,

contradiction checks,

policy guardrails,

audit trails,

and engine consistency.

4. Canonical ontology
The ontology must be final from the beginning.

4.1 Weakness ontology
The system must distinguish at least these weakness classes:

Foundational weakness — prerequisite concept absent or weak.

Retrieval weakness — concept present but not readily retrievable.

Fragile mastery — recent success without durability.

Transfer weakness — fails when format or context changes.

Pressure weakness — fails under time or pace.

Mixed-context weakness — fails when concepts are blended.

Representation weakness — handles verbal but not symbolic, symbolic but not visual, etc.

Misconception weakness — wrong rule or wrong mental model.

Interference weakness — concept A contaminates concept B.

Procedural weakness — knows idea but cannot execute steps reliably.

Explanation weakness — can answer but cannot articulate logic.

Confidence-mediated weakness — knowledge degraded by fear or low confidence.

Fatigue-mediated weakness — performance drops due to cognitive depletion.

Sequence-induced weakness — topic was introduced at a harmful time.

Teaching-fit weakness — learner needed a different mode.

4.2 Evidence ontology
Evidence classes must include:

correctness evidence,

latency evidence,

hesitation evidence,

retry evidence,

hint dependence evidence,

explanation evidence,

recall evidence,

transfer evidence,

pressure evidence,

mixed-context evidence,

delayed recall evidence,

intervention response evidence,

engagement evidence,

external observation evidence.

4.3 Mastery ontology
A topic can be in states such as:

untouched,

exposed,

partially grasped,

unstable,

operational,

fragile,

transferred,

pressure-ready,

delayed-recall verified,

mastery-proven,

mastery-suspect.

The audited engine already recognizes false mastery-like situations, proof-blocked topics, and speed gaps within Coverage reasoning. 


4.4 Learner state ontology
The learner state must at minimum include:

confidence stable / fragile / collapsed,

pressure tolerant / vulnerable / untested,

low / medium / high fatigue,

recovering / stalled / regressing,

engaged / neutral / avoiding,

concrete-preferring / balanced / abstraction-capable,

high / medium / low hint dependence,

fast-recovering / slow-recovering after failure.

4.5 Intervention ontology
Intervention families must include:

reteach,

prerequisite bridge,

worked example,

scaffolded solve,

retrieval repair,

contrast repair,

misconception dismantling,

pressure conditioning,

confidence rebuild,

mixed-context stabilization,

transfer strengthening,

spaced reinforcement,

retest verification.

4.6 Risk ontology
Risk must be more than “bad or good.”

The audited engine already contains a 12-class taxonomy, compound patterns, risk velocity, and early warnings.

The full ontology should include:

exam risk,

topic collapse risk,

forgotten gain risk,

false readiness risk,

sequence risk,

intervention failure risk,

overload risk,

confidence collapse risk,

systemic subject risk,

plan drift risk,

stagnation risk,

fragility risk.

4.7 Contradiction ontology
Contradictions are first-class events.

Examples:

mastery passed but transfer still failing,

risk low but forecast collapsing,

topic marked stable but same misconception recurring,

adaptation says improving while velocity says flat,

learner judged pressure-ready without pressure data,

content marked sufficient while diagnostics still unresolved.

5. Permanent engine constitution
Below is the permanent engine set. These are not optional modules.

Domain A — Evidence engines
1. Response Evidence Engine
Captures raw response behavior and transforms it into structured response evidence.

2. Behavior Evidence Engine
Captures non-answer behavioral patterns like hesitation, skipping, quitting, rushing, and persistence.

3. Session Evidence Engine
Captures contextual session facts: duration, pacing, block order, mode, subject mix, fatigue drift.

4. Observation Evidence Engine
Ingests parent/teacher notes and external academic observations.

5. Evidence Normalization Engine
Normalizes all evidence to common scales adjusted for trust, recency, and context.

6. Evidence Trust Engine
Weights sources by reliability and decides what evidence is weak, stale, noisy, or strong.

Domain B — Knowledge engines
7. Curriculum Graph Engine
Owns syllabus and learning objective structure.

8. Topic Scope Engine
Defines what belongs inside a topic, including concept boundaries and neighborhood.

9. Concept Graph Engine
Owns concept-level relationships and local adjacency.

10. Formula Dependency Engine
Tracks formula dependencies, preconditions, and related transformations.

11. Misconception Taxonomy Engine
Defines misconception classes, signatures, and likely repairs.

12. Interference Graph Engine
Tracks concept collision relationships and conflict intensities.

13. Exam Mapping Engine
Links topics and concepts to exam frequency, weight, and blueprint roles.

Domain C — Learner engines
14. Learner State Engine
Maintains the current composite learner state.

15. Pressure Profile Engine
Learns what happens to this learner under timed or compressed conditions.

16. Recovery Profile Engine
Models how the learner responds after failure or correction.

17. Fatigue Profile Engine
Detects performance degradation due to accumulated cognitive load.

18. Confidence Fragility Engine
Models confidence sensitivity and confidence-related distortions.

19. Engagement Drift Engine
Detects disengagement, avoidance, and participation decline.

20. Learning Preference Engine
Learns what modes succeed for this learner across topic types.

The audited Adaptation engine already begins part of this work via learner adaptive profiles, pressure response, confidence scaffolding need, and per-topic-type teaching preferences. 


Domain D — Diagnostic engines
21. Concept State Engine
Maintains confidence-rated state for each concept.

22. Topic State Engine
Aggregates concept truth into topic truth.

23. Hypothesis Competition Engine
Maintains multiple plausible explanations and ranks them.

24. Misconception Detection Engine
Detects wrong mental models from error patterns and explanations.

25. Interference Detection Engine
Detects when adjacent concepts are contaminating one another.

26. False Mastery Engine
Detects when a learner looks ready but is not.

27. Weakness Attribution Engine
Assigns dominant causes after competing explanations are evaluated.

28. Ambiguity Resolver Engine
Identifies unresolved uncertainty and what additional evidence is required.

29. Diagnostic Experiment Engine
Designs probes to disambiguate competing hypotheses.

Domain E — Decision engines
30. Teaching Strategy Engine
Chooses the instructional family best suited to the diagnosis.

31. Sequencing Engine
Chooses topic order and pivot rules. The audited version already uses 7 competing models, historical penalties, interference separation, and churn logic. 


32. Timing Engine
Chooses when review, retest, pacing, pressure, and reinforcement occur. The audited version already governs phase transitions, review scheduling, retests, golden windows, and pacing.

33. Risk Engine
Estimates multi-source academic risk. The audited version already includes risk velocity, class trends, early warning types, and persisted topic/subject/phase scopes. 


34. Adaptation Engine
Decides when to reopen diagnosis, reroute, compress, refine, or protect. The audited version already has trigger logic, stop lists, dwell times, and systemic pattern detection.

35. Session Composer Engine
Builds the actual shape of the learning session.

36. Content Selection Engine
Selects the most appropriate content asset for the present need.

37. Protection Rule Engine
Blocks harmful or premature actions.

38. Multi-Subject Arbitration Engine
Resolves competition between subjects when time is scarce.

Domain F — Execution design engines
39. Intervention Design Engine
Constructs the intervention as an object with steps and success conditions.

40. Drill Construction Engine
Builds weakness-specific drill structures.

41. Assessment Construction Engine
Builds diagnostics, proof checks, reinforcement tests, and mocks.

42. Explanation Construction Engine
Creates learner-appropriate explanations in the right representation mode.

43. Assignment Design Engine
Designs asynchronous learning missions.

44. Retest Construction Engine
Builds the right retest based on what must be verified.

45. Pressure Simulation Design Engine
Creates safe but diagnostic pressure experiences.

46. Contrast Session Design Engine
Builds sessions specifically meant to separate confused concepts.

Domain G — Memory and meta-learning engines
47. Topic Memory Engine
Stores topic history, recovery pattern, and recurrence.

48. Learner Memory Engine
Stores long-horizon learner tendencies.

49. Strategy Memory Engine
Stores what teaching strategies work in which contexts.

50. Intervention Outcome Engine
Scores intervention results with delayed effects.

51. Improvement Velocity Engine
Measures slope of progress, stagnation, and regression.

52. Recurrence Pattern Engine
Finds repeated problem forms across topics and time.

53. Forecast Engine
Projects likely future readiness under current trajectory.

54. Coach Self-Evaluation Engine
Judges whether the coach itself reasoned well.

Domain H — Governance engines
55. Decision Arbitration Engine
Resolves engine conflicts.

56. Confidence Gate Engine
Blocks strong action when evidence confidence is too weak.

57. Contradiction Check Engine
Finds incompatible judgments across the system.

58. Policy Guardrail Engine
Enforces inviolable system rules.

59. Audit Trace Engine
Stores the reason chain for every consequential decision.

60. Explanation Trace Engine
Stores why a learner-facing explanation or plan was chosen.

61. Engine Health Monitor
Checks whether engines are behaving coherently over time.

62. Consistency Validator
Checks schema, outputs, contracts, and logical consistency.

6. Decision rights and non-rights
Each engine must have a constitutional boundary.

A few examples:

Hypothesis Competition Engine
It may:

rank explanations,

assign confidence,

request more evidence.

It may not:

certify mastery,

choose session block order,

override risk policy.

Mastery-related engines
They may:

decide whether proof gates are met.

They may not:

suppress risk warnings,

assume readiness from shallow evidence.

Sequencing Engine
It may:

choose order,

impose separation gaps,

define pivot conditions.

It may not:

ignore Protection Rule blocks,

treat a demoralized learner as a neutral learner,

label content as sufficient.

Adaptation Engine
It may:

reopen diagnosis,

reroute,

compress,

refine,

protect.

It may not:

thrash the plan repeatedly without dwell consideration,

override hard governance rules.

The audited engine already shows early forms of these rights through planner integration, historical penalties, stop lists, and adaptation pacing.

7. Mastery proof constitution
Mastery must be constitutionally proven through gates.

A topic may only be marked mastery-proven if the system has enough evidence for the relevant subset of these gates:

Accuracy gate — can solve correctly.

Explanation gate — can explain why.

Transfer gate — can solve in altered form.

Mixed-context gate — can survive concept blending.

Pressure gate — can survive appropriate timing conditions.

Delayed recall gate — can reproduce later.

Misconception-cleared gate — no active misconception still contaminating the result.

Interference-cleared gate — nearby concept conflict no longer destabilizes performance.

Not every topic needs every gate at equal strength, but no topic may be certified from mere short-term correctness.

The audited system already contains proof-aware reasoning, proof-blocked classifications, and proof penalties in coverage/session estimation.

8. Diagnostic doctrine
The coach must diagnose like a disciplined investigator.

8.1 Diagnostic rule
Every weakness must be explained as:

observed behavior,

interpreted evidence,

ranked possible causes,

required additional evidence,

current best cause,

confidence level.

8.2 Competing hypothesis rule
At least several explanation families must remain available until evidence resolves them. Examples:

real knowledge gap,

retrieval weakness,

pressure weakness,

interference,

misconception,

teaching mismatch,

fatigue-mediated distortion,

transfer weakness.

8.3 Falsification rule
The system must ask: what evidence would prove our current favorite explanation wrong?

8.4 Experiment rule
When uncertainty is high, the system must run probes instead of acting falsely certain.

Probe examples:

timed vs untimed,

isolated vs mixed,

explain vs solve,

symbolic vs verbal,

guided vs independent,

now vs delayed recall.

9. Session composition constitution
A session is not just “questions plus notes.”

A session is a planned cognitive path.

The Session Composer must produce a block structure such as:

recall warm-up,

diagnostic probe,

focused reteach,

contrast segment,

guided solve,

independent attempt,

pressure micro-check,

recap,

memory anchor,

assignment handoff.

The exact shape depends on:

learner state,

dominant hypothesis,

fragility,

fatigue,

topic importance,

risk posture,

and content sufficiency.

The audited engine already supports ingredients for this, but not yet as a distinct constitutionally separate organ. That separation is required now.

10. Timing constitution
Timing is not merely calendar placement. It is part of cognition.

The Timing domain must permanently manage:

strategic review timing,

retest timing,

reinforcement window timing,

timing-pressure readiness,

daily pacing,

rest detection,

and session frequency.

The audited Timing engine already includes golden window detection, retest categories, daily pacing, and strategic timing logic.

The constitution adds these laws:

no pressure exposure without readiness evidence,

no repeated proof on a fatigued learner,

no over-review of stable material while critical weaknesses remain,

no delayed recall neglect after successful repair,

no exam-near plan that ignores time budget reality.

11. Risk constitution
Risk exists at topic, learner, subject, plan, and exam levels.

The Risk domain must always reason about:

current severity,

trajectory,

source drivers,

compounding effects,

and protective response.

The audited engine already handles risk velocity, early warnings, compound patterns, and topic/subject/phase persistence.

The full constitution adds:

risk cannot be summarized without source decomposition,

high risk must always generate protective implications,

rising risk matters more than static medium risk,

confidence collapse risk and overload risk are academic risks, not merely UX issues.

12. Adaptation constitution
Adaptation is a native property of the coach.

The audited Adaptation engine already tracks strategy health, trigger conditions, cross-topic systemic patterns, learner profiles, dwell times, and negative stop lists.

The constitutional rules are:

no adaptation without evidence conflict,

no repeated reopening without new basis,

no excessive switching inside dwell windows unless urgency is extreme,

no continuation of families that are repeatedly failing,

no ignoring of systemic patterns just because local topic results look acceptable.

Adaptation modes are permanent:

reopen diagnosis,

reroute,

refine,

compress,

protect,

stabilize,

escalate.

13. Memory constitution
The coach must remember in layers.

Topic memory
Stores:

past weakness forms,

repaired misconceptions,

interference history,

proof history,

retest outcomes,

fragility recurrence.

Learner memory
Stores:

pressure behavior,

recovery behavior,

confidence patterns,

fatigue patterns,

successful teaching modes,

persistent avoidance patterns.

Strategy memory
Stores:

intervention family performance,

context-conditioned success,

diminishing returns,

anti-patterns.

Coach memory
Stores:

when the coach diagnosed wrongly,

when it switched too early,

when it over-trusted thin evidence,

when it missed systemic patterns.

The audited engine already has intervention history, adaptation logs, and planner feedback loops that prove memory is already part of the current nucleus.
This constitution expands memory into permanent long-horizon reasoning.

14. Governance constitution
Governance is not an afterthought.

14.1 Confidence gate law
No strong intervention, mastery certification, or risky escalation should happen from thin evidence.

14.2 Contradiction law
The system must detect when two outputs cannot both be trusted.

14.3 Arbitration law
When engines disagree, conflict must be resolved by formal rules, not hidden priority hacks.

14.4 Policy law
Certain things are never allowed, such as:

mastery from recognition alone,

pressure escalation without readiness,

ignoring severe prerequisite block,

pretending low-confidence diagnosis is settled,

overloading a fragile learner for coverage optics.

14.5 Audit law
Every meaningful decision must leave:

evidence references,

engine contributors,

confidence values,

conflict notes,

and final rationale.

The audited engine’s cross-engine planner integration already shows why this matters: risk, adaptation, and timing outputs directly shift topic priority and plan behavior. 


The full version cannot allow such power without governance.

15. Canonical event model
The full system should run on event flow, not static snapshots alone.

Major event types include:

response.recorded

hint.used

question.skipped

session.completed

assessment.graded

topic.repaired

topic.retested

mastery.proof_updated

risk.recomputed

adaptation.triggered

strategy.changed

diagnostic.probe_requested

diagnostic.probe_completed

learner.state_updated

content.selected

intervention.executed

forecast.updated

contradiction.detected

arbitration.resolved

Every engine should react to defined event classes, not uncontrolled side effects.

16. Canonical data domains
The schema should be final-form from the start.

Evidence tables
response events,

attempts,

timing signals,

behavior signals,

explanation evidence,

normalized evidence,

evidence weights.

Knowledge tables
curriculum docs,

subjects,

topics,

concepts,

graph edges,

formulas,

representations,

misconception classes,

interference edges,

exam blueprint links.

Learner tables
learner state snapshots,

pressure profiles,

fatigue profiles,

confidence profiles,

engagement profiles,

recovery profiles,

preference profiles.

Diagnostic tables
concept states,

topic states,

hypotheses,

hypothesis scores,

misconception instances,

interference events,

false mastery flags,

ambiguity records,

diagnostic probes.

Decision tables
strategy selections,

sequence decisions,

timing decisions,

risk assessments,

adaptation events,

protection rules,

session plans,

content selection logs.

Execution tables
interventions,

intervention steps,

assessments,

generated drills,

explanations,

assignments,

retest blueprints,

pressure simulation plans.

Memory tables
topic memory,

learner memory,

strategy memory,

intervention outcomes,

coach evaluations,

velocity profiles,

forecasts.

Governance tables
decision traces,

contradiction events,

arbitration decisions,

confidence gate hits,

policy hits,

engine health logs.

17. Orchestration loop
The permanent runtime loop is:

Observe
Collect new evidence.

Normalize
Weight it by reliability, freshness, and context.

Update world models
Update concept, topic, learner, and graph-level states.

Diagnose
Generate and rank hypotheses.

Resolve ambiguity
Create probes where certainty is insufficient.

Decide
Choose strategy, order, timing, risk response, and protection posture.

Compose
Construct the session/intervention/assessment.

Execute
Run the selected educational action.

Verify
Check proof gates, repair success, transfer survival, pressure survival, and delayed recall obligations.

Remember
Update memory layers.

Meta-evaluate
Judge coach decision quality.

Govern
Run contradictions, confidence gates, arbitration, and audit completion.

That is the permanent loop.

18. Cross-engine supremacy rules
Not all engines are equal in all situations.

Examples:

Protection Rule Engine may block Session Composer.

Confidence Gate Engine may downgrade a decision from hard action to probe.

Contradiction Check Engine may reopen diagnosis.

Risk Engine may force stabilization over aggressive sequence advance.

Learner State Engine may constrain Timing and Session Composition.

Mastery Proof logic may override naive coverage assumptions.

Adaptation Engine may reroute sequence only within policy bounds.

This is important because your audited current system already proves that cross-engine writes materially affect planning. 


In the final constitution, those power relationships must be explicit.

19. Build doctrine
Because you want the full version from day 1, the doctrine is:

Design final. Schema final. Contracts final. Governance final. Populate progressively, but never with throwaway architecture.

That means:

no temporary intelligence bucket,

no reduced ontology,

no later redefinition of engine rights,

no placeholder governance,

no “we’ll deal with contradictions later.”

Implementation may still follow dependency order.
But the architecture must already reflect the whole truth.

20. Final constitutional statement
The academic coach is a governed adaptive reasoning system.

It is not allowed to:

guess when it should test,

certify when it should verify,

escalate when it should protect,

repeat when it should adapt,

simplify when the learner is more complex,

or plan without trace.

Its permanent organs are:

evidence,

knowledge,

learner,

diagnosis,

decision,

execution design,

memory,

and governance.

Its permanent duties are:

understand,

intervene,

verify,

remember,

and correct itself.

That is the architecture to lock.

The next document after this should be the Canonical Ontology and Data Constitution, where every state, event, table, field, enum, and edge type is defined formally.


proceed

I’m moving from the constitutional layer into the formal ontology/data layer now: exact state classes, event types, entities, and cross-engine records the full brain needs to operate cleanly.

Thought for 22s
Good. Below is the Canonical Ontology and Data Constitution for the academic coach intelligence system.

This is the document that turns the full constitution into a precise machine-usable structure: state classes, event classes, entities, ownership, and cross-engine records.

Your audited base already gives us the right seed shape: 6 real engines, 53 algorithms, 12,068 lines, with persisted outputs like ic_topic_teaching, ic_intervention_history, ic_timing_decisions, ic_risk_assessments, and ic_adaptation_log already forming the beginning of a durable intelligence data layer.

Canonical Ontology and Data Constitution
1. Purpose of this constitution
This document defines the permanent data language of the coach.

It answers:

what the coach is allowed to believe,

what kinds of states may exist,

what evidence types are valid,

what records must persist,

what events may occur,

how engines exchange truth,

and what invariants prevent corruption.

The goal is that every engine in the full system speaks the same ontology.

Without this, the system becomes:

semantically inconsistent,

hard to audit,

impossible to scale,

and vulnerable to silent contradictions.

2. Core design rules
Rule 1: every important judgment must become a first-class record
No hidden “computed in memory and discarded” truth for major intelligence decisions.

If the coach diagnoses something important, plans something important, blocks something important, or learns something important, it must persist.

Rule 2: every state must carry confidence
The system should almost never store raw categorical truth without:

confidence,

evidence support,

freshness,

provenance,

and last-updated metadata.

Rule 3: every durable record must separate observation from inference
For example:

response_event is observation.

retrieval_weakness hypothesis is inference.

Those must never be collapsed into one thing.

Rule 4: every major entity must have a stable identity
Topics, concepts, misconceptions, probes, mastery contracts, decisions, interventions, and contradictions all need durable ids.

Rule 5: engine write ownership must be explicit
Each engine should have clear write authority over certain records, even if many engines can read them.

Rule 6: no one-table dumping
Do not create a vague “brain_state” table that stores mixed truth. That will destroy clarity.

3. Canonical namespace structure
I would define these namespaces permanently.

j_   = knowledge graph / canonical domain knowledge
c_   = coach / learner operational domain
ic_  = intelligence core derived state and decisions
ie_  = intelligence evidence raw/normalized events
im_  = intelligence memory / longitudinal patterns
ig_  = intelligence governance / audit / contradictions
ix_  = intelligence execution artifacts
So examples become:

j_topics

j_concepts

c_learners

ie_response_events

ic_topic_states

ic_hypotheses

ix_interventions

im_strategy_memory

ig_decision_traces

This is much cleaner than letting everything accumulate under one prefix.

4. Identity constitution
Every major object gets a stable id and a version model.

4.1 Entity id format
Use durable ids like:

subj_...
topic_...
concept_...
learner_...
sess_...
resp_...
evp_...      // evidence packet
hyp_...
probe_...
mc_...       // misconception instance/class
intf_...     // interference event/edge
mproof_...   // mastery proof contract
risk_...
adapt_...
plan_...
interv_...
assign_...
trace_...
contr_...
forecast_...
4.2 Versioning rule
Important records should support revision history, especially:

topic state,

learner state,

hypothesis ranking,

mastery proof contracts,

session plans,

intervention designs,

forecasts,

policy rules.

At minimum, they need:

version_no

supersedes_id

is_current

created_at

created_by_engine

19. Permanent invariants
These should be enforced in code and, where possible, at the database layer.

Invariant 1
A mastery_verdict = mastery_proven must have an active mastery contract with required gates met.

Invariant 2
A hard_block protection rule must be considered before any session plan becomes executable.

Invariant 3
A contradiction of class mastery_vs_transfer cannot remain open while a fresh mastery-proven verdict is considered final.

Invariant 4
A hypothesis cannot be confirmed without evidence links of sufficient support strength.

Invariant 5
A pressure-readiness decision cannot exist if there is zero pressure evidence and zero probe result covering pressure.

Invariant 6
A forecast older than the latest material topic/risk/adaptation changes becomes stale.

Invariant 7
Any adaptation marked reroute must reference a prior plan or strategy being replaced.

Invariant 8
Evidence packets must always trace back to at least one raw or imported source.

Invariant 9
No topic may be marked low-risk if a critical unresolved prerequisite-chain risk exists.

Invariant 10
Decision traces are mandatory for:

mastery verdicts,

adaptation events,

sequence plans,

protection rules,

risk assessments above medium,

and session plans marked high confidence.
