22. Final doctrine of this data constitution
The coach must store not just what happened, but what it thinks, why it thinks it, how sure it is, what changed, what conflicts, and what must happen next.

That means the permanent data posture is:

observation is first-class,

inference is first-class,

proof is first-class,

contradiction is first-class,

memory is first-class,

governance is first-class.

That is the only way a full day-1 intelligence engine remains coherent as it grows.

The next logical document is the Decision Contract and Orchestration Constitution, where every engine gets exact inputs, outputs, precedence rules, and conflict-resolution behavior.


proceed

I’m defining the actual operating law now: who decides what, who can challenge what, the order of execution, and how conflicts get resolved so the full brain can run without hidden power struggles.

Thought for 15s
Below is the Decision Contract and Orchestration Constitution for the academic coach intelligence system.

This is the layer that makes the full brain operable. The ontology tells the system what can exist. This document tells it:

who may decide what,

what evidence threshold each engine needs,

what each engine may not do,

when an engine must defer,

how decisions move through the runtime loop,

how conflicts are resolved,

and how the final coaching action is released safely.

Decision Contract and Orchestration Constitution
1. Purpose
A full intelligence system fails when engine boundaries are vague.

Without decision contracts, you get:

duplicate reasoning,

conflicting outputs,

hidden overrides,

premature certainty,

unsafe escalation,

and silent corruption of trust.

This constitution prevents that by defining:

jurisdiction,

decision hardness,

precedence,

challenge rights,

release conditions,

and governance hooks.

The coach is therefore treated as a constitutional multi-engine system, not a pile of smart modules.

2. The five classes of engine authority
Every engine decision must belong to one of these authority classes.

Class A — Observational authority
These engines may record what happened, normalize it, and score evidence quality.

They may not diagnose or prescribe.

Examples:

Response Evidence Engine

Behavior Evidence Engine

Session Evidence Engine

Evidence Normalization Engine

Class B — Inferential authority
These engines may infer likely states and explanations from evidence.

They may not directly schedule learner-facing action unless explicitly delegated.

Examples:

Concept State Engine

Topic State Engine

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Misconception Detection Engine

Interference Detection Engine

Learner State Engine

Class C — Decisional authority
These engines may choose strategy, timing, order, risk posture, and protection posture.

They may not directly bypass governance.

Examples:

Teaching Strategy Engine

Sequencing Engine

Timing Engine

Risk Engine

Adaptation Engine

Protection Rule Engine

Class D — Construction authority
These engines may turn approved decisions into executable educational artifacts.

They may not redefine upstream diagnosis.

Examples:

Session Composer Engine

Intervention Design Engine

Drill Construction Engine

Assessment Construction Engine

Explanation Construction Engine

Class E — Governance authority
These engines may challenge, downgrade, block, arbitrate, and trace decisions.

They may not fabricate observational truth.

Examples:

Confidence Gate Engine

Contradiction Check Engine

Decision Arbitration Engine

Policy Guardrail Engine

Audit Trace Engine

3. Decision hardness model
Every engine output must declare its hardness.

This prevents weak internal advice from accidentally acting like law.

Hardness levels
1. Advisory
A soft signal for downstream consideration.

Example:
“Visual explanation may help.”

2. Preferred
A stronger recommendation that should be followed unless contradicted.

Example:
“Use contrast repair first.”

3. Required
A decision that downstream engines must respect unless formally overridden by governance.

Example:
“Retest required before topic advancement.”

4. Blocking
A constraint that prevents certain actions.

Example:
“No timing pressure today.”

5. Constitutional
A system law that cannot be bypassed by ordinary engines.

Example:
“No mastery-proven verdict without proof gates.”

Only governance engines may emit constitutional-level enforcement.

4. Global orchestration doctrine
The full coach runs as a governed decision pipeline.

The runtime is not random. It follows this constitutional order:

Stage 1: Observe
Evidence engines record and normalize events.

Stage 2: Interpret
Inferential engines update states and hypotheses.

Stage 3: Diagnose
Diagnostic engines rank explanations, detect misconceptions, detect interference, and identify ambiguity.

Stage 4: Decide
Decision engines choose strategy, sequence, timing, risk posture, adaptation posture, and protection posture.

Stage 5: Construct
Construction engines produce session plans, interventions, drills, assessments, and explanations.

Stage 6: Govern
Governance engines run contradictions, confidence gates, policy checks, arbitration, and tracing.

Stage 7: Release
Only after governance passes may the learner-facing plan be released.

Stage 8: Execute
The session or intervention runs.

Stage 9: Verify
Proof gates, outcomes, and response patterns are evaluated.

Stage 10: Learn
Memory and meta-learning engines update long-horizon patterns.

No stage may silently skip governance.

5. Engine jurisdiction constitution
Below is the jurisdiction map.

A. Evidence engines
Response Evidence Engine
May decide

correctness extraction,

latency extraction,

attempt counting,

skip detection,

answer revision markers.

May not decide

mastery,

weakness cause,

intervention type,

learner state.

Required input quality

raw event present.

Minimum output

normalized response record,

observation confidence,

source linkage.

Behavior Evidence Engine
May decide

hesitation markers,

persistence markers,

abandon markers,

rush markers.

May not decide

disengagement state as final truth,

fatigue as final truth,

confidence collapse as final truth.

It can only provide evidence toward such inferences.

Evidence Normalization Engine
May decide

evidence weight,

freshness class,

trust adjustment,

evidence polarity.

May not decide

whether a topic is weak,

whether a learner is pressure-vulnerable,

whether a strategy should switch.

It shapes evidence only.

B. Learner-state engines
Learner State Engine
May decide

learner state snapshot,

confidence fragility estimate,

fatigue estimate,

engagement state estimate,

abstraction tolerance estimate,

hint dependence estimate.

May not decide

teaching strategy,

content selection,

final risk severity,

sequence advancement.

May challenge

Timing decisions,

Session composition,

Pressure exposure recommendations.

Its output is constraining, not prescriptive.

Pressure Profile Engine
May decide

whether the learner has evidence of pressure sensitivity,

pressure-response pattern,

pressure recovery profile.

May not decide

whether pressure should be used next.

That belongs to Timing + Protection + Governance.

Recovery Profile Engine
May decide

expected recovery speed after failure,

likely recovery fit by intervention family.

May not decide

direct session plan structure.

C. Diagnostic engines
Concept State Engine
May decide

concept readiness estimate,

concept fragility estimate,

concept transfer readiness estimate,

concept pressure readiness estimate,

concept explanation strength estimate.

May not decide

topic certification,

session layout,

final intervention object.

Topic State Engine
May decide

topic state,

topic readiness,

topic fragility,

topic proof-progress score.

May not decide

sequence ordering by itself,

strategy family,

risk protection.

Hypothesis Competition Engine
May decide

ranked explanation families,

posterior scores,

ambiguity score,

unresolved explanatory set.

May not decide

final learner-facing action,

mastery certification,

retest schedule.

Must provide

top competing hypotheses,

support and counterevidence,

what evidence is missing.

Misconception Detection Engine
May decide

presence of likely misconception instances,

misconception class,

recurrence status,

severity.

May not decide

mastery as impossible forever,

full intervention design.

Interference Detection Engine
May decide

likely concept collisions,

interference type,

severity,

recommended need for separation.

May not decide

exact future sequence,

final session plan.

False Mastery Engine
May decide

whether apparent success is suspect,

which proof gaps invalidate strong readiness claims.

May not decide

final progression ban without protection/governance review.

Ambiguity Resolver Engine
May decide

uncertainty is too high,

a probe is required,

which probe families have the highest information gain.

May not decide

to act as if diagnosis is settled.

Diagnostic Experiment Engine
May decide

which probe to run,

expected information gain,

ambiguity target.

May not decide

long-term strategy independently.

Its role is diagnostic clarification, not full coaching command.

D. Decision engines
Teaching Strategy Engine
May decide

strategy family,

primary instructional approach,

fallback strategy family.

May not decide

final session block ordering,

detailed content artifact,

override hard protection rules.

Required inputs

dominant or bounded hypothesis set,

learner state,

topic state,

misconception/interference status.

Sequencing Engine
May decide

topic order,

bridge insertion,

separation constraints,

pivot triggers,

subject arbitration proposals.

May not decide

direct mastery certification,

timing-pressure readiness,

learner fatigue override.

Timing Engine
May decide

review timing,

retest timing,

delayed recall timing,

pacing recommendations,

readiness proposal for pressure exposure.

May not decide

pressure escalation if blocked by learner state or protection rules,

sequence advancement,

strategy replacement.

Risk Engine
May decide

risk class,

severity,

velocity,

drivers,

warning level,

protective implication proposals.

May not decide

direct session construction,

content selection,

final arbitration when conflicts emerge.

Adaptation Engine
May decide

reopen diagnosis,

reroute,

refine,

compress,

protect,

stabilize,

escalate.

May not decide

violate dwell rules without governance visibility,

erase prior decision traces,

fabricate new evidence.

Protection Rule Engine
May decide

blocks,

prohibitions,

temporary no-go zones,

downgrade requirements.

May not decide

positive session design,

final strategy family by itself.

This engine has negative authority. It tells the system what must not happen.

Multi-Subject Arbitration Engine
May decide

comparative urgency ranking across subjects,

allocation proposals under time scarcity.

May not decide

per-topic proof status,

local diagnostic truth.

E. Construction engines
Session Composer Engine
May decide

session block structure,

block order,

time allocation,

activity rhythm,

closing design.

May not decide

change upstream diagnosis,

ignore protection rules,

certify mastery.

Content Selection Engine
May decide

which artifact best fits the session request,

content fitness by goal,

when content insufficiency exists.

May not decide

change strategy family,

declare learner state.

Intervention Design Engine
May decide

step sequence of intervention,

success conditions,

retry conditions,

expected evidence kinds.

May not decide

reopen diagnosis without adaptation/governance basis.

Drill Construction Engine
May decide

drill family,

distractor structure,

repetition profile,

transfer stress level.

May not decide

whether the learner should take that drill now if blocked by fatigue or protection rules.

Assessment Construction Engine
May decide

targeted assessment blueprint,

item balance,

timing mode within approved bounds.

May not decide

use timing pressure contrary to Timing/Protection/Governance.

Explanation Construction Engine
May decide

representation mode,

explanation depth,

modality fit.

May not decide

whether explanation is the best intervention family overall.

F. Memory and meta-learning engines
Topic Memory Engine
May decide

durable topic narrative,

recurrence summary,

historical instability pattern.

May not decide

real-time instructional choice directly.

Its role is support and constraint.

Learner Memory Engine
May decide

durable learner tendencies,

repeated pressure patterns,

successful teaching families,

recurring avoidance patterns.

May not decide

replace current evidence with stale memory.

Memory informs, but freshness still matters.

Strategy Memory Engine
May decide

historical strategy fit by context,

diminishing returns risk.

May not decide

force a strategy if current diagnosis strongly contradicts it.

Coach Self-Evaluation Engine
May decide

whether the system likely diagnosed poorly,

whether it overreacted or underreacted,

whether strategy fit was weak.

May not decide

rewrite history silently.

It must produce explicit evaluation records.

Forecast Engine
May decide

projected readiness,

projected risk,

scenario summaries.

May not decide

override fresh protection rules,

certify present mastery from future projection.

G. Governance engines
Confidence Gate Engine
May decide

downgrade action hardness,

require probes,

prevent strong actions from thin evidence.

May not decide

invent new diagnosis.

Contradiction Check Engine
May decide

contradiction exists,

contradiction class,

escalation priority,

need to reopen diagnosis or arbitration.

May not decide

choose final winner among conflicting engines.

Decision Arbitration Engine
May decide

which engine output governs in a conflict,

what rationale resolves the conflict,

whether a new diagnostic cycle is required.

May not decide

suppress traceability,

bypass policy guardrails.

Policy Guardrail Engine
May decide

constitutional violations,

action invalidation,

hard system blocks.

May not decide

session design details.

Audit Trace Engine
May decide

completeness of reason chain,

whether a decision is releasable from an audit standpoint.

May not decide

strategy substance.

6. Engine precedence constitution
When multiple engines matter at once, this is the precedence law.

Highest precedence
Policy Guardrail Engine

Confidence Gate Engine

Protection Rule Engine

These can block release.

Next precedence
Contradiction Check Engine

Decision Arbitration Engine

These resolve inconsistency.

Next precedence
Learner State Engine

Risk Engine

False Mastery / Proof-related engines

These heavily constrain action.

Next precedence
Adaptation Engine

Timing Engine

Sequencing Engine

Teaching Strategy Engine

These shape the main plan.

Next precedence
Session Composer Engine

Content Selection Engine

Construction engines

These implement within constraints.

Lowest precedence
Forecast and memory engines as advisory context, unless explicitly elevated by governance rules.

This order matters because the system must privilege:

safety,

evidence sufficiency,

learner fragility,

and internal consistency
before elegance of plan.

7. Mandatory input contracts
No engine may run on arbitrary half-inputs if the contract says more is required.

A. Teaching Strategy Engine minimum contract
It must have:

topic state,

learner state,

either a dominant hypothesis or bounded ambiguity,

misconception/interference snapshot where relevant,

current protection rules.

If ambiguity is too high and no probe was attempted, strategy hardness must be downgraded.

B. Sequencing Engine minimum contract
It must have:

topic states,

prerequisite graph,

current risk assessments,

learner load constraints,

interference edges,

exam horizon or planning horizon.

C. Timing Engine minimum contract
It must have:

proof progress or mastery gate state,

learner fatigue/pressure state,

recent timing evidence,

review/retest obligations.

D. Session Composer minimum contract
It must have:

approved teaching strategy,

approved timing posture,

active protection rules,

learner state,

content availability or insufficiency signal.

E. Mastery-verdict release minimum contract
It must have:

mastery contract,

required gate statuses,

contradiction check result,

confidence gate pass,

trace record.

No exceptions.

8. Challenge rights constitution
This system must allow engines to challenge one another.

Who can challenge diagnosis?
Contradiction Check Engine

Coach Self-Evaluation Engine

Diagnostic Experiment Engine

Confidence Gate Engine

Who can challenge sequencing?
Interference Detection Engine

Learner State Engine

Risk Engine

Protection Rule Engine

Who can challenge timing?
Pressure Profile Engine

Fatigue Profile Engine

Protection Rule Engine

Confidence Gate Engine

Who can challenge strategy choice?
Strategy Memory Engine

Learner State Engine

Contradiction Check Engine

Coach Self-Evaluation Engine

Who can challenge session composition?
Protection Rule Engine

Learner State Engine

Risk Engine

Policy Guardrail Engine

Who can challenge mastery verdict?
False Mastery Engine

Contradiction Check Engine

Confidence Gate Engine

Policy Guardrail Engine

Challenge rights are not optional. They are part of the constitutional immune system.

9. Escalation pathways
When certain conditions occur, the system must escalate instead of continuing normally.

Escalation type 1: evidence insufficiency
Triggered when:

ambiguity remains high,

evidence freshness is poor,

evidence strength is weak,

major action is being proposed.

Required action:

probe creation,

action downgrade,

or temporary stabilization plan.

Escalation type 2: learner fragility
Triggered when:

confidence fragile/collapsed,

fatigue high/severe,

pressure vulnerability high,

repeated recent failure.

Required action:

protective mode,

reduced pressure,

confidence-aware session design,

no aggressive sequencing.

Escalation type 3: contradiction
Triggered when:

two major outputs are incompatible.

Required action:

contradiction record,

arbitration,

optionally reopened diagnosis.

Escalation type 4: repeated failed adaptation
Triggered when:

several successive reroutes or refinements fail.

Required action:

deep diagnostic reopen,

intervention family rethink,

maybe cross-domain cause search.

Escalation type 5: forecast-risk divergence
Triggered when:

present state seems stable but future projection deteriorates.

Required action:

plan review,

possible time-budget or sequence correction.

10. Orchestration loop in exact operational form
Here is the more concrete runtime flow.

Cycle 1 — evidence intake
response.recorded

evidence engines create normalized packets

evidence lineage stored

learner/session evidence aggregates updated

Cycle 2 — state update
concept states updated

topic states updated

learner state snapshot updated

proof-related gate observations updated

Cycle 3 — diagnosis
hypotheses reranked

misconceptions checked

interference checked

false mastery checked

ambiguity measured

Cycle 4 — decision proposal
strategy proposal emitted

timing proposal emitted

sequence impact proposal emitted if needed

risk assessment emitted

adaptation event proposed if thresholds met

protection rules refreshed

Cycle 5 — construction
session plan composed

content selected

intervention or drill artifacts generated

assessment or retest built if needed

Cycle 6 — governance
contradiction checks run

confidence gates run

policy guardrails run

arbitration runs if conflicts remain

decision trace assembled

Cycle 7 — release
plan status set to releasable

learner-facing action published

Cycle 8 — post-execution learning
intervention outcomes logged

memory engines update

self-evaluation runs

forecast refreshed if materially needed

That is the permanent operational choreography.

11. Conflict-resolution constitution
Conflicts must be resolved formally.

Conflict family A: diagnosis conflict
Example:

Hypothesis engine favors retrieval weakness.

Misconception engine strongly signals rule confusion.

Resolution order:

check evidence strength by family,

check ambiguity score,

if unresolved, Diagnostic Experiment Engine proposes probe,

Teaching Strategy hardens only to bounded or blended mode,

Confidence Gate may downgrade action.

Conflict family B: learner-state vs timing conflict
Example:

Timing wants pressure exposure.

Learner state says fatigue high and pressure vulnerable.

Resolution:

learner fragility outranks pressure ambition,

protection rule likely emitted,

timing downgraded or deferred.

Conflict family C: sequencing vs risk conflict
Example:

sequence says advance to next topic,

risk says critical prerequisite instability remains.

Resolution:

risk + prerequisite integrity outrank advance,

sequence may insert bridge or rollback.

Conflict family D: strategy vs memory conflict
Example:

Teaching Strategy wants worked-example-first,

Strategy Memory says this repeatedly underperformed for this learner in similar contexts.

Resolution:

strategy memory triggers challenge,

arbitration weighs current evidence vs historical fit,

output may become alternate or blended strategy.

Conflict family E: proof vs coverage conflict
Example:

Coverage appears broad,

proof gates remain weak.

Resolution:

proof outranks exposure counts,

topic cannot be considered secure.

12. Blended decision doctrine
The system must sometimes combine engines instead of forcing one winner.

Three valid forms exist:

Blended diagnosis
Two or more plausible causes remain active.
Example:

retrieval weakness + pressure weakness.

Blended strategy
One session may include:

confidence rebuild,

retrieval reinforcement,

light contrast repair.

Blended sequencing
A path may combine:

rescue-first locally,

dependency-first globally.

Blending is allowed only when:

the combination is coherent,

protection rules are respected,

and ambiguity is openly represented.

Blending must never be used to hide unresolved confusion.

13. Reopen rules
The system needs formal rules for when prior settled decisions must be reopened.

Reopen diagnosis when:
contradiction emerges,

repeated repair fails,

strong counterevidence appears,

probe result overturns prior belief,

false mastery flag appears.

Reopen timing when:
fatigue changes materially,

pressure profile shifts,

missed review obligations accumulate,

risk velocity increases sharply.

Reopen sequence when:
prerequisite instability emerges,

time horizon changes materially,

subject competition changes,

interference becomes newly visible.

Reopen strategy when:
intervention outcome weak,

learner state changes materially,

memory shows repeated mismatch,

self-evaluation flags poor fit.

Every reopen action must produce an explicit record, not a silent overwrite.

14. Release gate constitution
A learner-facing plan may only be released if all these pass.

Gate 1 — data sufficiency
Enough fresh evidence exists for the hardness of the action proposed.

Gate 2 — contradiction status
No unresolved critical contradiction remains.

Gate 3 — policy compliance
No constitutional rule has been hit.

Gate 4 — protection compliance
No blocking protection rule is violated.

Gate 5 — audit completeness
Trace exists for all major decisions.

Gate 6 — learner-state compatibility
The proposed session is compatible with current learner fragility and fatigue.

Gate 7 — content fitness
The selected content actually fits the goal.

If any gate fails, release is denied or downgraded.

15. Timing of recomputation
Not every engine should recompute at the same cadence.

Per response or micro-batch
evidence normalization

concept state local update

learner micro-state update

hypothesis rerank for targeted topics

Per session
topic state refresh

risk refresh

session-level adaptation consideration

intervention outcomes

self-evaluation

Per day or planning horizon
sequencing

forecast

review debt recalculation

subject arbitration

On trigger only
deep diagnostic probe design

contradiction arbitration

policy escalation

This protects the system from both sluggishness and thrash.

16. Anti-thrash constitution
A full intelligence system can become unstable if it switches too often.

So define these laws:

Law 1
No repeated strategy reroute without materially new evidence unless risk is critical.

Law 2
No sequence churn inside a protected dwell window except for safety reasons.

Law 3
No timing reversal more than once inside the same local proof cycle without traceable cause.

Law 4
No repeated diagnostic reopening for the same topic without a new uncertainty pattern or failed intervention.

Law 5
No oscillation between blended and singular diagnosis without an explicit ambiguity change.

These are crucial because smart systems often fail through instability, not ignorance.

17. Session-level orchestration contract
For each planned session, the runtime should produce a Session Decision Envelope.

This envelope contains:

session_decision_envelope {
  learner_state_ref
  topic_state_refs
  dominant_hypothesis_refs
  active_misconception_refs
  active_interference_refs
  approved_strategy_ref
  approved_timing_ref
  active_protection_rules
  active_risk_refs
  session_plan_ref
  selected_content_refs
  governance_status
  trace_ref
}
The session is not executable without this envelope.

18. Constitutional trace requirements
For these actions, a trace is mandatory:

mastery verdict,

adaptation reroute,

protection block,

critical or high risk assessment,

sequence plan creation or material modification,

pressure exposure approval,

contradiction resolution,

final session release.

The trace must include:

source evidence ids,

contributing engine outputs,

final rationale,

confidence,

challenger engines if any,

arbitration record if any,

policy hits if any.

19. What the orchestrator actually does
The orchestrator is not the “smartest engine.”

Its constitutional role is narrower and cleaner.

It:

invokes stages in the correct order,

gathers engine outputs,

enforces contracts,

routes conflicts to governance,

manages release gates,

persists traces,

and ensures no engine acts outside jurisdiction.

It does not replace:

hypothesis reasoning,

sequencing reasoning,

learner-state reasoning,

or governance reasoning.

The orchestrator is a constitutional executor, not a shadow dictator.

20. Final doctrine
A full day-1 intelligence system is not defined just by many engines. It is defined by disciplined power.

This constitution establishes that discipline:

evidence engines observe,

inferential engines explain,

decision engines choose,

construction engines build,

governance engines challenge and protect,

memory engines teach the system over time,

and the orchestrator enforces constitutional order.

That is what prevents the coach from becoming:

overconfident,

inconsistent,

unstable,

or impossible to trust.

The next document should be the Engine-by-Engine Technical Spec, starting with the deepest core:
Hypothesis Competition Engine, Learner State Engine, Misconception Detection Engine, Interference Detection Engine, and Session Composer Engine.

Thought for 22s
Good. Here is the Engine-by-Engine Technical Spec for the deepest core of the coach brain.

I am starting with the 5 engines that most directly determine whether the system is truly intelligent or merely well-organized:

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Learner State Engine

Misconception Detection Engine

Interference Detection Engine

Session Composer Engine

These specs assume the full constitutional model we already locked, and they intentionally expand the real nucleus you already have. In the audit, Topic Intelligence already contains a 3-layer hypothesis system, a 17-metric evidence spine, a 6-checkpoint mastery proof contract, and 9 teaching modes with reroute logic, while Adaptation already builds a learner adaptive profile and stop list.

1. Hypothesis Competition Engine
Mission
This engine decides why the learner is struggling, but it does so without collapsing uncertainty too early.

Its job is not to guess one cause. Its job is to maintain a ranked field of plausible causes, update them as evidence changes, and request disambiguation when confidence is not good enough.

This engine is the formal expansion of the audited 3-layer hypothesis system currently living inside Topic Intelligence. 


What it owns
It owns:

competing explanatory hypotheses,

posterior confidence,

ambiguity score,

support/counterevidence structure,

probe recommendations,

dominant-cause handoff to decision engines.

It does not own:

final strategy selection,

session design,

sequencing,

mastery verdicts.

Canonical hypothesis families
At minimum, the engine must support:

prerequisite_weakness

knowledge_deficit

misconception

retrieval_weakness

transfer_weakness

pressure_performance

confidence_emotional

fatigue_mediated

representation_mismatch

interference

procedural_instability

mixed_context_fragility

explanation_gap

false_fluency

Your current audited engine already uses 7 families and adjusts them using evidence spine signals and intervention memory. The full engine formalizes and expands that. 


Inputs
Primary inputs:

ic_concept_states

ic_topic_states

ie_evidence_packets

ic_misconception_instances

ic_interference_events

ic_learner_state_snapshots

im_strategy_memory

im_intervention_outcomes

ic_mastery_gates

ic_diagnostic_probe_results

Secondary inputs:

curriculum topology

representation metadata

question-type metadata

timing history

recent session block history

Outputs
Primary writes:

ic_hypotheses

ic_hypothesis_evidence_links

ic_ambiguity_records

Secondary emits:

hypothesis.created

hypothesis.re_ranked

diagnostic.probe_requested

Internal submodules
This engine should be split internally into 7 submodules.

A. Candidate Generator
Produces the initial set of plausible hypotheses from state patterns.

Examples:

low transfer + okay accuracy → transfer_weakness candidate

timed failure + untimed success → pressure_performance candidate

repeated same wrong rule → misconception candidate

weak delayed recall + decent immediate solve → retrieval_weakness candidate

B. Prior Estimator
Assigns base priors from:

concept topology,

learner history,

topic family,

prior recurrence patterns,

known fragility profiles.

C. Evidence Matcher
Evaluates every new evidence packet against every active hypothesis.

It should classify evidence as:

supportive,

weakening,

neutral,

or contradictory.

D. Counterevidence Handler
Separately tracks evidence that should reduce confidence.

This is important because many systems only count support and become biased.

E. Posterior Updater
Recomputes posterior scores after each meaningful evidence update.

F. Ambiguity Scorer
Measures whether the hypothesis landscape is still too flat or too conflicted.

G. Probe Recommender
Selects the highest-value diagnostic experiment when ambiguity is too high.

Core scoring logic
Use a layered posterior model, similar in spirit to your existing audited 3-layer system, but cleaner. 


A workable structure:

posterior = normalize(prior + evidence_support - counterevidence + memory_adjustment + learner_context_adjustment + probe_adjustment)

Where:

prior = baseline plausibility

evidence_support = weighted positive evidence

counterevidence = weighted negative evidence

memory_adjustment = if similar cases historically resolved to this cause

learner_context_adjustment = learner-specific modifiers

probe_adjustment = extra certainty from diagnostic probes

Example evidence mapping
high hint dependence → boosts prerequisite_weakness, knowledge_deficit

delayed recall fail → boosts retrieval_weakness

timed-only failure → boosts pressure_performance

recurring same distractor pattern → boosts misconception

format-specific collapse → boosts representation_mismatch

after learning adjacent concept, old concept worsens → boosts interference

Ambiguity logic
The engine must compute an explicit ambiguity score.

Example:

ambiguity = 1 - (top_hypothesis_score - second_hypothesis_score)

Then adjust upward if:

both top hypotheses have strong support,

evidence diversity is poor,

recent data is stale,

probe coverage is absent.

Ambiguity response law
low ambiguity → downstream engines may consume dominant hypothesis with moderate hardness

medium ambiguity → strategy may be blended

high ambiguity → hard decisions downgraded, probe recommended

State machine
Each hypothesis moves through:

proposed

active

downgraded

confirmed

falsified

archived

Rules:

cannot become confirmed without multi-source support

cannot become falsified from one weak counterexample

archived hypotheses remain queryable for recurrence analysis

Failure modes
This engine is at risk of:

Cause collapse
Choosing one explanation too early.

Memory bias
Over-trusting historical patterns over fresh evidence.

Evidence imbalance
Overweighting accuracy and underweighting timing, transfer, or delayed recall.

Hypothesis stickiness
Failing to reopen after new evidence.

Duplicate labeling
Treating misconception and knowledge deficit as the same thing.

Guardrails
Confidence Gate Engine must downgrade strong actions when ambiguity stays high.

Contradiction Check Engine may reopen diagnosis if downstream behavior disagrees.

Probe Recommender must activate when evidence is thin.

Observability
Track:

top hypothesis churn rate,

ambiguity trend,

probe yield,

false-confirmation rate,

time-to-stable-diagnosis,

diagnosis reversal rate.

Success criterion
A good Hypothesis Competition Engine does not merely “label weakness.”
It reduces diagnostic error over time and makes strategy selection materially more correct.

2. Learner State Engine
Mission
This engine models the learner as a living system, not a score container.

Its job is to answer:

what state is this learner currently in,

what kind of instruction can they absorb now,

what kind of pressure is safe,

how likely are they to recover after failure,

and how much instability is coming from the learner condition rather than the topic itself.

Your current audited Adaptation engine already builds a learner adaptive profile from intervention report, pressure response, confidence scaffolding need, and teaching mode preferences. This full engine is the formal expansion of that profile into a first-class state system. 


What it owns
It owns:

current learner state snapshot,

pressure profile,

fatigue profile,

recovery profile,

engagement drift,

hint dependence,

abstraction tolerance,

mode affinity.

It does not own:

direct strategy choice,

direct timing schedule,

final risk judgment.

It constrains those other engines.

Inputs
ie_response_events

ie_behavior_events

ie_session_events

ie_external_observations

ic_topic_states

ic_concept_states

im_learner_memory

im_intervention_outcomes

c_session_runtime_metrics

ic_diagnostic_probe_results

Outputs
Writes:

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_pressure_profiles

ic_recovery_profiles

ic_learning_preference_profiles

Emits:

learner.state.updated

Internal state axes
The learner state must be multi-axis.

Confidence axis
stable

fragile

collapsed

rebuilding

Pressure axis
tolerant

vulnerable

untested

collapsing

Fatigue axis
low

medium

high

severe

Engagement axis
engaged

neutral

drifting

avoiding

Recovery axis
fast

moderate

slow

impaired

Abstraction axis
concrete_preferring

balanced

abstract_capable

Hint dependence axis
low

medium

high

Attention stability axis
stable

variable

fragmented

Internal submodules
A. Session Condition Analyzer
Looks at same-session indicators:

latency drift,

abandon behavior,

repeated idle intervals,

late-session error inflation,

hint escalation.

B. Pressure Response Analyzer
Compares timed and untimed evidence.

C. Recovery Analyzer
Measures what happens after:

a wrong answer,

correction,

reteach,

contrast intervention,

failed retest.

D. Confidence Fragility Analyzer
Detects cases where:

small failure causes major behavioral collapse,

learner stops attempting,

latency spikes after error,

easy items are later missed due to shaken confidence.

E. Engagement Drift Analyzer
Detects:

slower starts,

incomplete sessions,

repeated skipping,

partial compliance with assignments.

F. Mode Affinity Learner
Learns which delivery patterns tend to work:

visual-first,

worked-example-first,

contrast-first,

retrieval-first,

guided-to-independent,

compression.

Core logic examples
Pressure score
pressure_delta = untimed_accuracy - timed_accuracy
pressure_vulnerability = f(pressure_delta, timed_latency_instability, timed_error_pattern)

Fatigue score
Use within-session slope:

later block accuracy drop

later block latency increase

hint usage spike late in session

quit probability

Recovery score
Track outcome quality after recent failure:

next attempt correctness

time to regain baseline

effectiveness of encouragement/reteach

Confidence fragility score
Signals:

sharp drop after one mistake

avoidance after correction

underperformance on previously secure content after emotional disruption

State update doctrine
The engine should not overreact to one event.

Use a rolling update model:

fast channel for live session signals

slow channel for durable tendencies

Example:
current_state = 0.7 * recent_window + 0.3 * memory_baseline
but
durable_profile = 0.2 * recent_window + 0.8 * historical_pattern

That lets the coach detect today’s fragile state without erasing long-term truth.

Decision effects
This engine should influence:

Timing
block pressure exposure if learner is fatigued or pressure-vulnerable

Session Composer
shorten sessions when fatigue high

front-load wins when confidence fragile

delay mixed drills when engagement drifting

Strategy
prefer confidence rebuild over exam rescue when learner state is unstable

prefer worked example when abstraction tolerance low

Protection
emit no-new-content pressure protection where appropriate

Failure modes
State conflation
Confusing weak knowledge with weak confidence.

Overfitting to one bad day
Treating temporary fatigue as durable trait.

Under-detecting collapse
Missing emotional or cognitive destabilization because correctness still looks okay.

False mode preference learning
Assuming a mode works because it was used on easier content.

Guardrails
state snapshots must include confidence and freshness

stale learner state cannot overrule fresh evidence

strong learner-state claims require repeated patterns, not single anomalies

Observability
Track:

pressure vulnerability stability,

fatigue detection precision,

recovery prediction quality,

engagement drift lead time,

mode-affinity usefulness.

Success criterion
A good Learner State Engine prevents the system from making technically logical but educationally foolish decisions.

3. Misconception Detection Engine
Mission
This engine detects wrong mental models, not just wrong answers.

Its job is to identify when the learner is applying a consistent but incorrect rule, concept, interpretation, or shortcut.

What it owns
It owns:

misconception detection,

misconception class mapping,

recurrence tracking,

repair status.

It does not own:

full diagnosis by itself,

final teaching strategy,

mastery blocking without governance.

Inputs
ie_response_events

ie_evidence_packets

j_misconception_classes

distractor metadata

open-response explanation artifacts

ic_hypotheses

im_topic_memory

j_concepts

j_formulas

Outputs
Writes:

ic_misconception_instances

Emits:

misconception.detected

Types of misconception
At minimum:

rule_swap

sign_error

formula_confusion

false_generalization

representation_confusion

category_confusion

causal_misread

procedural inversion

near-concept blend

exception blindness

Internal submodules
A. Error Signature Extractor
Builds patterns from:

repeated wrong option selection,

common wrong transformations,

repeated language markers in explanation,

same procedural failure across items.

B. Misconception Matcher
Matches extracted signatures to misconception classes in j_misconception_classes.

C. Open-Ended Reasoning Analyzer
Uses explanation text or step traces to detect wrong reasoning explicitly, not just result error.

D. Recurrence Tracker
Tracks whether the same misconception reappears across:

time,

item format,

topic variation,

pressure conditions.

E. Repair Tracker
Monitors status:

open

in_repair

cleared

recurring

Detection doctrine
A misconception is not “student got it wrong twice.”
It is “student is applying a wrong model with enough consistency to infer structure.”

Detection threshold ideas
A misconception candidate strengthens when:

same error family appears 3+ times,

survives across different item forms,

appears in explanation language,

persists after lightweight correction.

Weaker signals
one-off slip,

random wrong answer,

timed-only noisy error.

These may support hypotheses but should not become strong misconception records immediately.

Example detection flows
Formula confusion
If learner repeatedly substitutes formula A where formula B is needed:

detect concept overlap

detect same substitution pattern

boost misconception class = formula_confusion

False generalization
If learner applies a rule correctly in one narrow context and then incorrectly everywhere:

detect expansion beyond valid scope

class = false_generalization

Representation confusion
If learner succeeds on symbolic items but repeatedly fails diagrammatic equivalents:

class = representation_confusion

Relationship to hypothesis engine
Misconception Detection Engine provides structured evidence to Hypothesis Competition Engine.

It does not replace it.

Example:

hypothesis says “misconception likely”

misconception engine says “specifically sign_error on formula transformation”

That distinction matters.

Relationship to mastery
A misconception instance should strongly block mastery confidence, especially if recurring.

But governance should enforce how strong that block is.

Failure modes
Slip vs misconception confusion
Treating careless mistakes as structural misconception.

Over-labeling
Creating too many misconception records from thin data.

Under-detecting deep misconceptions
Missing wrong models because accuracy appears okay on memorized items.

Taxonomy weakness
Bad misconception library leads to fuzzy detections.

Guardrails
require recurrence or structured explanation evidence for strong labeling

misconception records must carry confidence

cleared misconceptions should remain historically visible

Observability
Track:

misconception recurrence rate,

false-positive misconception labels,

repair success by misconception class,

misconception detection lead time before major collapse.

Success criterion
A good Misconception Detection Engine lets the coach fix the actual wrong idea, not keep rehearsing around it.

4. Interference Detection Engine
Mission
This engine detects when one concept corrupts another.

This is one of the most important advanced engines because many learner problems are not about lack of learning, but about collision between nearby learnings.

You explicitly raised this kind of problem earlier: the learner can know one thing, then learn a similar thing, and the second one destabilizes the first. This engine exists for exactly that.

Your audited system already includes contrast pairs, interference separation in sequencing, and early warning for interference proximity. This full engine makes interference a permanent first-class diagnostic object.

What it owns
It owns:

interference event detection,

concept collision classification,

severity,

recency,

repair need,

separation recommendations.

It does not own:

final sequencing plan,

final contrast session construction.

Inputs
j_interference_edges

ie_evidence_packets

ic_concept_states

ic_topic_states

recent teaching history

ic_misconception_instances

im_topic_memory

ct_contrast_pairs or equivalent contrast metadata

j_representations

Outputs
Writes:

ic_interference_events

Emits:

interference.detected

Types of interference
confusion_pair

sequence_conflict

representation_collision

formula_blend

procedure_blend

term_swap

exception_overwrite

near-neighbor contamination

Internal submodules
A. Pair Candidate Finder
Finds possibly colliding concepts based on:

graph adjacency,

historical confusion pairs,

recent study proximity,

shared representations,

shared lexical patterns.

B. Performance Shift Detector
Looks for pattern:

concept A stable,

concept B recently taught,

concept A worsens soon after.

C. Cross-Activation Analyzer
Checks if learner is importing rules/features from one concept into another.

D. Separation Need Estimator
Computes whether the concepts now require:

spacing,

contrast session,

explicit compare-and-contrast,

re-isolation.

E. Repair Tracker
Marks interference as:

active

monitored

repaired

recurring

Core logic examples
Sequence conflict detection
If concept A performance drops materially within X sessions after concept B instruction, and B is a known neighbor, boost sequence_conflict.

Contrast failure detection
If learner repeatedly misclassifies A as B and B as A across comparison tasks, boost confusion_pair.

Representation collision detection
If learner handles verbal distinctions but fails when both concepts appear symbolically, detect representation_collision.

Severity scoring
Interference severity should combine:

recency of competing instruction,

performance deterioration magnitude,

recurrence count,

exam leverage of affected concepts,

breadth across representations.

Example:
severity = deterioration * recency_weight * recurrence_factor * leverage_factor

Effects on other engines
Sequencing
May impose spacing or anti-adjacency constraints.

Session Composer
May require compare-and-contrast block.

Strategy
May force ContrastRepair or ConceptCompression-like pathways.

Risk
May raise fragility or false-readiness risk.

False Mastery
May downgrade confidence in apparent readiness.

Failure modes
Coincidence misread as interference
Two concepts deteriorate near the same time for unrelated reasons.

Missing multi-way interference
Only detecting pairwise conflict when the real issue is a cluster.

Under-detecting slow contamination
Interference can emerge over time, not just immediately.

Overreacting to adjacency
Not every neighboring concept causes interference.

Guardrails
use topology + behavior + deterioration, not adjacency alone

require evidence of contamination, not just co-study

keep interference records revisable

Observability
Track:

interference recurrence,

interference repair success,

sequence-change benefit after interference detection,

contrast-session effectiveness.

Success criterion
A good Interference Detection Engine catches “learning that breaks learning” before it becomes invisible academic rot.

5. Session Composer Engine
Mission
This engine turns diagnosis and decision into an actual learning experience.

Without this engine, the coach can know a lot and still deliver mediocre sessions.

Its job is to decide:

what the session should do first,

what cognitive path the learner should walk through,

when to test,

when to teach,

when to compare,

when to stop,

and how to end for retention.

What it owns
It owns:

session block order,

activity rhythm,

cognitive pacing,

block duration,

success criteria by block,

closure design.

It does not own:

upstream diagnosis,

high-level strategy family,

policy override.

Inputs
Required:

ic_teaching_decisions

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_topic_states

ic_hypotheses

ic_misconception_instances

ic_interference_events

ic_timing_decisions

ic_protection_rules

ic_risk_assessments

content availability / content_selection_request

Optional but valuable:

topic memory

recent session history

unfinished probe obligations

mastery gate gaps

Outputs
Writes:

ic_session_plans

ic_session_blocks

Emits:

session.plan.created

Session block vocabulary
The engine should compose from a controlled vocabulary such as:

warm_recall

confidence_warmup

diagnostic_probe

misconception_check

focused_reteach

compare_contrast

worked_example

guided_solve

independent_solve

timed_microcheck

mixed_context_check

explanation_check

memory_anchor

recap

handoff_assignment

Internal submodules
A. Objective Resolver
Translates high-level strategy into session purpose.

Examples:

“repair misconception”

“separate confused pair”

“test whether pressure is the real issue”

“stabilize fragile gain”

“rebuild confidence before proof attempt”

B. Block Library Selector
Chooses which block families are even eligible.

C. Block Sequencer
Orders blocks based on learner state and diagnostic target.

D. Pacing Allocator
Sets durations and intensity.

E. Exit Condition Builder
Defines success criteria:

what counts as enough for this block,

when to move on,

when to stop and protect.

F. Closure Planner
Determines whether the session ends with:

recap,

delayed recall setup,

assignment,

retest scheduling,

confidence-preserving finish.

Core composition doctrine
Rule 1: session structure must reflect diagnosis
If dominant issue is misconception, the session must not look like generic drill-first practice.

Rule 2: learner state shapes ordering
If confidence is fragile, start with recoverable success before challenge.
If fatigue high, shorten and compress.
If pressure vulnerability high, do not front-load hard timing.

Rule 3: ambiguity changes session design
If diagnosis uncertainty remains high, early session blocks should include diagnostic probes.

Rule 4: interference requires separation or contrast
If active interference exists, include compare-and-contrast or isolation blocks.

Rule 5: mastery gaps determine end-game
If explanation gate missing, end with explanation check.
If delayed recall needed, hand off retention task.

Example composition patterns
Pattern A: misconception repair session
confidence_warmup

misconception_check

focused_reteach

compare_correct_vs_wrong_rule

guided_solve

independent_solve

explanation_check

memory_anchor

Pattern B: pressure diagnosis session
warm_recall untimed

independent_solve untimed

timed_microcheck

compare outcomes

light strategy coaching

short retest

confidence-stable close

Pattern C: interference repair session
isolated concept A check

isolated concept B check

compare_contrast

boundary cues block

mixed classification

guided transfer

recap of difference markers

Pacing logic
Pacing must combine:

learner fatigue,

learner recovery profile,

difficulty target,

session objective,

recency of failure,

exam pressure.

Example heuristics:

fragile learner → shorter early blocks, early success

high-fatigue → fewer transitions, no heavy mixed context

exam rescue mode → tighter blocks, but only if confidence and fatigue allow

Exit criteria
Every block must have explicit success logic.

Examples:

misconception_check ends once error pattern is clearly confirmed or rejected

worked_example block ends once learner articulates rule correctly

timed_microcheck ends after enough evidence for pressure inference, not after arbitrary item count

Relationship to other engines
Teaching Strategy Engine
Provides the “what kind of teaching” frame.

Timing Engine
Provides pressure and retest constraints.

Protection Rule Engine
Can remove blocks from eligibility.

Content Selection Engine
Provides fitting artifacts for each block.

Mastery Proof system
Defines what session should help prove or repair.

Failure modes
Pretty but irrelevant sessions
Blocks look good but do not target the real problem.

Overpacked sessions
Too many block types for current learner state.

Rigid sessions
No adaptation to live evidence.

Bad closure
Session ends at peak confusion or fatigue, damaging retention.

Guardrails
session plan must include rationale

session must respect all active protection rules

every session must name primary objective and fallback objective

block count should be bounded by learner state and duration

Observability
Track:

block-level success,

session completion quality,

objective attainment,

block transition failure points,

session-to-outcome effectiveness by pattern.

Success criterion
A good Session Composer Engine makes the learner experience feel uncannily well-timed and targeted, because it is.

Cross-engine integration among the 5
These five engines form a tight inner loop:

Hypothesis Competition
says:
“Here are the most plausible causes.”

Learner State
says:
“Here is the learner condition in which any intervention must operate.”

Misconception Detection
says:
“This is not just weakness; this specific wrong model is active.”

Interference Detection
says:
“This concept is being destabilized by that neighboring concept.”

Session Composer
says:
“Given all of the above, this is the cognitive journey the learner should experience next.”

That is the true inner coaching loop.

And it fits the direction already visible in your current audited architecture: hypothesis logic, evidence ingestion, intervention history, learner adaptive profile, cross-engine reads, and study-planner feedback are already real.

What should come next
The next technical spec should cover the next ring around this core:

Teaching Strategy Engine

Timing Engine

Risk Engine

Adaptation Engine

Content Selection Engine

That would complete the operational decision layer around the diagnostic core.

Thought for 11s
Good. Here is the next ring of the core: the operational decision layer around diagnosis.

This layer takes the outputs of the deep diagnostic engines and decides what the coach should actually do next. It also expands the audited nucleus you already have, where Topic Intelligence already selects teaching modes, Timing governs reinforcement and retests, Risk tracks multi-class warnings, and Adaptation manages triggers, velocity, and stop lists.

6. Teaching Strategy Engine
Mission
This engine decides the instructional family that should be used right now for this learner, on this topic, under this diagnosis and state.

It answers:

should we reteach or probe?

should we repair misconception or rebuild confidence?

should we isolate or contrast?

should we use worked example first or retrieval first?

should we stay local or bridge prerequisites?

It does not design the full session.
It chooses the strategic teaching posture.

Your audited Topic Intelligence already has 9 teaching modes, reroute logic, confidence scaffolding, and evidence-aware mode shifts. This engine is the formal permanent version of that logic. 


What it owns
It owns:

strategy family selection,

fallback strategy selection,

strategy confidence,

strategy rationale,

strategy constraints,

strategy success criteria at a high level.

It does not own:

block order,

exact artifacts,

final timing schedule,

policy overrides.

Inputs
Required:

ic_hypotheses

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_topic_states

ic_concept_states

ic_misconception_instances

ic_interference_events

ic_mastery_gates

ic_risk_assessments

im_strategy_memory

im_intervention_outcomes

Helpful:

curriculum role of the topic

topic memory

recent session outcomes

current time horizon pressure

Outputs
Writes:

ic_teaching_decisions

Emits:

teaching.decision.emitted

Canonical strategy families
At minimum:

prerequisite_rollback

misconception_repair

contrast_repair

retrieval_reinforcement

worked_example_first

guided_to_independent

confidence_rebuild

transfer_training

pressure_conditioning

concept_compression

exam_rescue

mixed_context_stabilization

explanation_first

probe_first

These should be permanent strategy families, not ad hoc labels.

Internal submodules
A. Strategy Candidate Generator
Maps diagnostic patterns to eligible strategies.

Examples:

misconception dominant → misconception_repair, contrast_repair

retrieval weakness dominant → retrieval_reinforcement

pressure weakness dominant + learner not too fragile → pressure_conditioning

ambiguity high → probe_first

fragile confidence + repeated failure → confidence_rebuild

prerequisite weakness → prerequisite_rollback

B. Strategy Eligibility Filter
Removes strategies incompatible with:

learner fatigue,

confidence fragility,

active protection rules,

missing content support,

time horizon reality.

C. Strategy Fit Scorer
Scores each eligible strategy against:

dominant hypothesis fit,

learner state fit,

historical success for similar contexts,

mastery gap fit,

risk posture fit.

D. Strategy Memory Adjuster
Uses im_strategy_memory to raise or lower candidates based on past success.

E. Fallback Constructor
Builds backup strategy if primary underperforms or live evidence shifts.

Core scoring logic
Example structure:

strategy_fit = diagnosis_fit + learner_fit + memory_fit + risk_fit + proof_gap_fit - incompatibility_penalty

Where:

diagnosis_fit = how well it targets dominant cause

learner_fit = whether learner can absorb this mode now

memory_fit = prior success in similar contexts

risk_fit = whether it helps manage current risk

proof_gap_fit = whether it advances the missing mastery gate

incompatibility_penalty = fatigue, fragility, blocked pressure, etc.

Example mappings
Case 1: misconception + stable learner
Primary:

misconception_repair

Fallback:

contrast_repair

Case 2: ambiguity high between pressure weakness and retrieval weakness
Primary:

probe_first

Fallback:

retrieval_reinforcement_light

Case 3: fragile confidence + weak but recoverable knowledge
Primary:

confidence_rebuild

Fallback:

worked_example_first

Case 4: interference between neighboring concepts
Primary:

contrast_repair

Fallback:

concept_compression

Case 5: exam near, real foundation weakness, learner stable
Primary:

prerequisite_rollback with compression

Fallback:

exam_rescue if rollback infeasible

Failure modes
Strategy overfitting
Choosing one family too rigidly because it worked before.

Strategy glamour bias
Preferring sophisticated modes over the simplest effective one.

Memory dominance
Letting historical success outweigh fresh diagnosis.

State-blind strategy
Choosing a technically correct strategy the learner cannot absorb today.

Guardrails
no pressure_conditioning if active no_timing protection exists

no exam_rescue unless risk/time horizon justify it

no misconception_repair without structured misconception evidence or strong hypothesis support

no concept_compression when abstraction tolerance is too low unless heavily scaffolded

Success criterion
A good Teaching Strategy Engine makes the system choose the right kind of teaching, not merely “some teaching.”

7. Timing Engine
Mission
This engine decides when learning actions should happen and under what temporal conditions.

It governs:

review timing,

retest timing,

delayed recall timing,

pacing intensity,

pressure exposure timing,

and rest or spacing obligations.

Your audited Timing engine already manages phase progression, retests, golden windows, review timing, daily pacing, and frequency recommendations. This full engine formalizes that as a permanent temporal intelligence layer.

What it owns
It owns:

timing decisions,

review windows,

retest schedule,

delayed recall schedule,

pacing intensity,

readiness proposals for pressure exposure,

session density suggestions.

It does not own:

overall teaching family,

risk severity,

final release if learner fragility blocks timing choices.

Inputs
Required:

ic_mastery_gates

ic_topic_states

ic_concept_states

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_pressure_profiles

ic_recovery_profiles

ic_risk_assessments

im_topic_memory

ie_evidence_packets

ix_assessments / recent outcomes

Helpful:

calendar/exam horizon

assignment completion history

attendance rhythm

session duration capacity

Outputs
Writes:

ic_timing_decisions

Emits:

timing.decision.emitted

Internal timing domains
A. Review Timing
When a topic should reappear for reinforcement.

B. Retest Timing
When a repaired topic should be verified.

C. Delayed Recall Timing
When memory durability must be tested.

D. Pressure Timing
When timed conditions should be introduced, maintained, escalated, or removed.

E. Session Pacing
How dense, long, and demanding the next session should be.

F. Rest / Recovery Timing
When to avoid continued pressure or repeated proof attempts.

Internal submodules
A. Reinforcement Window Detector
Identifies optimal recall and reinforcement windows.

B. Retest Obligation Manager
Schedules verification after repair.

C. Pressure Readiness Evaluator
Judges whether pressure exposure is safe and useful.

D. Forgetting Curve Adapter
Adjusts timing based on actual delayed recall behavior, not generic spacing alone.

E. Session Density Planner
Determines how much cognitive load the learner can sustain in the next block/day.

F. Timing Conflict Resolver
Handles clashes such as:

retest due, but learner fatigued,

review due, but critical rescue topic emerging.

Core logic examples
Review timing
review_urgency = fragility_score + forgetting_risk + exam_leverage - current_overload_penalty

Retest timing
If repair happened recently and was strong:

retest after short delay for fragile topics

longer delay for more secure topics

Pressure readiness
Pressure should require:

acceptable untimed baseline,

non-collapsed confidence state,

no strong no_timing protection,

enough recovery capacity.

Session pacing
Use:

fatigue state,

recent intensity,

recovery profile,

risk urgency,

session objective.

Temporal laws
no pressure exposure without readiness evidence

no repeated heavy retest while fatigue high

no over-review of stable topics while unresolved critical weaknesses remain

no delayed recall omission after meaningful repair

no dense mixed-context session after confidence collapse unless explicitly therapeutic and small

Failure modes
Calendar intelligence without cognitive intelligence
Good schedule, bad learner fit.

Overtriggered retesting
Turning the system into a constant verifier.

Underusing delayed recall
Mistaking immediate repair for stability.

Pressure misuse
Introducing timing when the learner is not ready and mistaking collapse for ignorance.

Guardrails
timing decisions must reference learner state freshness

pressure timing must check protection rules

retest timing must respect recovery state

high-urgency timing can still be downgraded by governance

Success criterion
A good Timing Engine makes learning land at the right time, not just in the right order.

8. Risk Engine
Mission
This engine estimates academic danger and learning instability across scopes:

concept,

topic,

subject,

plan,

learner,

exam horizon.

Your audited Risk engine already includes a multi-class taxonomy, compound risk patterns, velocity tracking, early warnings, and persisted assessments. This full engine keeps that spirit and expands it into a complete risk operating system. 


What it owns
It owns:

risk class,

severity,

velocity,

drivers,

early warnings,

risk source decomposition,

protective implications.

It does not own:

session design,

final arbitration,

content artifact choice.

Inputs
Required:

ic_topic_states

ic_concept_states

ic_hypotheses

ic_misconception_instances

ic_interference_events

ic_mastery_verdicts

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_timing_decisions

ic_sequence_plans

im_forecasts

curriculum/exam relevance

Helpful:

attendance reliability

assignment completion

recent adaptation failures

strategy memory

Outputs
Writes:

ic_risk_assessments

ic_risk_drivers

ic_early_warnings

Emits:

risk.assessed

warning.emitted

Canonical risk classes
At minimum:

topic_collapse_risk

false_readiness_risk

forgetting_risk

pressure_failure_risk

interference_risk

misconception_persistence_risk

overload_risk

confidence_collapse_risk

stagnation_risk

sequence_risk

review_debt_risk

exam_exposure_risk

subject_systemic_risk

adaptation_failure_risk

Internal submodules
A. Local Risk Assessor
Evaluates concept/topic-level risk.

B. Compound Risk Builder
Combines multiple risks into bigger exposure patterns.

C. Velocity Tracker
Measures whether risk is rising, falling, or stable.

D. Early Warning Generator
Produces actionable warnings ahead of failure.

E. Driver Decomposer
Explains exactly what is causing the risk.

F. Protective Implication Mapper
Translates risk into suggested or required protection moves.

Core risk logic
Example topic collapse risk
Depends on:

fragility,

unresolved misconception,

weak proof gates,

repeated failed intervention,

high exam leverage.

Example false readiness risk
Depends on:

strong recent accuracy,

weak transfer or delayed recall,

pressure untested,

explanation gate missing.

Example overload risk
Depends on:

fatigue high,

session density high,

many simultaneous obligations,

confidence fragile,

mixed-context demand too high.

Example sequence risk
Depends on:

prerequisite instability,

adjacency to known interference pair,

timing mismatch,

pressure schedule conflict.

Risk velocity
Risk is not only state; it is direction.

Example:
velocity = current_risk_score - trailing_average_risk_score

Fast-rising medium risk may deserve more attention than flat high risk already being handled.

Risk source decomposition
Every serious risk must explain:

what is driving it,

how much each driver matters,

whether drivers are local or systemic.

This is critical for useful intervention.

Failure modes
Monolithic risk
Collapsing very different risks into one score.

Warning fatigue
Emitting too many weak warnings.

Late warning
Only recognizing danger when failure already happened.

Missing learner-mediated risk
Treating fatigue/confidence collapse as secondary UX issues instead of academic risk.

Guardrails
all medium+ risk assessments must store drivers

critical risk must produce protective implications

low confidence risk estimates should be marked as such, not hidden

contradiction with forecast or mastery must trigger governance review

Success criterion
A good Risk Engine makes the coach preemptive, not reactive.

9. Adaptation Engine
Mission
This engine decides when the system should change course.

It answers:

should we keep going,

should we refine,

should we reopen diagnosis,

should we reroute,

should we compress,

should we stabilize,

should we protect,

should we escalate?

Your audited Adaptation engine already has triggers, velocity logic, systemic pattern detection, learner adaptive profiles, dwell times, and stop lists. This full engine turns that into the formal strategy-revision organ of the coach. 


What it owns
It owns:

adaptation events,

adaptation mode,

trigger records,

urgency,

old-vs-new strategy/plan relation,

dwell logic,

stop/continue verdicts.

It does not own:

full diagnosis by itself,

final arbitration against governance,

direct session blocks.

Inputs
Required:

ic_hypotheses

ic_topic_states

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_risk_assessments

ic_teaching_decisions

ic_timing_decisions

im_intervention_outcomes

im_strategy_memory

im_coach_evaluations

im_topic_memory

recent sequence plans

Helpful:

contradictions

confidence gate hits

policy hits

forecast drift

Outputs
Writes:

ic_adaptation_events

Emits:

adaptation.event.emitted

Canonical adaptation modes
reopen_diagnosis

reroute

refine

compress

protect

stabilize

escalate

rollback

hold_course

Internal submodules
A. Trigger Detector
Looks for conditions such as:

repeated failed intervention,

stagnation,

rising risk,

state deterioration,

contradiction,

false mastery,

systemic cross-topic pattern.

B. Dwell Window Manager
Prevents thrashing by tracking how recently major changes were made.

C. Adaptation Mode Selector
Chooses which kind of change is appropriate.

D. Strategy Continuity Checker
Ensures adaptation links old and new plans coherently.

E. Stop List Manager
Marks families or behaviors that should not be repeated under current context.

F. Systemic Pattern Scanner
Looks across multiple topics/subjects for repeating failure forms.

Core adaptation logic examples
Reopen diagnosis
Use when:

interventions fail repeatedly,

ambiguity stays high,

contradiction emerges,

new evidence strongly weakens prior cause.

Refine
Use when:

strategy seems directionally right,

but pacing, representation, or block design is off.

Reroute
Use when:

current family is wrong,

another strategy clearly fits better,

risk is rising under current route.

Compress
Use when:

exam horizon tight,

learner stable enough,

wider route infeasible.

Protect
Use when:

confidence/fatigue/overload risk rising,

aggressive moves would worsen learning.

Stabilize
Use when:

repair happened,

now the goal is consolidation, not expansion.

Dwell logic
No intelligent system should swing wildly.

Example:

do not reroute twice in one short proof window without materially new evidence

do not reopen diagnosis repeatedly for same topic unless new contradiction or failed repair arises

allow dwell override only for critical risk or policy-triggered conflict

Failure modes
Over-adaptation
Changing too fast and never letting a plan mature.

Under-adaptation
Staying loyal to a failing route too long.

Local adaptation blindness
Fixing one topic while missing a wider recurring pattern.

Cosmetic adaptation
Renaming the plan without meaningfully changing the mechanism.

Guardrails
every adaptation must record trigger reason

every reroute must reference what it replaced

dwell override must be explicit

repeated failed adaptations should escalate to deeper diagnostic review

Success criterion
A good Adaptation Engine makes the coach self-correcting without becoming unstable.

10. Content Selection Engine
Mission
This engine chooses the best content artifact for the current educational job.

It answers:

which note,

which example,

which drill,

which question set,

which representation,

which contrast item,

which retest object,

or whether content must be generated because nothing suitable exists.

Without this engine, even a smart diagnosis and strategy can fail because the wrong material is delivered.

What it owns
It owns:

content fitness scoring,

content artifact selection,

insufficiency detection,

content request generation when gaps exist.

It does not own:

high-level strategy,

session block order,

ontology of knowledge itself.

Inputs
Required:

ic_teaching_decisions

ic_session_plans

ic_session_blocks

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_topic_states

ic_misconception_instances

ic_interference_events

content library metadata

j_concepts

j_representations

Helpful:

past artifact effectiveness

learner preference profile

mastery gap targets

pressure mode requirements

Outputs
Writes:

content_selection_logs

content_requests when insufficient

selected artifact references for session blocks

Emits:

content.selected

Content fitness dimensions
Each artifact should be scored on dimensions like:

topic fit

concept fit

misconception repair fit

interference repair fit

learner mode fit

representation fit

difficulty fit

timing fit

proof-gap fit

freshness/quality score

Internal submodules
A. Artifact Candidate Finder
Pulls all plausible assets.

B. Fitness Scorer
Scores candidates against current need.

C. Representation Matcher
Chooses proper form:

visual,

symbolic,

verbal,

comparative,

worked example,

drill,

mixed-context set,

timed set.

D. Insufficiency Detector
Flags when no artifact crosses threshold.

E. Content Request Builder
Creates a demand object for generation or ingestion when content is lacking.

Core logic examples
Misconception repair content
Prefer:

explicit wrong-vs-right contrast,

explanation-rich worked examples,

boundary-case items,

misconception-targeted drills.

Pressure diagnosis content
Prefer:

matched untimed/timed equivalents,

short calibrated item sets,

low ambiguity scoring items.

Interference repair content
Prefer:

compare-and-contrast sets,

classification items,

paired examples,

isolation blocks before mixed blocks.

Confidence rebuild content
Prefer:

medium-confidence wins,

scaffolded examples,

low humiliation risk,

visible progress items.

Transfer strengthening content
Prefer:

representation shift,

context shift,

application questions,

non-template items.

Failure modes
Topic-only matching
Selecting any topic-relevant item without regard to the actual need.

Representation mismatch
Giving symbolic content to a learner who currently needs visual anchoring.

Quality blindness
Using low-quality or ambiguous content because it matches tags.

Library tunnel vision
Forcing existing content when better generated content is needed.

Guardrails
every selected artifact must log fitness rationale

insufficient content must create a first-class request, not silent compromise

representation should match both concept need and learner state

high-stakes proof or risk sessions should require high-quality artifacts only

Success criterion
A good Content Selection Engine ensures the coach uses the right material for the right reason at the right time.

“This is when and under what temporal conditions it should happen.”

says:
“These are the dangers if we proceed badly or delay wrongly.”

says:
“This route should hold, refine, reopen, reroute, or protect.”

“These are the actual artifacts fit for the job.”

says:
“This is the cognitive path the learner will experience next.”

That is the real coaching brain loop.
