11. Mastery Proof Engine
Mission
This engine decides whether the learner has earned the right to advance, consolidate, or be treated as truly secure on a concept or topic.

Its job is not to ask, “Did they get some items right?”
Its job is to ask:

can they explain?

can they solve independently?

can they transfer?

can they survive mixed context?

can they survive light pressure?

can they remember later?

have misconception and interference liabilities really cleared?

Your audited Topic Intelligence already contains a 6-checkpoint proof contract with explain, independent_solve, transfer, delayed_recall, mixed_context, and light_pressure, plus evidence-based capping so proof cannot exceed what question data supports. 


This engine turns that into a permanent standalone proof authority.

What it owns
It owns:

mastery contracts,

mastery gates,

proof progress,

proof capping,

final proof-oriented readiness verdicts.

It does not own:

teaching strategy,

topic sequencing,

content selection,

learner-state diagnosis.

Inputs
Required:

ic_concept_states

ic_topic_states

ie_evidence_packets

ic_misconception_instances

ic_interference_events

ic_diagnostic_probe_results

ic_learner_state_snapshots

recent assessment outcomes

recent intervention outcomes

Helpful:

exam blueprint significance

memory of prior false mastery

strategy memory

topic memory

Outputs
Writes:

ic_mastery_contracts

ic_mastery_gates

ic_mastery_verdicts

Emits:

mastery.contract.created

mastery.gate.updated

mastery.verdict.emitted

Canonical gate families
At minimum:

explain

independent_solve

transfer

mixed_context

delayed_recall

light_pressure

misconception_cleared

interference_cleared

Not every topic must require all gates equally, but no serious topic should reach mastery_proven through shallow correctness alone.

Internal submodules
A. Contract Builder
Creates the proof contract for a concept or topic based on:

topic type,

exam leverage,

abstraction level,

learner state,

known risk profile.

B. Gate Requirement Resolver
Determines which gates are required versus optional.

Example:

factual definition topic may need lighter transfer burden

procedural/high-exam topic may require stronger independent solve + pressure gate

C. Evidence Aggregator
Pulls proof-relevant evidence from:

direct responses,

explanations,

timed sets,

delayed recall checks,

mixed-context sets,

intervention outcomes

D. Gate Evaluator
Marks each gate as:

unmet

partially met

met

met but fragile

E. Proof Cap Engine
Prevents apparent readiness from outrunning real evidence.

This is vital and already present in spirit in your audited topic engine. 


F. False Mastery Reconciler
Checks whether any false-mastery flags should block proof advancement.

Core proof logic
A simple frame:

gate_score = direct_evidence + diversity_bonus + consistency_bonus - fragility_penalty - contradiction_penalty

Then:

proof_progress = weighted_sum(required_gate_scores)

Then apply capping:

final_proof_progress = min(proof_progress, highest_supported_evidence_band)

This avoids a common failure mode: profile averages saying “ready” while evidence diversity says “not proven.”

Example gate logic
Explain gate
Satisfied only if:

learner can articulate correct reasoning,

explanation is not purely memorized phrasing,

explanation survives light variation.

Independent solve gate
Satisfied only if:

learner solves without heavy scaffolds,

not merely after repeated hints or step prompting.

Transfer gate
Satisfied only if:

learner succeeds when representation or context shifts.

Mixed-context gate
Satisfied only if:

learner still performs when adjacent concepts are blended or competing.

Delayed recall gate
Satisfied only if:

evidence appears after temporal gap, not immediately after teaching.

Light-pressure gate
Satisfied only if:

learner maintains acceptable performance under modest timing constraints,

assuming pressure is actually appropriate for this learner.

Canonical verdicts
not_ready

early_progress

partially_proven

fragile_ready

provisionally_ready

mastery_proven

mastery_suspect

Failure modes
Accuracy masquerade
Equating high correctness with mastery.

Recency illusion
Mistaking “just taught” for “learned.”

Single-format proof
Treating one representation as enough.

Pressure blindness
Promoting a learner who collapses once time enters.

Structural-clearing failure
Advancing despite unresolved misconception or interference.

Guardrails
no mastery_proven without contradiction check

no pressure gate if learner-state/protection rules say pressure is unsafe

no proof gate satisfied from stale evidence alone

unresolved misconception/interference should cap verdict confidence

Success criterion
A good Mastery Proof Engine sharply reduces false readiness and makes advancement trustworthy.

12. Sequencing Engine
Mission
This engine decides the best order of topic experience for a learner.

It does not merely sort topics by weakness. It designs a path that respects:

prerequisites,

learner confidence,

interference,

exam pressure,

current fragility,

and opportunity cost.

Your audited Sequencing Intelligence already chooses among 7 models, compares alternatives, defines pivot conditions, detects interference separations, and monitors sequence stability. 


This engine formalizes that as a permanent strategic planner.

What it owns
It owns:

sequence plans,

ordering logic,

bridge insertion,

separation rules,

pivot conditions,

stability monitoring.

It does not own:

proof certification,

final timing schedule,

session design,

policy override.

Inputs
Required:

ic_topic_states

ic_concept_states

ic_risk_assessments

j_prerequisite_edges

j_interference_edges

ic_learner_state_snapshots

im_topic_memory

im_forecasts

current exam horizon

coverage gap data

Helpful:

strategy memory

session success patterns

subject-level time budget

current confidence profile

Outputs
Writes:

ic_sequence_plans

ic_sequence_items

Emits:

sequence.plan.created

Canonical sequencing models
At minimum:

dependency_first

confidence_first

rescue_first

exam_weight_first

fragility_repair_first

anti_interference_first

mixed_portfolio_blend

Your audited system already uses model comparison logic; this makes those models permanent and explicit. 


Internal submodules
A. Candidate Path Generator
Builds multiple plausible ordering candidates.

B. Dependency Integrity Checker
Ensures prerequisites are respected or deliberately bridged.

C. Confidence Path Planner
Designs sequences that protect or restore morale when needed.

D. Interference Separation Planner
Adds spacing or anti-adjacency between collision-prone topics.

E. Exam Leverage Planner
Raises urgent high-yield material where appropriate.

F. Sequence Stability Monitor
Detects harmful churn and sequence instability.

G. Pivot Condition Builder
Defines when the learner should remain, advance, revisit, or rollback.

Core scoring logic
A good sequence score should combine:

sequence_score = prerequisite_integrity + risk_reduction + confidence_fit + exam_leverage + interference_safety + time_budget_fit - churn_penalty

Where:

prerequisite_integrity = structural coherence

risk_reduction = how much danger this order reduces

confidence_fit = whether it preserves learner stability

exam_leverage = whether it improves practical exam readiness

interference_safety = anti-collision benefit

time_budget_fit = feasible within time horizon

churn_penalty = instability from over-reordering

Sequence item structure
Each sequence item should carry:

topic id

reason for placement

required entry condition

desired exit condition

adjacent separation rules

rollback trigger

bridge dependencies if any

Example sequence patterns
Case 1: prerequisite crisis
Order:

weak prerequisite bridge

core blocked topic

nearby dependent topic

Case 2: fragile learner, many weak topics
Order:

recoverable win topic

bridge topic

deeper weak topic

another stabilizing win

Case 3: interference pair active
Order:

isolate concept A

space

isolate concept B

contrast session

mixed verification

Case 4: exam near, broad medium weakness
Order:

high-leverage topics

fragile high-value topics

review debt cleanup

low-yield deferrables later

Pivot conditions
The engine must define explicit sequence pivots such as:

advance only if proof progress crosses threshold

rollback if prerequisite weakness persists

pause if overload risk rises

branch to contrast repair if interference emerges

compress if exam horizon tightens

Failure modes
Weakness sorting masquerading as sequencing
Just ranking topics by low score.

Dependency tyranny
Over-prioritizing structure while learner morale collapses.

Confidence vanity
Protecting confidence so much that major exam risk is ignored.

Churn
Reordering constantly whenever one signal changes.

Anti-interference failure
Placing collision-prone topics too close together.

Guardrails
no sequence item should violate hard prerequisite or protection law without explicit governance override

sequence plans must carry stability metadata

churn thresholds should be enforced

unresolved false mastery should reduce advancement confidence

Success criterion
A good Sequencing Engine creates an order that feels both educationally intelligent and strategically humane.

13. Diagnostic Experiment Engine
Mission
This engine is the formal mechanism for testing hypotheses instead of pretending the system is certain when it is not.

It exists to answer:

which probe will most reduce uncertainty,

what exactly should be compared,

how much information gain is expected,

and what inference should be updated from the result.

This is a crucial engine because without it, the coach can become overconfident.

What it owns
It owns:

probe selection,

probe design logic,

expected information gain,

probe-to-ambiguity mapping,

probe result interpretation.

It does not own:

long-term strategy family,

session release by itself,

topic sequencing.

Inputs
Required:

ic_hypotheses

ic_ambiguity_records

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_topic_states

ic_concept_states

ic_misconception_instances

ic_interference_events

recent evidence history

Helpful:

strategy memory

prior probe results

current protection rules

content availability

Outputs
Writes:

ic_diagnostic_probes

ic_diagnostic_probe_results

Emits:

probe.created

probe.completed

Canonical probe families
At minimum:

timed_vs_untimed

isolated_vs_mixed

explain_vs_solve

recognition_vs_recall

guided_vs_independent

verbal_vs_symbolic

visual_vs_symbolic

immediate_vs_delayed

concept_A_vs_concept_B discrimination

Internal submodules
A. Ambiguity Targeter
Determines exactly what uncertainty is unresolved.

B. Probe Library Matcher
Finds candidate probe families suited to that ambiguity.

C. Information Gain Scorer
Estimates which probe will most reduce uncertainty per minute/cost.

D. Probe Safety Filter
Removes probes that are unsafe or misleading under current learner state.

E. Result Interpreter
Maps probe outcomes back into hypothesis/posterior updates.

Core logic
Example:

If top ambiguity is between:

retrieval_weakness

pressure_performance

Then candidate probes may be:

timed_vs_untimed

immediate_vs_delayed

explain_vs_solve

Expected information gain should score higher for probes that:

cleanly differentiate candidate causes,

are short,

are safe,

use existing good content,

have high interpretation clarity.

Example mappings
Ambiguity: misconception vs retrieval weakness
Best probes:

explain_vs_solve

classification of correct/incorrect rules

error explanation

Ambiguity: pressure weakness vs knowledge weakness
Best probes:

matched untimed/timed pair

short calibrated timed set

latency/error shape comparison

Ambiguity: interference vs isolated weakness
Best probes:

isolated concept A

isolated concept B

mixed A/B discrimination set

Ambiguity: representation mismatch
Best probes:

verbal item vs symbolic equivalent

visual equivalent vs numerical application

Probe result doctrine
A probe result must:

update hypothesis support,

potentially create or downgrade misconception/interference records,

potentially alter learner state,

potentially trigger adaptation.

It should not simply store pass/fail and die there.

Failure modes
Busywork probes
Running tests that do not actually reduce ambiguity.

Unsafe probing
Adding pressure to a fragile learner just to get clarity.

Low-interpretability probes
Results are noisy and do not help posterior updates.

Probe addiction
Endlessly probing instead of teaching.

Guardrails
each probe must target a named ambiguity

each probe must have expected information gain

probe count should be bounded by urgency and learner condition

if uncertainty is already low, prefer action over more probing

Success criterion
A good Diagnostic Experiment Engine makes the coach scientifically disciplined rather than diagnostically theatrical.

14. Protection Rule Engine
Mission
This engine defines what the system must not do under current conditions.

It is one of the most important safety mechanisms in the whole architecture.

It answers:

what actions are dangerous now,

what should be deferred,

what should be blocked,

what must be downgraded,

and what the coach must protect before attempting again.

Your audited Adaptation engine already has a stop list and Timing/Risk already emit pressure- and fragility-relevant signals. 

 


This engine formalizes those negative constraints into first-class protective law.

What it owns
It owns:

active protection rules,

rule severity,

rule duration,

rule hardness,

rule expiration or clearing conditions.

It does not own:

positive teaching strategy,

full session composition,

arbitration of all conflicts.

Inputs
Required:

ic_learner_state_snapshots

ic_pressure_profiles

ic_risk_assessments

ic_mastery_verdicts

ic_false_mastery_flags

ic_interference_events

ic_misconception_instances

ic_adaptation_events

recent session outcomes

Helpful:

timing decisions

forecast divergence

contradiction events

strategy memory

Outputs
Writes:

ic_protection_rules

Emits:

protection.rule.created

Canonical protection rule families
At minimum:

no_timing_pressure

no_topic_mix

no_new_content

no_sequence_advance

no_retest_today

no_overreview

no_high_density_session

no_exam_rescue_mode

no_mixed_context_yet

stabilize_before_advance

Internal submodules
A. Risk-to-Rule Mapper
Converts risk states into protection implications.

B. Learner-Fragility Filter
Creates rules from fatigue, confidence collapse, pressure vulnerability, and recovery fragility.

C. False-Mastery Blocker
Blocks advancement or proof overclaim when evidence is insufficient.

D. Interference Protection Builder
Adds separation/mixing restrictions when concept collisions are active.

E. Duration Resolver
Decides whether the rule lasts:

one session,

one day,

until next probe,

until next proof update,

until contradiction resolved.

Core examples
High fatigue + rising overload risk
Create:

no_high_density_session

no_retest_today

Active pressure vulnerability
Create:

no_timing_pressure

Unresolved interference pair
Create:

no_topic_mix

stabilize_before_advance

False mastery flag
Create:

no_sequence_advance

Repeated failed strategy family
May create:

no_exam_rescue_mode
or another family-specific constraint where appropriate

Rule hardness
Each rule should have:

advisory

preferred

required

blocking

Blocking rules must be respected by Session Composer, Timing, Sequencing, and Assessment Construction unless governance explicitly overrides.

Failure modes
Protection inflation
Too many rules, system becomes timid.

Protection neglect
Rules exist but downstream engines quietly ignore them.

Wrong duration
Rules remain too long or expire too soon.

Indirect contradiction
Rule says no timing, but assessment engine still slips timing in.

Guardrails
every blocking rule must have explicit cause

every rule must have clear expiry or review condition

downstream plans must validate against active rules before release

protection should constrain action, not silently replace diagnosis

Success criterion
A good Protection Rule Engine stops the coach from harming progress while still allowing intelligent movement.

15. Governance Cluster
This is the cluster that keeps the whole system honest.

Without it, a large intelligence engine can become:

overconfident,

contradictory,

quietly unsafe,

or impossible to audit.

The governance cluster contains:

Confidence Gate Engine

Contradiction Check Engine

Decision Arbitration Engine

Policy Guardrail Engine

Audit Trace Engine

15A. Confidence Gate Engine
Mission
This engine decides whether the evidence quality is strong enough for the hardness of the action being proposed.

It owns
action downgrading,

probe requirements,

confidence threshold enforcement.

It does not own
diagnosis generation,

strategy generation.

Inputs
hypotheses

evidence packet strength/freshness

learner state uncertainty

mastery proof state

proposed action hardness

Outputs
gate pass/fail

downgrade actions

required_next_action = probe / gather_more_evidence / proceed_softly

Core law
Strong action requires strong evidence.

Examples:

mastery_proven requires high sufficiency

pressure exposure required requires real pressure evidence or safe inferential substitute

reroute requires enough contradiction with current plan

Failure modes
thresholds too loose → overconfidence

thresholds too strict → paralysis

Success criterion
The system becomes appropriately humble under uncertainty.

15B. Contradiction Check Engine
Mission
This engine detects when two outputs cannot both be trusted at the same time.

It owns
contradiction detection,

contradiction classification,

contradiction severity,

reopen recommendation.

Inputs
mastery verdicts

topic states

learner states

risk assessments

forecasts

adaptation events

session outcomes

Outputs
ig_contradictions

Canonical contradiction classes
mastery_vs_transfer

readiness_vs_pressure_collapse

low_risk_vs_bad_forecast

adaptation_says_improving_vs_velocity_flat

stable_topic_vs_recurring_same_misconception

learner_fatigue_high_vs_high_density_session

no_timing_rule_vs_timed_assessment

Core doctrine
Contradictions are not noise. They are evidence that the system’s beliefs need repair.

Success criterion
The system notices when its own story stops making sense.

15C. Decision Arbitration Engine
Mission
This engine resolves conflicts between engines when they disagree materially.

It owns
final conflict resolution,

winning engine/output,

rationale for override or compromise.

Inputs
contradiction events

candidate engine outputs

precedence rules

policy hits

confidence gate state

active protection rules

Outputs
ig_arbitration_decisions

Common arbitration conflicts
Sequencing vs Risk
Sequence wants advance; Risk says prerequisite collapse likely.

Timing vs Learner State
Timing wants pressure; learner state says vulnerable.

Teaching Strategy vs Strategy Memory
Fresh diagnosis wants one route; long-run history says that route often fails here.

Session Composer vs Protection Rule
Session plan includes mixed context; protection forbids it.

Arbitration doctrine
Arbitration must choose among:

engine A wins

engine B wins

blended compromise

reopen diagnosis

downgrade action

Success criterion
Disagreement becomes structured resolution, not silent chaos.

15D. Policy Guardrail Engine
Mission
This engine enforces constitutional rules that ordinary engines may not violate.

It owns
policy validation,

hard invalidation,

constitutional blocks.

Inputs
all major decisions

mastery contracts

protection rules

session plans

assessments

release requests

Outputs
ig_policy_hits

Canonical guardrail laws
At minimum:

no mastery_proven without proof contract support

no pressure exposure when hard no_timing rule active

no advance on blocked prerequisite without formal override

no strong diagnosis release under insufficient confidence

no high-density session when severe overload protection active

no session release without trace for major decisions

Success criterion
The system cannot “cheat” its own architecture.

15E. Audit Trace Engine
Mission
This engine creates the reason chain that makes the system inspectable.

It owns
decision traces,

trace completeness,

evidence lineage references,

engine contribution ordering.

Your audited architecture already has meaningful cross-engine writes that shape planner behavior — Topic → Coverage, Topic → Timing, Topic → Risk/Adaptation, Risk → Study Planner, Adaptation → Study Planner, Timing → Study Planner/Delayed Recall. 


Once a system has that much cross-engine power, audit trace is no longer optional.

Inputs
all major output records

evidence lineage

arbitration decisions

policy hits

confidence gate hits

contradiction events

Outputs
ig_decision_traces

ig_decision_trace_steps

Trace doctrine
For major decisions, the trace should show:

source evidence ids

key intermediate engine outputs

challenger engines if any

final winning rationale

action hardness

release status

Success criterion
Any serious coaching decision can be reconstructed later.

That is a real cognitive operating system.

The complete coaching loop now
Evidence arrives

That is the full inner brain.

Thought for 15s
Good. The next layer is the shared computational substrate that all the engines sit on.

Without this layer, you can have many good engines and still end up with:

incompatible scores,

hidden confidence assumptions,

one engine treating stale evidence like fresh evidence,

one engine calling something “high risk” while another calls the same pattern “moderate,”

and orchestration that feels intelligent in isolation but incoherent as a whole.

Your audited nucleus already hints at this substrate: the current system has an evidence spine, proof checkpoints, persisted timing decisions, persisted risk assessments, and adaptation logs feeding planning behavior. 

 

 


Now we formalize the underlying shared grammar.

Shared Scoring and Interaction Substrate
1. Mission of the substrate
This layer defines the common language for all engines to answer questions like:

how strong is this evidence?

how fresh is it?

how confident are we?

how severe is this problem?

how urgent is action?

how ambiguous is diagnosis?

when should one engine trust another engine’s output?

how should multiple engine outputs combine?

This layer is not an “extra utility package.”
It is the mathematical and orchestration constitution of the coach brain.

2. Core design laws
Law 1: every serious engine output must be uncertainty-aware
No engine should emit naked truth like:

“topic weak”

“learner fatigued”

“use pressure”

“mastery proven”

Instead, it must emit:

value,

confidence,

evidence support count,

freshness,

provenance,

and often severity or urgency.

Law 2: evidence, confidence, severity, urgency, and ambiguity are different things
They must never be collapsed into one number.

For example:

a diagnosis can be high severity but low confidence,

a problem can be low severity but high urgency,

a topic can be high confidence weak but low exam urgency,

a hypothesis can be high ambiguity even if one explanation is currently leading.

Law 3: every score must declare scope
A score must say what it applies to:

concept,

topic,

learner,

session,

subject,

exam horizon,

or plan.

Law 4: every score must declare time relevance
Freshness matters. A session from yesterday is not equal to a pattern seen over 6 weeks.

Law 5: engine outputs must be composable
If Risk, Learner State, and Timing all speak different numerical dialects, the system will become brittle.

3. Canonical score families
I would standardize the entire system around 7 score families.

3.1 Evidence strength
How much a piece of evidence should count.

Range:
0.0 - 1.0

Interpretation:

0.0–0.2 weak

0.2–0.4 light

0.4–0.6 moderate

0.6–0.8 strong

0.8–1.0 decisive

Used by:

hypothesis support,

proof gates,

misconception confidence,

interference confidence,

learner-state inference.

3.2 Confidence
How sure the engine is about its own conclusion.

Range:
0.0 - 1.0

Interpretation:

0.0–0.2 very low

0.2–0.4 low

0.4–0.6 moderate

0.6–0.8 high

0.8–1.0 very high

Used by:

hypothesis outputs,

learner-state snapshots,

risk assessments,

strategy decisions,

mastery verdicts.

3.3 Severity
How bad the problem is if it is real.

Range:
0.0 - 1.0

Used by:

risk assessments,

misconception instances,

interference events,

contradictions,

protection rules.

3.4 Urgency
How quickly the system should respond.

Range:
0.0 - 1.0

Example:

moderate issue with exam tomorrow = high urgency

high issue on low-leverage future topic = moderate urgency

Used by:

timing,

risk,

adaptation,

arbitration,

session planning.

3.5 Ambiguity
How unresolved the explanatory field is.

Range:
0.0 - 1.0

Used by:

hypothesis competition,

diagnostic experiment engine,

confidence gate engine,

teaching strategy hardness downgrade.

3.6 Fragility
How unstable a topic, learner state, or success pattern is.

Range:
0.0 - 1.0

Used by:

mastery proof,

timing,

risk,

adaptation,

sequencing.

3.7 Readiness
How prepared a learner is for a next action.

Range:
0.0 - 1.0

Used by:

timing pressure readiness,

topic advancement,

mixed-context exposure,

proof escalation,

sequence exit conditions.

4. Canonical shared wrappers
Every engine should emit a shared structural envelope.

4.1 Scored value wrapper
struct ScoredValue<T> {
    value: T,
    confidence: f32,
    evidence_strength: f32,
    freshness: f32,
    scope_type: ScopeType,
    scope_id: String,
    computed_at: DateTime<Utc>,
    provenance_refs: Vec<String>,
}
This is the base wrapper.

4.2 Risk-style wrapper
struct RiskValue {
    class: RiskClass,
    severity: f32,
    urgency: f32,
    confidence: f32,
    velocity: f32,
    driver_refs: Vec<String>,
}
4.3 Hypothesis-style wrapper
struct HypothesisValue {
    family: HypothesisFamily,
    posterior: f32,
    confidence: f32,
    ambiguity_contribution: f32,
    support_refs: Vec<String>,
    counterevidence_refs: Vec<String>,
}
4.4 Gate-style wrapper
struct GateResult {
    gate_type: MasteryGateType,
    met: bool,
    observed_score: f32,
    threshold: f32,
    confidence: f32,
    freshness: f32,
    blocking_issue_refs: Vec<String>,
}
4.5 Decision-style wrapper
struct DecisionValue<T> {
    value: T,
    hardness: DecisionHardness,
    confidence: f32,
    rationale_refs: Vec<String>,
    blocker_refs: Vec<String>,
    challenger_refs: Vec<String>,
}
This becomes the standard payload style across the system.

5. Shared evidence weighting framework
This is one of the most important parts.

Your audited nucleus already has an evidence spine and proof-aware logic. 


Now we define how evidence should be weighted everywhere.

5.1 Evidence weight formula
A canonical formula:

final_weight = source_weight * freshness_weight * relevance_weight * diversity_weight * reliability_weight * context_weight

Where:

source_weight
Base trust by source type:

direct answer event

explanation artifact

timed assessment

delayed recall test

parent note

teacher note

imported exam result

freshness_weight
Recent evidence matters more, but not infinitely more.

relevance_weight
How directly the evidence touches the current question.

Example:

delayed recall evidence is highly relevant to delayed recall gate,

less relevant to representation mismatch.

diversity_weight
Evidence gets extra weight when it comes from multiple formats or conditions.

Example:

correct in verbal + symbolic + applied context is stronger than three verbal-only successes.

reliability_weight
Adjust for known noise:

guessable item,

low-quality content,

ambiguous prompt,

over-scaffolded solve,

high hint count.

context_weight
Context matters:

learner severely fatigued may reduce interpretation certainty,

pressure condition evidence only relevant to pressure-related claims,

mixed-context evidence matters more for interference or transfer claims.

5.2 Evidence source baselines
You should define canonical source baselines like:

direct independent solve: high

heavy-hint solve: low to moderate

self-confidence report: light

teacher observation: moderate

delayed recall success: high

timed micro-check: moderate to high

repeated same distractor pattern: strong for misconception support

open-ended explanation: strong when quality assessed well

5.3 Evidence diversity bonus
The system should reward diversity of proof.

Example:
if support comes from:

explanation,

independent solve,

delayed recall,

mixed context,

that should weigh more than 4 similar multiple-choice successes.

A possible form:

diversity_bonus = log(1 + distinct_evidence_modes_count) / log(max_modes + 1)

5.4 Evidence contradiction penalty
If strong counterevidence exists, the system must reduce certainty.

Example:

strong transfer success should weaken transfer weakness hypothesis,

recent pressure success should weaken pressure collapse claim,

cleared misconception evidence should weaken active misconception severity.

6. Shared confidence framework
Confidence is not the same as the score value.

A topic can have low readiness but high confidence in that low readiness.

6.1 Confidence components
I would compute engine confidence from 5 ingredients:

confidence = evidence_volume_factor * evidence_consistency_factor * evidence_diversity_factor * freshness_factor * model_alignment_factor

Where:

evidence_volume_factor
How much evidence exists.

evidence_consistency_factor
Whether the evidence agrees or fights itself.

evidence_diversity_factor
Whether evidence comes from multiple forms and conditions.

freshness_factor
How recent the evidence is.

model_alignment_factor
Whether multiple engines support the same conclusion.

Example:

hypothesis engine, misconception engine, and intervention outcome all align.

6.2 Confidence downgrade rules
Confidence should be downgraded when:

evidence mostly comes from one format,

evidence is stale,

contradiction exists,

high ambiguity remains,

learner state is unusually noisy,

content quality is poor,

sample size is too small.

6.3 Confidence propagation law
Downstream engines must not treat low-confidence upstream outputs as hard truth.

Example:

low-confidence diagnosis should make Teaching Strategy emit only preferred/advisory decisions,

not hard required moves.

7. Freshness and staleness model
Every engine needs a shared temporal truth model.

7.1 Freshness classes
live

recent

aging

stale

expired

7.2 Freshness decay
Different signals decay at different rates.

Fast decay
current fatigue state

live engagement state

immediate frustration markers

Medium decay
pressure response profile

topic fragility

misconception repair confidence

Slow decay
durable learning preferences

long-term strategy memory

stable prerequisite graph truth

7.3 Decay function
Use per-signal decay functions rather than one universal half-life.

Example:
freshness_weight = exp(-lambda(signal_type) * age_in_hours_or_days)

Where lambda differs by signal family.

7.4 Freshness law
No stale signal may silently override strong fresh evidence.

But stale long-memory may still act as a prior.

That distinction is crucial.

8. Shared ambiguity model
Ambiguity is a first-class concept because the coach must know when it does not know enough.

8.1 Hypothesis ambiguity
A baseline formula:

ambiguity = 1 - (top_score - second_score)

Then adjust upward if:

evidence diversity is poor,

evidence freshness weak,

top score itself is low,

contradictory support exists.

8.2 Action ambiguity
Even if diagnosis ambiguity is moderate, action ambiguity may be low if multiple strategies converge.

Example:

whether it is retrieval weakness or fragile mastery, spaced retrieval may still be a safe move.

So maintain:

diagnosis ambiguity

action ambiguity

They are not identical.

8.3 Ambiguity law
High diagnosis ambiguity does not always block action.
It blocks overconfident action.

That means:

safe blended strategies may proceed,

hard mastery verdicts may not.

9. Shared severity and urgency framework
These must be standardized or Risk, Adaptation, and Protection will constantly disagree.

9.1 Severity computation
Severity should combine:

likely harm if unaddressed,

leverage of affected topic,

structural centrality,

current instability,

recurrence.

Example:
severity = harm * leverage * centrality * instability_adjustment

9.2 Urgency computation
Urgency should combine:

severity,

time horizon,

velocity,

learner exposure frequency,

reversibility.

Example:
urgency = severity * time_pressure_factor * velocity_factor * irreversibility_factor

9.3 Severity vs urgency examples
High severity, medium urgency
Deep prerequisite weakness on a topic not immediately needed.

Medium severity, high urgency
Pressure weakness right before exam.

High severity, high urgency
False mastery on a high-leverage exam-near topic.

Low severity, high urgency
Minor but immediate session-level fatigue constraint.

10. Shared fragility framework
Fragility is central to your coach vision.

A learner/topic may look okay but still be fragile.

10.1 Fragility inputs
inconsistent performance

delayed recall decay

mixed-context failure

pressure drop

recurrence after repair

support dependence

session-to-session volatility

10.2 Fragility formula
Example:

fragility = volatility + delayed_failure + support_dependence + context_collapse + recurrence_penalty

Bounded to 0..1.

10.3 Fragility law
Fragility should cap:

mastery confidence,

sequence advancement confidence,

pressure escalation,

aggressive compression.

11. Shared readiness framework
Readiness is action-specific.

The learner may be ready for:

scaffolded solve
but not ready for:

timed mixed-context proof.

11.1 Readiness types
ready_for_reteach

ready_for_independent_solve

ready_for_transfer

ready_for_mixed_context

ready_for_light_pressure

ready_for_topic_advance

ready_for_exam_mock

11.2 Readiness formula
Example:

readiness(action) = skill_fit * state_fit * proof_progress * risk_inverse * protection_compliance

This lets different actions use the same shared logic but different thresholds.

12. Cross-engine event choreography
Now we define how engines interact at runtime.

Your audited nucleus already shows cross-engine writes materially changing planning behavior. 


Now we formalize the choreography.

12.1 Primary event families
Evidence events
response.recorded

behavior.recorded

session.completed

observation.received

State events
concept.state.updated

topic.state.updated

learner.state.updated

Diagnostic events
hypothesis.reranked

misconception.detected

interference.detected

ambiguity.raised

probe.created

probe.completed

Decision events
teaching.decision.emitted

timing.decision.emitted

risk.assessed

adaptation.event.emitted

protection.rule.created

sequence.plan.created

Construction events
session.plan.created

content.selected

intervention.created

assessment.created

Governance events
confidence.gate.hit

contradiction.detected

arbitration.resolved

policy.hit

decision.trace.completed

Learning events
intervention.outcome.logged

memory.updated

forecast.updated

coach.evaluated

12.2 Event causation law
Every event should carry:

event_id

causation_id

correlation_id

This lets you reconstruct full reasoning episodes.

Example:
a session.plan.created event may trace back to:

response.recorded

hypothesis.reranked

risk.assessed

protection.rule.created

all inside one causal chain.

13. Engine API contract model
Every engine should expose a disciplined interface.

13.1 Evaluation contract
Each engine should have something like:

trait IntelligenceEngine<I, O> {
    fn evaluate(&self, input: I, ctx: EngineContext) -> EngineResult<O>;
}
Where EngineResult<O> includes:

output payload

confidence

severity/urgency if relevant

blockers

challenger hooks

trace refs

generated events

13.2 Shared engine result
struct EngineResult<T> {
    output: T,
    confidence: f32,
    evidence_strength: f32,
    freshness: f32,
    hardness: Option<DecisionHardness>,
    blocker_refs: Vec<String>,
    warning_refs: Vec<String>,
    trace_step: TraceStepDraft,
    emitted_events: Vec<DomainEvent>,
}
13.3 Context object
struct EngineContext {
    learner_id: String,
    session_id: Option<String>,
    subject_id: Option<String>,
    topic_scope: Vec<String>,
    now: DateTime<Utc>,
    correlation_id: String,
}
14. Engine input packs
To avoid constant scattered queries, use structured input packs.

14.1 Diagnostic pack
struct DiagnosticPack {
    topic_states: Vec<TopicState>,
    concept_states: Vec<ConceptState>,
    learner_state: LearnerStateSnapshot,
    evidence_packets: Vec<EvidencePacket>,
    misconception_instances: Vec<MisconceptionInstance>,
    interference_events: Vec<InterferenceEvent>,
    mastery_gates: Vec<MasteryGate>,
}
14.2 Decision pack
struct DecisionPack {
    dominant_hypotheses: Vec<HypothesisSummary>,
    learner_state: LearnerStateSnapshot,
    risk_values: Vec<RiskValue>,
    protection_rules: Vec<ProtectionRule>,
    mastery_status: Vec<MasteryVerdict>,
    topic_states: Vec<TopicState>,
    timing_state: Vec<TimingDecision>,
}
14.3 Session composition pack
struct SessionCompositionPack {
    teaching_decision: TeachingDecision,
    learner_state: LearnerStateSnapshot,
    topic_state: TopicState,
    active_misconceptions: Vec<MisconceptionInstance>,
    active_interference: Vec<InterferenceEvent>,
    timing_constraints: Vec<TimingDecision>,
    protection_rules: Vec<ProtectionRule>,
    content_candidates: Vec<ContentArtifact>,
}
This makes engine calls cleaner and testable.

15. Engine dependency graph
Not every engine may call every other engine directly.

15.1 Allowed direct dependencies
Evidence layer
May feed everyone downstream.

Diagnostic layer
May consume:

evidence,

learner state,

knowledge graph,

memory priors.

Decision layer
May consume:

diagnostic outputs,

learner state,

risk,

mastery proof,

memory,

protection rules.

Construction layer
May consume:

approved decision outputs only.

Governance layer
May inspect all major outputs, but should not fabricate new evidence.

15.2 Forbidden patterns
Session Composer directly rewriting hypotheses

Content Selection overriding strategy family

Forecast directly certifying present mastery

Memory engine overriding fresh learner-state updates

Risk engine silently inserting session blocks

Adaptation engine bypassing governance on hard reroute

These forbidden patterns matter a lot.

16. Shared thresholds and calibration doctrine
This system will live or die by threshold quality.

16.1 Threshold classes
You need thresholds for:

confidence

severity

urgency

ambiguity

fragility

readiness

evidence sufficiency

contradiction severity

dwell override

16.2 Threshold law
Thresholds should be:

centralized,

versioned,

subject-adjustable where justified,

learner-adjustable only through controlled logic,

auditable.

Do not scatter magic numbers across engines.

16.3 Calibration loop
Thresholds should be reviewed against:

false mastery rate

unnecessary probe rate

overblocking rate

underprotection rate

adaptation churn rate

warning precision

17. Shared explanation substrate
The system should also have a machine-readable explanation grammar for traces and learner-facing summaries.

17.1 Internal explanation grammar
Every major decision should support:

what was decided

why

what evidence mattered most

what uncertainty remains

what blocked stronger action

what would change the decision

17.2 Example internal explanation object
struct DecisionExplanation {
    summary: String,
    top_reasons: Vec<String>,
    key_evidence_refs: Vec<String>,
    uncertainty_notes: Vec<String>,
    blockers: Vec<String>,
    next_clarifying_actions: Vec<String>,
}
This improves both auditability and debugging.

18. Shared priority and queueing model
The orchestrator needs a common way to decide what recomputes first.

18.1 Priority factors
event recency

severity

urgency

scope size

learner currently active?

contradiction present?

policy hit present?

exam horizon pressure

18.2 Queue types
Immediate queue
response-based diagnostic updates

active session learner-state changes

governance blocks

contradiction events

Near-term queue
timing recalculations

risk refresh

adaptation review

Planning queue
sequencing

forecast

content sufficiency analysis

This avoids recomputing everything constantly.

19. Shared testing doctrine
The substrate itself must be tested, not only the engines.

19.1 What to test
evidence weighting consistency

confidence propagation

freshness decay

ambiguity calculations

severity/urgency separation

readiness calculation

threshold behavior

event causation chains

trace completeness

19.2 Golden scenarios
Build canonical scenarios such as:

knows topic untimed, fails timed

learns new concept, old concept destabilizes

correct today, forgets tomorrow

high confidence learner with shallow understanding

fragile learner after repeated failure

misconception hidden under decent raw scores

These should become permanent benchmark scenarios.

That would give the whole engine family a shared backbone.

21. Final doctrine of the substrate
The academic coach should not be a collection of clever engines negotiating in chaos.

It should be a single reasoning civilization with:

shared evidence law,

shared uncertainty law,

shared freshness law,

shared action hardness law,

shared event choreography,

shared trace grammar,

and shared thresholds.

That is what will make the full day-1 architecture behave like one true brain.

I’m now defining the lifecycle machinery: how hypotheses, mastery, sessions, interventions, adaptations, and contradictions move over time instead of existing as static records.

Thought for 18s
Good. Now we move from engines and shared scoring into the lifecycle layer.

This is where the system stops being a set of intelligent evaluators and becomes a living operating system with durable flows over time. Your audited nucleus already contains the beginnings of this: a topic clinic feedback loop that re-analyzes a topic after each clinic session, writes intervention history, schedules delayed recall, and reloads intervention memory; a 6-checkpoint mastery contract; persisted timing decisions; adaptation logs with dwell logic; and planner-facing cross-engine feedback.

State Machines and Lifecycle Constitution
1. Purpose of lifecycle design
The coach should not treat states as static labels.

A topic is not just “weak.”
A hypothesis is not just “retrieval weakness.”
An intervention is not just “used.”

Each of these moves through a life.

The purpose of lifecycle design is to define:

how something is born,

how it becomes active,

how it changes,

how it is verified,

how it is downgraded or closed,

how it reopens,

and what events move it from one state to another.

Without lifecycle structure, the coach will become:

forgetful,

jumpy,

contradictory,

and hard to debug.

2. Lifecycle design laws
Law 1: every major intelligence object needs a temporal state machine
At minimum:

hypotheses

topic states

mastery contracts

interventions

adaptations

contradictions

sessions

Law 2: state transitions must be event-driven
No silent mutation of major states.

Law 3: reopen is a first-class transition
The system must support reopening without corrupting history.

Law 4: closed does not mean forgotten
Closed items remain available to memory and audit.

Law 5: advancement and closure should usually require evidence, not just time passing
3. Hypothesis lifecycle
This is one of the most important lifecycles in the whole system.

3.1 Hypothesis mission
A hypothesis explains a learner problem under uncertainty.

Examples:

misconception

retrieval weakness

pressure performance issue

interference

prerequisite weakness

The audited Topic engine already runs a 3-layer hypothesis system: generate from concept patterns, adjust via evidence spine, then adjust via intervention memory.

3.2 Hypothesis states
I would define these states:

proposed

active

leading

bounded

confirmed

downgraded

falsified

archived

reopened

Meaning
proposed
Generated from initial patterns but not yet well-supported.

active
Currently plausible and part of the competitive field.

leading
Currently highest-ranked explanation.

bounded
Not fully confirmed, but safe enough to act around with a limited strategy.

confirmed
Evidence sufficiently strong across multiple modes.

downgraded
Still plausible but materially weaker than before.

falsified
Evidence now strongly contradicts it.

archived
Closed for active decision use, but retained historically.

reopened
A previously downgraded/falsified/archived hypothesis becomes relevant again.

3.3 Hypothesis transitions
Birth
Triggered by:

topic analysis,

new evidence cluster,

probe result,

repeated intervention failure,

contradiction.

Transition:

none → proposed

Activation
If support rises above minimum threshold:

proposed → active

Leadership
If it becomes top explanation:

active → leading

Bounded action transition
If ambiguity remains moderate but safe action exists:

active or leading → bounded

This is important because not every useful action requires full confirmation.

Confirmation
If:

evidence strong enough,

counterevidence weak,

diversity sufficient,

confidence gate passes:

leading or bounded → confirmed

Downgrade
If:

fresh counterevidence appears,

a probe weakens it,

intervention success contradicts it:

active/leading/confirmed → downgraded

Falsification
If:

direct strong disconfirming evidence accumulates:

active/leading → falsified

Reopen
If:

same pattern reappears,

similar contradiction returns,

previous repair decays:

downgraded/falsified/archived → reopened → active

3.4 Hypothesis closure law
A hypothesis should not vanish silently.
When it leaves active service, it becomes:

downgraded,

falsified,

or archived.

3.5 Hypothesis lifecycle metrics
Track:

time to stable leading hypothesis

reversal rate

false confirmation rate

reopen frequency

probe yield per ambiguity cluster

4. Topic lifecycle
A topic needs a richer lifecycle than just weak/strong.

4.1 Topic mission
A topic is a managed instructional object with:

concept structure,

proof obligations,

intervention history,

risk exposure,

memory.

4.2 Topic states
I would define:

unseen

scoped

entered

diagnosing

repairing

stabilizing

proving

provisionally_ready

mastery_proven

fragile

regressing

reopened

retired

Meaning
unseen
No meaningful work yet.

scoped
Topic graph loaded; prerequisites and concept neighborhood known.

entered
Topic actively selected for coaching work.

diagnosing
Coach is still determining core weakness pattern.

repairing
Intervention in progress against identified problems.

stabilizing
Initial repair succeeded, but topic still needs consolidation.

proving
Mastery gates are actively being checked.

provisionally_ready
Strong enough for cautious advancement, but not fully secure.

mastery_proven
Proof contract satisfied sufficiently.

fragile
Looks okay but instability remains high.

regressing
Previously improved, now declining.

reopened
Was thought secure or parked, now needs active attention again.

retired
No longer active in current planning horizon; kept historically.

4.3 Topic transitions
Topic entry flow
unseen → scoped → entered → diagnosing

Diagnosis resolves
diagnosing → repairing
or

diagnosing → proving if topic was mostly strong and only needed proof completion

Early repair success
repairing → stabilizing

Proof attempt path
stabilizing → proving

proving → provisionally_ready

proving → mastery_proven

proving → fragile if gates met unevenly

Failure path
repairing or proving → regressing

regressing → reopened

reopened → diagnosing or repairing depending on cause clarity

Stability loss
mastery_proven → fragile

mastery_proven → reopened if false mastery appears

provisionally_ready → fragile if delayed recall or mixed-context collapse occurs

4.4 Topic lifecycle law
A topic should not jump from first exposure to mastery_proven without passing through proof-aware states.

That is already consistent with your audited nucleus, which explicitly builds proof contracts and delayed recall obligations.

4.5 Topic lifecycle metrics
Track:

time in diagnosing

repair cycles required

fragile-to-regress rate

false mastery reopen rate

average time from entered to mastery_proven

5. Mastery lifecycle
The mastery lifecycle is distinct from the topic lifecycle.

A topic can be active without mastery being close.
Mastery is the lifecycle of proof.

5.1 Mastery states
not_started

contract_open

gathering_evidence

partially_met

conditionally_met

fragile_pass

met

suspect

invalidated

reopened

closed

Meaning
not_started
No contract yet.

contract_open
Proof contract exists.

gathering_evidence
Evidence accumulating across gates.

partially_met
Some gates met, others not.

conditionally_met
Enough for cautious temporary use, but not full proof.

fragile_pass
Core gates met, but fragility too high.

met
Contract genuinely satisfied.

suspect
Looks met, but false mastery or contradiction has appeared.

invalidated
Proof no longer trusted.

reopened
Contract reopened due to new evidence.

closed
Retired from active checking, archived historically.

5.2 Gate progression
Each gate also has a mini-state:

unobserved

observed_weak

partially_met

met

met_fragile

blocked

Example:
transfer may be:

met on one representation,

but blocked on mixed context.

5.3 Mastery transitions
not_started → contract_open

contract_open → gathering_evidence

gathering_evidence → partially_met

partially_met → conditionally_met

conditionally_met → fragile_pass or met

met → suspect if contradiction or false mastery flag appears

suspect → invalidated or reopened

reopened → gathering_evidence

5.4 Invalidators
Mastery can be invalidated by:

delayed recall collapse

pressure failure

mixed-context failure

recurring misconception

unresolved interference

contradiction event

5.5 Mastery lifecycle law
No met verdict should be terminal forever.
Mastery must remain vulnerable to invalidation by future strong counterevidence.

5.6 Mastery lifecycle metrics
Track:

conditional-to-met conversion rate

met-to-suspect rate

false mastery invalidation rate

gate-specific bottlenecks

6. Intervention lifecycle
An intervention should be treated like an experiment with memory, not a disposable activity.

Your audited nucleus already writes intervention history and infers outcomes after clinic sessions.

6.1 Intervention states
designed

approved

scheduled

in_progress

completed

awaiting_outcome

evaluated

effective

partially_effective

ineffective

harmful

retired

reused

reopened

Meaning
designed
Constructed by engine but not yet approved.

approved
Governance and decision layer allow it.

scheduled
Attached to a session or timing slot.

in_progress
Being used now.

completed
Execution finished.

awaiting_outcome
Need immediate or delayed evidence before judging.

evaluated
Outcome assessment complete.

effective / partially_effective / ineffective / harmful
Permanent outcome classes.

retired
No longer used actively.

reused
Selected again due to prior fit.

reopened
Reconsidered after prior retirement because context changed.

6.2 Intervention transitions
designed → approved → scheduled → in_progress → completed → awaiting_outcome → evaluated

evaluated → effective / partially_effective / ineffective / harmful

effective may later → reused

ineffective/harmful may later → retired

retired may → reopened in a changed context

6.3 Outcome windows
Interventions need multiple windows:

immediate effect

short-delay effect

durable effect

A misconception repair that looks good immediately but fails next day should not be labeled fully effective.

6.4 Intervention lifecycle law
The coach must distinguish:

used

worked

seemed to work

kept working

6.5 Intervention metrics
Track:

immediate vs delayed effectiveness

harmful intervention rate

reuse success rate

family-level effectiveness by diagnosis class

7. Adaptation lifecycle
Your audited Adaptation engine already has dwell logic, stop lists, and pacing windows.

Adaptation needs a clean lifecycle so the system does not thrash.

7.1 Adaptation states
candidate

recommended

gated

approved

executing

observing

validated

invalidated

suppressed

expired

Meaning
candidate
Potential change detected.

recommended
Engine proposes it.

gated
Waiting on confidence/governance/protection checks.

approved
Allowed to proceed.

executing
Change now active.

observing
Waiting to see if the change helped.

validated
Change seems to have worked.

invalidated
Change did not help or caused harm.

suppressed
Blocked due to dwell window, low confidence, or policy.

expired
Old adaptation no longer relevant.

7.2 Adaptation transitions
candidate → recommended

recommended → gated

gated → approved or suppressed

approved → executing

executing → observing

observing → validated or invalidated

validated/invalidated → expired

invalidated may generate new candidate

7.3 Dwell relationship
One of the key lifecycle laws:

a new adaptation candidate may exist

but remain suppressed during dwell unless urgency/severity crosses override threshold

That matches your audited dwell concept directly. 


7.4 Adaptation lifecycle law
Adaptation must support waiting.
Not every detected change need should become immediate action.

7.5 Adaptation metrics
Track:

candidate-to-approved rate

suppression rate

validated vs invalidated adaptations

dwell overrides

adaptation churn rate

8. Contradiction lifecycle
Contradictions must not just be detected; they must be managed through resolution.

8.1 Contradiction states
detected

classified

under_review

arbitrating

resolved

reopened

suppressed_invalid

escalated

Meaning
detected
A contradiction signal appeared.

classified
Type identified.

under_review
System is gathering relevant engine outputs.

arbitrating
Formal conflict resolution running.

resolved
Winner/compromise/reopen path chosen.

reopened
Contradiction returns after prior resolution.

suppressed_invalid
Turned out not to be a real contradiction.

escalated
Too severe or complex; forced broader re-evaluation.

8.2 Contradiction transitions
detected → classified → under_review → arbitrating → resolved

resolved → reopened if same inconsistency recurs

classified → suppressed_invalid if evidence weak

under_review → escalated if system-wide implications emerge

8.3 Resolution outputs
Resolution may produce:

downgrade mastery

block session release

reopen diagnosis

issue protection rule

approve blended compromise

update risk posture

8.4 Contradiction lifecycle law
No critical contradiction should remain permanently open while the system continues as though nothing happened.

8.5 Contradiction metrics
Track:

resolution latency

reopen rate

contradiction classes by frequency

engine pairs most often in conflict

9. Session lifecycle
The session is where many other lifecycles converge.

9.1 Session states
draft

composed

governance_pending

approved

released

active

interrupted

completed

post_processed

outcome_linked

archived

Meaning
draft
Initial plan skeleton exists.

composed
Session Composer built full structure.

governance_pending
Awaiting confidence/policy/contradiction/protection checks.

approved
Passed core checks.

released
Made available for actual use.

active
Currently running.

interrupted
Stopped or materially disrupted.

completed
Execution done.

post_processed
Evidence extracted and normalized.

outcome_linked
Intervention outcomes, state updates, and memory updates connected.

archived
Historical session, immutable except annotations.

9.2 Session transitions
draft → composed → governance_pending → approved → released → active → completed → post_processed → outcome_linked → archived

Interruptions can insert:

active → interrupted → active
or

active → interrupted → post_processed

9.3 Session release gates
A session should not move from governance_pending to approved unless:

no blocking policy hit

no unresolved blocking protection rule

confidence sufficient for major actions

contradiction state acceptable

trace generated

9.4 Session lifecycle law
A session is not truly complete until its evidence has been absorbed back into the brain.

That is exactly the spirit of your audited topic clinic feedback loop. 


9.5 Session metrics
Track:

governance rejection rate

interruption rate

block completion quality

post-processed latency

session-to-outcome linkage completeness

10. Cross-lifecycle interactions
These state machines do not live separately.

10.1 Hypothesis ↔ Intervention
leading hypothesis spawns intervention design

intervention outcome updates hypothesis confidence

failed intervention may reopen or downgrade hypothesis

10.2 Topic ↔ Mastery
topic enters proving state when mastery contract active

mastery invalidation can move topic from mastery_proven to fragile or reopened

10.3 Session ↔ Intervention
session may contain one or more interventions

session completion triggers intervention outcome evaluation

10.4 Adaptation ↔ Session
adaptation may invalidate a queued session

poor session outcome may create adaptation candidate

10.5 Contradiction ↔ Mastery / Risk / Strategy
contradiction may downgrade mastery

contradiction may escalate risk

contradiction may trigger arbitration or reopen diagnosis

10.6 Topic ↔ Sequence
topic lifecycle state constrains sequence advancement

fragile/regressing topic should affect sequence stability

11. Reopen doctrine across all lifecycles
Reopen is central to your whole vision.

The coach should understand that:

things once repaired can relapse

things once proven can become suspect

things once dismissed can return

things once blocked can become possible

11.1 Universal reopen triggers
Across objects, common reopen triggers include:

strong fresh counterevidence

repeated recurrence

contradiction

delayed recall collapse

interference emergence

failed validation after provisional success

11.2 Reopen law
Reopen must never erase prior history.
It creates a new active phase on top of historical lineage.

Each lifecycle should be driven by explicit events.

14. Final doctrine of lifecycle design
The coach should not behave like a stateless recommender.

It should behave like a living academic reasoning organism in which:

hypotheses compete and mature,

topics move through diagnosis, repair, stability, proof, and relapse,

mastery can be earned, suspected, invalidated, and reopened,

interventions are judged over time,

adaptations can be proposed, suppressed, validated, or invalidated,

contradictions are managed, not ignored,

and sessions are only complete when their evidence flows back into the brain.

That is what turns your intelligence engine from a set of clever modules into a true long-horizon coach.

I’m defining the meta-signal layer now: the tensions the coach should constantly feel and the rules for how those tensions shape diagnosis, protection, pacing, and intervention choice.

Thought for 11s
Good. This next layer is critical because it gives the coach felt pressure.

Without a tension system, the engine can compute scores but still miss the deeper reality that:

a learner may be progressing and destabilizing at the same time,

a topic may look “ready” but feel brittle,

a plan may be structurally correct but psychologically too heavy,

the system may be trying to advance while several hidden pressures are building underneath.

Tension System and Meta-Signals Constitution
1. Purpose
The tension layer is the coach’s meta-signal nervous system.

Its role is to answer:

what pressure is building?

what conflict is increasing?

what is becoming unstable before it visibly fails?

what is pulling against progress?

what should the coach protect, compress, delay, or escalate?

This layer does not replace topic state, learner state, risk, or adaptation.

It sits above them and synthesizes pressure gradients from their interactions.

Think of it like this:

topic state says: “this topic is 0.63 ready”

learner state says: “confidence fragile”

mastery says: “transfer gate still weak”

timing says: “retest due”

risk says: “exam exposure rising”

The tension layer says:

“There is a rising conflict between desired advancement and actual stability.”

That is a different kind of intelligence.

2. What a tension is
A tension is not just a score.

A tension is a dynamic pressure field generated by friction between states, goals, constraints, or trajectories.

It has 6 permanent properties:

source — what is in conflict

direction — what it is pushing toward or against

magnitude — how strong the pressure is

velocity — whether it is rising or falling

scope — learner, topic, subject, plan, session, exam horizon

resolvability — whether it is easy, moderate, or hard to relieve

So for example:

a learner can have high confidence tension, moderate plan tension, low time tension

a topic can have low readiness tension but high contradiction tension

a session can have low risk but high overload tension

That is why tension must be first-class.

3. Why this layer matters
Many of the hardest coaching failures do not begin as obvious weaknesses.
They begin as tensions.

Examples:

The learner is succeeding, but every success is scaffolded and fragile.

The sequence is structurally sound, but it is eroding confidence.

The plan is technically on track, but time pressure is now forcing compression.

The topic looks stable, but interference pressure is quietly climbing.

The coach is piling obligations faster than recovery can absorb them.

A normal analytics layer notices the failure late.

A tension layer notices the strain early.

That fits the spirit of your current engine, where adaptation is already triggered by tension signals and planner behavior is already being modified by protect/compress/reroute outputs.

4. Canonical tension families
I would define these as permanent.

4.1 Confidence Tension
Conflict between:

what the learner is being asked to do,

and what their confidence state can currently carry.

Typical sources:

recent failure clusters,

fragile confidence state,

challenge intensity too high,

repeated exposure to unresolved weakness,

poor recovery after mistakes.

This one already exists implicitly in your audited adaptation triggers.

4.2 Plan Tension
Conflict between:

what the plan intends,

and what the system can realistically execute well.

Typical sources:

too many obligations,

too many weak topics,

too little time,

over-complex session architecture,

excessive adaptation churn.

This also already appears explicitly in your audited adaptation triggers. 


4.3 Time Tension
Conflict between:

what remains to be learned/proven,

and how much real calendar runway remains.

Typical sources:

exam horizon compression,

delayed review debt,

too many unstable high-yield topics,

slow mastery conversion.

4.4 Readiness Tension
Conflict between:

the desire to advance or escalate,

and the learner/topic not being truly ready.

Typical sources:

weak proof gates,

fragile readiness,

low pressure readiness,

unresolved misconception/interference.

4.5 Progress Tension
Conflict between:

visible activity,

and meaningful progress.

Typical sources:

repeated sessions without slope improvement,

“busy but not moving,”

high effort with flat outcomes,

cosmetic gains.

4.6 Overload Tension
Conflict between:

cognitive/emotional/system load,

and recovery capacity.

Typical sources:

high fatigue,

too many concurrent obligations,

dense mixed-context demands,

retest clustering,

low recovery speed.

4.7 Contradiction Tension
Conflict between:

two or more major system beliefs.

Typical sources:

mastery verdict vs transfer results,

low risk vs worsening forecast,

stable topic vs recurring misconception,

no_timing protection vs timed assessment plan.

4.8 Interference Tension
Conflict generated by concept collision pressure.

Typical sources:

confusion pairs studied too close,

representation collisions,

procedure blend,

near-neighbor instability.

4.9 Fragility Tension
Conflict between:

apparent competence,

and lack of structural robustness.

Typical sources:

recent repair not yet stabilized,

high volatility,

support dependence,

delayed recall weakness.

4.10 Coverage Tension
Conflict between:

what must be covered,

and what can be covered without harming depth and proof.

Typical sources:

uncovered high-yield topics,

too much fragile review,

blocked downstream chains,

false mastery slowing plan reliability.

4.11 Strategy Tension
Conflict between:

the current strategy family,

and the signals suggesting it is losing fit.

Typical sources:

diminishing returns,

repeated low intervention success,

learner state drift,

memory mismatch.

4.12 Recovery Tension
Conflict between:

how much damage a failure created,

and how well the learner is bouncing back.

Typical sources:

confidence drop after error,

slow recovery profile,

repeated re-entry difficulty after challenge.

5. Meta-signals above tension
Tensions are the main field, but above them I would define a smaller set of meta-signals that summarize broader system posture.

5.1 Stability Signal
How coherent and non-fragile the current learning system is.

Built from:

fragility tension

contradiction tension

learner volatility

recent reopen rate

5.2 Momentum Signal
Whether the learner is truly moving forward.

Built from:

progress tension inverse

improvement velocity

validated adaptation rate

intervention success trend

5.3 Pressure Signal
How compressed and stressed the system currently is.

Built from:

time tension

overload tension

plan tension

risk urgency

5.4 Trustworthiness Signal
How much the coach should trust its current understanding.

Built from:

ambiguity

contradiction tension

confidence gate activity

evidence freshness/diversity

5.5 Recovery Signal
How resilient the learner currently is after challenge.

Built from:

recovery tension inverse

confidence fragility

fatigue

recent bounce-back quality

These meta-signals are not just dashboard fluff.
They should feed adaptation, protection, pacing, and release hardness.

6. Canonical data model
I would create these permanent entities.

6.1 Tension record
ic_tensions

Core fields:

That gives you both live tension and longitudinal tension memory.

7. Tension computation doctrine
A tension should almost always be computed from a conflict function, not a raw score.

General form:

Tension = conflict(A, B, constraints, trajectory)

Where:

A = desired or expected state

B = actual or supported state

constraints = risk, learner limits, proof requirements, time limits

trajectory = whether the gap is widening or closing

This is different from a simple weakness score.

8. Tension formulas by family
8.1 Confidence Tension
Use:

confidence fragility

recent failure density

challenge intensity

recovery weakness

public/visible error sensitivity if modeled

Example:

confidence_tension = fragility * challenge_load * failure_recency_adjustment * recovery_penalty

Boost when:

learner is being pushed while fragile,

repeated wrongs happened recently,

confidence rebuild need already known.

This aligns directly with your current adaptation trigger using confidence tension as a protect signal.

8.2 Plan Tension
Use:

active obligations

unresolved weak topics

review debt

session density

available calendar budget

adaptation churn

Example:

plan_tension = workload_pressure * unresolved_instability * time_compression * churn_penalty

This maps directly to your current plan tension → compress behavior. 


8.3 Time Tension
Use:

exam horizon

estimated sessions needed

proof burden remaining

high-yield instability

review obligations

Example:

time_tension = remaining_work / feasible_capacity

Then modulate by:

urgency of affected topics

compressibility of content

8.4 Readiness Tension
Use:

desired next action difficulty

current readiness for that action

fragility

active blockers

Example:

readiness_tension = desired_action_threshold - actual_readiness

Adjusted upward by:

false mastery risk,

unresolved misconception,

unresolved interference.

8.5 Progress Tension
Use:

recent effort intensity

recent actual improvement velocity

repeated ineffective interventions

same-topic recurrence

Example:

progress_tension = effort_index - validated_progress_index

This is powerful because it reveals “lots happening, not much changing.”

8.6 Overload Tension
Use:

fatigue state

number of concurrent demands

session density

retest clustering

recovery profile

Example:

overload_tension = load / recovery_capacity

8.7 Contradiction Tension
Use:

contradiction count

contradiction severity

criticality of affected beliefs

openness duration

recurrence

Example:

contradiction_tension = severity * criticality * unresolved_duration_factor

8.8 Interference Tension
Use:

active interference events

recency of adjacent instruction

deterioration magnitude

concept centrality

unresolved contrast repairs

Example:

interference_tension = active_interference_severity * recency_of_collision * leverage

8.9 Fragility Tension
Use:

volatility

delayed recall weakness

support dependence

pressure collapse

mixed-context weakness

Example:

fragility_tension = instability_pattern * insufficient_proof_depth

8.10 Coverage Tension
Use:

uncovered high-yield material

blocked downstream chains

over-investment in stable topics

false mastery suspects

time budget strain

This fits your audited coverage logic around gap detection, proof-blocked topics, speed gaps, and over-invested stable topics. 


8.11 Strategy Tension
Use:

intervention success drop

diminishing returns

strategy memory mismatch

repeated refine/reroute signals

adaptation trigger count

8.12 Recovery Tension
Use:

recovery profile weakness

post-failure degradation

repeated incomplete recovery

confidence drop magnitude

next-session re-entry quality

9. Tension state machine
Each tension should itself have a lifecycle.

States:

emerging

active

rising

critical

easing

resolved

recurrent

archived

Transition examples
low magnitude appears → emerging

threshold crossed → active

velocity positive and above threshold → rising

severity + urgency high → critical

after successful protective action or improved evidence → easing

after sustained relief → resolved

if same tension reappears in same scope → recurrent

This is important because the coach should respond differently to:

a first-time emerging tension

a chronic recurrent one

a rapidly rising one

10. How tensions feed engines
This is where the layer becomes useful.

10.1 Adaptation Engine
Tensions are major adaptation triggers.

Examples:

high confidence tension → protect

high plan tension → compress

high strategy tension → refine or reroute

high contradiction tension → reopen_diagnosis or arbitration request

high progress tension → reopen_diagnosis or strategy rethink

Your current audited adaptation logic already uses confidence tension and plan tension directly.

10.2 Protection Rule Engine
Tensions can emit or strengthen protective rules.

Examples:

overload tension → no_high_density_session

readiness tension → no_sequence_advance

interference tension → no_topic_mix

confidence tension → no_timing_pressure or stabilize_before_advance

10.3 Teaching Strategy Engine
Tensions help choose family.

Examples:

high confidence tension → confidence_rebuild

high interference tension → contrast_repair

high strategy tension → avoid current family

high progress tension → probe_first or diagnosis reopen support

10.4 Timing Engine
Tensions shape pacing.

Examples:

overload tension reduces density

time tension increases compression pressure

fragility tension delays proof escalation

recovery tension delays repeated retest

10.5 Sequencing Engine
Tensions change order.

Examples:

confidence tension may front-load a stabilizing win

interference tension may enforce spacing

coverage + time tension may move high-yield topics upward

plan tension may collapse low-value branches

10.6 Governance Cluster
Tensions can trigger:

confidence gate downgrade

contradiction review

arbitration

policy blocks if severe enough

11. Tension interaction rules
Tensions should not be treated independently.

There are important compound patterns.

Pattern A: High confidence tension + high progress tension
Interpretation:
The learner is trying, but the path is eroding belief and not yielding gains.

Likely actions:

protect

confidence rebuild

maybe reduce challenge density

Pattern B: High time tension + high readiness tension
Interpretation:
The system wants to move fast, but the learner/topic is not ready.

Likely actions:

compress carefully

choose exam rescue or bridge compression

strengthen governance around false mastery

Pattern C: High contradiction tension + high trustworthiness drop
Interpretation:
The coach should stop pretending it understands the situation.

Likely actions:

reopen diagnosis

diagnostic probe

downgrade hard actions

Pattern D: High overload tension + high recovery tension
Interpretation:
The learner is under too much load and not bouncing back well.

Likely actions:

no high-density session

shorter blocks

fewer simultaneous obligations

Pattern E: High interference tension + high fragility tension
Interpretation:
The topic looks unstable because concept collisions are still live.

Likely actions:

separate

contrast

do not advance on profile averages alone

12. Meta-signal computation
Now we convert the tensions into higher-order posture.

12.1 Stability Signal
Example:

stability = 1 - weighted_sum(fragility_tension, contradiction_tension, interference_tension, reopen_rate)

Interpretation:
How much the system can trust that gains are holding together.

12.2 Momentum Signal
Example:

momentum = validated_progress - progress_tension - adaptation_failure_drag

Interpretation:
Whether the learner is truly moving.

12.3 Pressure Signal
Example:

pressure = weighted_sum(time_tension, overload_tension, plan_tension, urgency_weighted_risk)

Interpretation:
How compressed the system feels overall.

12.4 Trustworthiness Signal
Example:

trustworthiness = confidence_average * evidence_diversity * freshness - contradiction_penalty - ambiguity_penalty

Interpretation:
How much the coach should trust its own current model.

12.5 Recovery Signal
Example:

recovery = inverse(recovery_tension) adjusted by recent bounce-back success

Interpretation:
How safely the system can introduce challenge again.

13. Tension-aware release gates
Before a plan is released, the system should inspect tensions.

Gate A: Critical contradiction tension?
If yes, no hard release without review.

Gate B: Critical confidence tension?
If yes, challenge intensity must be downgraded or protected.

Gate C: Critical overload tension?
If yes, session density reduced or blocked.

Gate D: High readiness tension?
If yes, no topic advance or proof escalation.

Gate E: High plan tension?
If yes, no oversized multi-objective session.

This makes the release system more intelligent than using risk alone.

14. Planner integration
Your current planner already applies boosts and penalties from risk, adaptation, and timing — for example risk > 0.6 gets a priority boost, protect mode boosts, reroute deprioritizes, retest due jumps to top, compress reduces estimated sessions, and “stop new learning” can propagate across topics. 


The tension layer should feed the planner more directly.

Examples:

high confidence tension on a topic → avoid harsh assessment-first planning

high plan tension globally → reduce portfolio breadth

high time tension → boost compressible high-yield topics

high coverage tension → surface proof-blocked/high-yield neglected topics

high interference tension → avoid adjacent scheduling

This makes the planner feel more humane and less mechanical.

15. Observability and analytics
Track these per tension family:

average magnitude

average velocity

time spent active

recurrence rate

resolution latency

false alarm rate

downstream action effectiveness after tension-triggered changes

Also track meta-signal stability over time:

trustworthiness drift

pressure wave patterns

momentum collapses

recovery collapses

This lets you see not just what the student is doing, but what the system is feeling.
