17. Final doctrine
The tension layer is where the coach becomes sensitive rather than merely analytical.

It gives the system the ability to feel:

that confidence is being strained before the learner quits,

that the plan is becoming too heavy before performance stalls,

that time pressure is distorting priorities before the exam arrives,

that mastery is brittle before it collapses,

that contradiction is accumulating before the coach makes a bad call.

Your current engine already contains the seeds of this through ic_tensions, confidence-tension and plan-tension adaptation triggers, and confidence-scaffolding signals in the adaptive profile.

The full version should promote that from a side input into a permanent governing layer of the coach brain.

The next document should define the memory, recurrence, and pattern-mining constitution — how the coach detects long-range patterns across weeks, topics, subjects, and intervention families, and turns them into smarter future decisions.


proceed

I’m extending the brain into its long-horizon memory now: how it remembers recurrence, detects patterns across weeks and subjects, and turns history into better future judgment instead of just storing logs.

Thought for 12s
Good. Now we move into the Memory, Recurrence, and Pattern-Mining Constitution.

This is the layer that makes the coach genuinely cumulative. Your audited nucleus already contains the seeds of this: Topic Intelligence closes a clinic feedback loop after every session, writes intervention history, reloads intervention memory, and schedules delayed recall; Adaptation builds a learner adaptive profile from intervention reports, pressure response, confidence tension, and teaching mode preferences; it also detects 4 cross-topic systemic patterns and maintains a stop list and adaptation log.

Memory, Recurrence, and Pattern-Mining Constitution
1. Purpose
This layer answers:

what keeps happening again?

what works for this learner, and under what conditions?

what looks local but is actually systemic?

what was repaired before but tends to relapse?

what family of strategy works on one topic but fails across others?

what slow-moving pattern is emerging before it becomes obvious failure?

Without this layer, the coach can be intelligent in the moment but still remain historically shallow.

With this layer, the coach becomes able to say:

“this learner often looks fine immediately after repair but fails delayed recall two days later,”

“contrast repair works for this learner on conceptual confusion but not on procedural instability,”

“when fatigue rises, worked-example-first becomes much more effective than retrieval-first,”

“three topics in this subject are regressing together, so this is probably not a local topic problem,”

“we are over-reviewing stable material while unresolved weak material is stalling growth.”

That last class of logic is already visible in your audited stop-list and cross-topic pattern detection.

2. Foundational doctrine
Law 1: memory is not logging
A log is chronology. Memory is structured retained judgment.

Law 2: recurrence must be explicitly modeled
If the same failure form returns, the system must know it is recurrence, not treat it as a brand-new random event.

Law 3: patterns may exist at multiple scopes
A pattern may live at:

concept level,

topic level,

subject level,

learner-global level,

strategy-family level,

or exam-horizon level.

Law 4: history must influence future judgment, but not dominate fresh evidence
Memory should act as:

prior,

warning,

fit-adjuster,

reopen trigger,

and pattern detector.

It must not crush fresh evidence under old habit.

Law 5: recurrence and pattern mining are causal tools, not just analytics
They must actually influence:

diagnosis,

timing,

protection,

sequencing,

adaptation,

and release hardness.

3. Memory architecture
I would divide memory into 6 permanent memory layers.

3.1 Episodic Memory
Stores what happened in specific sessions and interventions.

Examples:

session structure

intervention used

immediate outcome

learner state at time

trace and evidence lineage

This is the direct expansion of your current intervention history and clinic feedback loop.

3.2 Topic Memory
Stores how a topic behaves over time for this learner.

Examples:

common weakness forms

typical relapse pattern

proof bottlenecks

delayed recall tendency

frequent misconception classes

fragility pattern

3.3 Learner Memory
Stores durable tendencies of the learner.

Examples:

pressure profile tendency

confidence scaffolding need

preferred/avoided intervention families

session duration sweet spot

recovery signature

topic-type teaching preferences

Your audited learner adaptive profile already contains exactly this kind of data: preferred and avoided families, successful session duration, pressure response, learns-from-mistakes detection, confidence scaffolding need, and teaching mode preferences by topic type. 


3.4 Strategy Memory
Stores what teaching/intervention families tend to work or fail, in what contexts.

Examples:

worked_example_first success on abstract topics

contrast_repair success on interference topics

diminishing returns after repeated use

harmful overuse contexts

3.5 Systemic Pattern Memory
Stores cross-topic and cross-subject patterns.

Examples:

family_systemic_failure

subject_regression_wave

systemic_speed_deficit

systemic_recall_decay

Your audited adaptation engine already detects exactly these 4 cross-topic systemic patterns.

3.6 Governance Memory
Stores how the coach itself behaves over time.

Examples:

recurring contradiction classes

repeated overconfidence conditions

common reasons for confidence gate hits

adaptation churn tendencies

policy-hit hotspots

5. Recurrence ontology
Recurrence is so important that it needs its own explicit ontology.

5.1 Recurrence families
At minimum:

misconception_recurrence

interference_recurrence

delayed_recall_recurrence

false_mastery_recurrence

confidence_collapse_recurrence

pressure_failure_recurrence

strategy_failure_recurrence

overreview_recurrence

regression_recurrence

contradiction_recurrence

adaptation_churn_recurrence

proof_gate_bottleneck_recurrence

5.2 Recurrence properties
Every recurrence should store:

family

scope

count

average interval

severity trend

current openness

linkage to prior episodes

whether recurrence is accelerating or easing

5.3 Recurrence law
A recurrence is not just “happened more than once.”

It becomes first-class when:

it has happened enough to matter,

the pattern is structurally similar,

and it should change future judgment.

Example:
one delayed recall fail is evidence.
repeated delayed recall failure across repaired topics is recurrence.

6. Pattern-mining constitution
This is where the coach becomes able to discover higher-order truth.

6.1 Pattern scopes
Pattern mining must operate at:

concept scope

topic scope

subject scope

learner-global scope

strategy-family scope

calendar/exam-horizon scope

6.2 Pattern families
I would define permanent pattern families like:

Learning-pattern families
repeated_fragile_gain

delayed_recall_decay

pressure_specific_collapse

representation_specific_failure

mixed_context_instability

recovery_delay_pattern

Strategy-pattern families
family_systemic_failure

diminishing_returns_family

strategy_topic_type_fit

strategy_harm_pattern

strategy_recovery_fit

Portfolio-pattern families
subject_regression_wave

systemic_speed_deficit

systemic_recall_decay

overinvested_stable_topics

blocked_downstream_cluster

Governance-pattern families
contradiction_hotspot

confidence_overreach_pattern

false_mastery_cluster

adaptation_churn_cluster

Your audited system already contains several of these in embryonic form, especially the 4 systemic patterns and stop-list logic.

7. Pattern detectors
I would build dedicated miners, not one vague “pattern detector.”

7.1 Topic Recurrence Miner
Finds repeated patterns inside one topic.

Examples:

same misconception returns after every repair

same proof gate remains bottleneck

same interference pair keeps reopening

same transfer weakness survives multiple interventions

7.2 Learner Tendency Miner
Finds stable learner tendencies.

Examples:

succeeds better with shorter sessions

pressure collapses when fatigue medium+

improves after failure only when scaffolded

abstract topics do better with visual-first

confidence rebuild needed after two consecutive misses

Again, much of this is already foreshadowed in your adaptive profile. 


7.3 Strategy Effectiveness Miner
Finds what intervention families work where.

Examples:

contrast_repair is effective for concept blends

retrieval_reinforcement is weak when misconception remains active

worked_example_first becomes weak after repeated use on the same topic

compress mode helps only when baseline mastery already > 0.5

That last pattern is echoed by your existing adaptation trigger: time > 0.7 and mastery > 0.5 leading to compress/exam rescue. 


7.4 Systemic Pattern Miner
Finds patterns across multiple topics or subjects.

Your audited engine already defines:

family_systemic_failure

subject_regression_wave

systemic_speed_deficit

systemic_recall_decay

This miner should become the formal home for those.

7.5 Recurrence Interval Miner
Learns how often things relapse.

Examples:

misconceptions on this topic recur every 6–8 days if not retested

pressure readiness decays after 2 weeks without timed exposure

recall problems emerge 3 days after apparent repair

7.6 Contradiction Pattern Miner
Finds recurring conflict types.

Examples:

mastery vs transfer contradictions spike on procedural topics

no_timing protections are repeatedly violated by assessment construction

risk stays low while forecast worsens on one subject

8. Pattern detection rules
8.1 Support threshold rule
A pattern should not become first-class from one or two noisy events unless severity is extreme.

8.2 Similarity rule
Pattern mining must detect structural similarity, not just shared labels.

Example:
Three different “wrong answers” are not one pattern unless they share a causal form.

8.3 Scope rule
A topic-level pattern should not automatically be promoted to learner-global truth.

8.4 Recency rule
Fresh recurrence should matter more than distant historical recurrence.

8.5 Actionability rule
A pattern is valuable only if it can influence future decision quality.

9. Memory update doctrine
Memory should update in layers, not with one blunt write.

9.1 Immediate memory updates
After a session/intervention:

episode memory

intervention outcome

local topic memory update

immediate learner-state-linked notes

This already matches your clinic feedback loop structure.

9.2 Rolling memory updates
At daily or planning cadence:

strategy memory refresh

recurrence aggregation

pattern mining pass

systemic pattern refresh

9.3 Slow memory updates
At wider intervals:

learner preference stabilization

durable recovery profile refinement

governance pattern learning

long-horizon forecast calibration

10. Forgetting and memory decay
Not all memory should stay equally active forever.

10.1 Memory freshness classes
hot

warm

cool

archived

10.2 Memory decay law
Memories decay in influence, not in existence.

Meaning:

old strategy success should matter less than fresh contradictory evidence

old misconception recurrence should still exist, but as a weaker prior unless repeated again

10.3 Memory reinforcement rule
A memory becomes stronger when:

pattern recurs,

evidence is diverse,

pattern matters across scopes,

outcome consequences were meaningful

11. How memory influences the engines
11.1 Hypothesis Competition Engine
Memory provides priors.

Examples:

repeated delayed recall collapse boosts retrieval_weakness prior

recurring confusion pair boosts interference prior

historical misconception class boosts misconception prior

The audited hypothesis system already adjusts using intervention memory. 


11.2 Learner State Engine
Memory stabilizes durable tendencies.

Examples:

preferred session length

pressure vulnerability pattern

recovery speed baseline

confidence scaffolding need

Again, this is directly aligned with your adaptive profile. 


11.3 Teaching Strategy Engine
Memory adjusts family fit.

Examples:

if a strategy repeatedly underperforms in similar contexts, downweight it

if a family consistently repairs a certain problem for this learner, upweight it

11.4 Timing Engine
Memory shapes spacing and retests.

Examples:

if this topic class decays quickly, shorten delayed recall interval

if learner recovers slowly after proof failure, avoid rapid retest clustering

11.5 Adaptation Engine
Memory detects when the current route resembles a historically failing route.

That is already visible in your stop list:

stop ineffective families

stop diminishing-return families

stop over-reviewing stable topics when stalled topics exist

11.6 Risk Engine
Memory strengthens forecasts and early warnings.

Examples:

repeated fragile new learning on similar topics raises future fragility priors

repeated recall decay raises review-debt sensitivity

11.7 Protection Rule Engine
Memory can emit stronger or longer-lived protections for recurrent harmful patterns.

12. Stop-list constitution
This deserves its own formal place because your audited system already contains a real stop-list.

12.1 Stop-list purpose
The stop list is negative memory operationalized.

It answers:

what should we stop repeating?

what pattern of action is now more harmful than helpful?

what family has crossed into diminishing returns?

what should be avoided until conditions change?

12.2 Canonical stop-list families
stop_ineffective_family

stop_new_content

stop_diminishing_return_family

stop_overreview_stable_topics

stop_timed_pressure_for_scope

stop_high_density_sessions

stop_mixed_context_before_stability

stop_exam_rescue_in_wrong_context

12.3 Stop-list activation rules
A stop recommendation becomes active when:

repeated poor performance is established,

harm or stagnation exceeds threshold,

current learner/topic conditions make repetition unsafe or wasteful.

12.4 Stop-list expiry
A stop entry should expire only by:

changed learner state,

fresh contradictory evidence,

successful alternative pathway,

or governance-reviewed override.

13. Systemic pattern constitution
Your audited engine already names four high-value systemic patterns.
These should be locked as first-class constitutional objects.

13.1 family_systemic_failure
Definition:
Same intervention family failing across 3+ topics.

Interpretation:
The issue is probably not just local-topic difficulty. The family is wrong for this learner or wrong for this context.

Likely consequences:

downweight family globally

trigger refine or reroute

add stop-list entry

13.2 subject_regression_wave
Definition:
3+ topics regressing in the same subject.

Interpretation:
There may be a deeper foundational gap, overload pattern, or sequence problem.

Likely consequences:

subject-level risk increase

sequencing review

adaptation escalation

possible prerequisite root search

13.3 systemic_speed_deficit
Definition:
Multiple otherwise-stable topics showing very low speed.

Interpretation:
Exam readiness gap, not necessarily knowledge gap.

Likely consequences:

pressure/timing program

exam-rescue or speed-conditioning branch

no false readiness certification

13.4 systemic_recall_decay
Definition:
Several topics look okay in readiness but have weak recall.

Interpretation:
Review ratio or reinforcement architecture is failing.

Likely consequences:

timing refinement

spacing redesign

increased delayed recall obligations

possibly stop new content temporarily

14. Pattern-driven alerts
Pattern mining should emit actionable alerts, not just reports.

14.1 Recurrence alerts
Examples:

“Misconception recurrence detected on topic X after prior repair”

“Pressure collapse recurrence emerging across timed tasks”

14.2 Systemic alerts
Examples:

“Subject regression wave detected in mathematics”

“Systemic recall decay detected across 5 repaired topics”

14.3 Strategy alerts
Examples:

“WorkedExampleFirst showing diminishing returns on procedural topics”

“ContrastRepair highly effective on recent interference clusters”

14.4 Governance alerts
Examples:

“False mastery cluster emerging around transfer gate”

“Confidence gate hits rising for pressure-related decisions”

15. Recurrence-aware reopen rules
Memory and recurrence should strongly influence reopen behavior.

Reopen diagnosis when:
same failure form returns after prior repair

a systemic pattern suggests local diagnosis was incomplete

same contradiction class reappears

same proof bottleneck keeps re-emerging

Reopen timing when:
recall-decay recurrence appears

retest spacing repeatedly fails to hold gains

Reopen strategy when:
family_systemic_failure emerges

diminishing-returns pattern emerges

same family repeatedly needs rescue by fallback

Reopen sequence when:
subject_regression_wave appears

interference cluster emerges due to adjacency

17. Final doctrine
The coach should remember in a way that changes future intelligence.

It should remember:

what was tried,

what worked,

what only seemed to work,

what keeps coming back,

what fails across many topics,

what this learner responds to,

what this learner cannot currently carry,

and what the coach itself tends to misjudge.

Your current engine already contains the beginnings of this through intervention history, hypothesis adjustment using intervention memory, learner adaptive profile, systemic pattern detection, and the stop-list.

The full version should elevate those pieces into a permanent memory-and-pattern-mining constitution.

The next document should define the content intelligence and curriculum absorption constitution — how the brain ingests curriculum, textbooks, past questions, notes, glossaries, formulas, explanations, and generated artifacts, then turns them into trusted instructional building blocks for all the other engines.

I’m moving into the content brain now: how the system ingests curriculum, textbooks, notes, formulas, questions, and generated material, and turns all of that into usable, trusted instructional intelligence.

Thought for 21s
Good. Now we define the Content Intelligence and Curriculum Absorption Constitution.

This is the layer that gives the coach real academic substance. Without it, the rest of the brain can diagnose, prioritize, and adapt, but it will still be bottlenecked by weak material, missing representations, shallow explanations, and poor instructional fit.

Your audited nucleus already contains the seeds of this layer. Topic Intelligence ingests coaching-step evidence into ic_topic_evidence_points, reads concept nodes, topic clusters, concept edges, contrast pairs, misconceptions, responses, question instances, and evidence events, then writes ic_topic_teaching, ic_topic_evidence_points, intervention history, and timing decisions. Coverage then reads proof contracts and evidence spines from Topic Intelligence to classify topics and detect proof-blocked or false-mastery-suspect gaps. Adaptation already uses concept nodes plus intervention history and tensions to build learner profiles and stop lists. 

 

 


Content Intelligence and Curriculum Absorption Constitution
1. Purpose
This layer answers:

what is the curriculum actually asking the learner to know?

what concepts, formulas, definitions, procedures, representations, and misconceptions sit inside each topic?

what source materials do we trust?

what content artifact should exist for each instructional need?

where are the content gaps?

how should raw materials be transformed into coach-grade instructional assets?

how should generated content be checked before it is allowed into the system?

The core principle is:

the coach must not merely have content; it must understand, structure, trust-rate, and operationalize content.

2. Foundational doctrine
Law 1: raw material is not yet coach content
A curriculum PDF, textbook scan, past question pack, teacher note, or glossary sheet is only a source. It becomes coach content only after parsing, structuring, graphing, quality checks, and instructional packaging.

Law 2: content must be represented at multiple levels
The system must store content as:

source documents,

extracted units,

concept-aligned knowledge objects,

instructional artifacts,

diagnostic artifacts,

and learner-facing delivery variants.

Law 3: curriculum is the canonical outer frame
No matter how much content is ingested, curriculum and exam blueprint remain the backbone that says:

what belongs,

what is optional,

what is central,

what is prerequisite,

and what has exam leverage.

Law 4: one topic must support many artifact forms
For a single concept, the system may need:

definition,

explanation,

worked example,

step-by-step procedure,

contrast pair,

misconception note,

formula card,

recall drill,

timed probe,

mixed-context check,

and delayed-recall asset.

Law 5: content must be quality-governed
The coach should never blindly trust:

OCR output,

scraped content,

generated explanations,

ambiguous question sets,

or poorly aligned textbook fragments.

Law 6: content intelligence is bidirectional
It is not only:

curriculum → content.
It is also:

learner evidence → needed content,

strategy need → content request,

proof gap → artifact request,

recurrence pattern → content repair.

3. Permanent content domains
I would divide the content brain into 8 permanent domains.

Domain A — Source Absorption
This domain ingests raw materials:

curriculum guides,

syllabi,

textbooks,

class notes,

past questions,

marking schemes,

glossary sheets,

formula sheets,

teacher-authored resources,

generated resources.

Domain B — Structural Extraction
This domain extracts:

subjects,

topics,

concepts,

sub-concepts,

formulas,

definitions,

worked steps,

diagrams,

examples,

question-answer alignments,

misconception cues.

Domain C — Knowledge Packaging
This domain turns extracted material into knowledge objects:

concept notes,

formula cards,

glossary entries,

misconception entries,

prerequisite links,

representation variants,

example families.

Domain D — Assessment Packaging
This domain creates or organizes:

question items,

probes,

timed sets,

mixed-context sets,

mastery checks,

recall checks,

contrast drills,

error-correction drills.

Domain E — Instructional Packaging
This domain creates:

lesson blocks,

worked examples,

explanation variants,

contrast sheets,

retrieval packs,

recap cards,

memory anchors,

assignment bundles.

Domain F — Content Quality and Trust
This domain rates:

source trust,

extraction confidence,

ambiguity,

instructional usefulness,

representation coverage,

exam alignment,

misconception coverage.

Domain G — Content Need and Gap Detection
This domain detects:

missing topic content,

missing representation coverage,

missing proof-support artifacts,

weak question diversity,

missing misconception repair materials,

insufficient timed material,

weak delayed recall assets.

Domain H — Content Governance and Feedback
This domain tracks:

which artifacts were used,

whether they worked,

when they should be retired,

when a generated artifact must be revised,

when source trust should be downgraded.

4. Canonical content ontology
The content layer needs its own ontology that is compatible with the rest of the coach brain.

4.1 Source ontology
Entity family:

curriculum source

textbook source

notes source

question-bank source

marking-scheme source

glossary source

formula source

generated source

Each source should carry:

source type

publisher/origin

curriculum alignment

education level

trust baseline

extraction status

parse confidence

usage history

4.2 Knowledge object ontology
Core object families:

subject

topic

concept

sub-concept

prerequisite

formula

representation

misconception class

example

procedure

theorem/principle

definition

glossary entry

4.3 Artifact ontology
Instructional/assessment artifacts should include:

note artifact

explanation artifact

worked example artifact

drill artifact

assessment artifact

contrast artifact

formula card

glossary card

recap artifact

assignment artifact

pressure probe artifact

delayed recall artifact

4.4 Representation ontology
Every content object should know which representation modes it supports:

verbal

symbolic

numeric

diagrammatic

graphical

applied/word-problem

procedural

comparative

4.5 Proof-support ontology
Artifacts should also know which proof gates they support:

explain

independent_solve

transfer

mixed_context

delayed_recall

light_pressure

misconception_clearance

interference_clearance

That is vital because Coverage already uses proof contracts and proof-blocked states as part of portfolio logic. 


5. Canonical content entities
I would introduce a dedicated content namespace, for example cx_ for content intelligence objects.

5.1 Source registry
cx_sources

Stores raw source records:

source type

title

origin

subject

grade level

curriculum system

trust baseline

parse status

5.2 Source segments

Stores chunked source units:

source id

raw text / structured payload

OCR confidence where relevant

extracted entity links

5.3 Extracted concept objects
cx_concept_objects

Stores concept-aligned extracted units:

concept object id

concept id

5.4 Glossary entries

Stores:

term

Stores final coach-usable artifacts:

Stores unmet needs:

This is crucial because the brain should explicitly know when it lacks content, not silently improvise low-quality substitutes.

5.10 Content usage outcomes
cx_content_usage_outcomes

Stores:

6. Curriculum absorption pipeline
This is the main ingestion pipeline for formal curriculum and syllabus materials.

Stage 1: Source registration
The curriculum document is registered with:

subject

level

curriculum system

source trust

version

source lineage

Stage 2: Structural parse
Extract:

subject hierarchy

topics

subtopics

objectives

skills

assessment expectations

sequencing hints

prerequisites if stated

Stage 3: Curriculum graph formation
Map parsed items into:

j_subjects

j_topics

j_concepts

j_prerequisite_edges

j_exam_blueprint_links

Stage 4: Objective decomposition
Each topic is broken into:

concept atoms

formula units

procedure units

definition units

misconception-sensitive units

representation expectations

Stage 5: Coverage expectation seeding
The curriculum should seed expectations like:

minimum representation coverage

minimum proof-support coverage

minimum question diversity

required misconception coverage

exam leverage weighting

Stage 6: Gap requests
If the curriculum graph exists but content objects do not, the system emits content requests automatically.

This aligns with your earlier vision that once curriculum lands, the deeper intelligence layers should start populating topics with notes, glossary entries, questions, and other material rather than waiting passively.

7. Textbook and note absorption pipeline
This is where raw academic material becomes structured coach intelligence.

Stage 1: Source ingestion
Register textbook, scan, or notes bundle.

Stage 2: Parsing and segmentation
Split into:

chapter

section

concept passage

example block

exercise block

formula block

summary block

diagram block

Stage 3: Entity extraction
Extract:

topic references

concept mentions

formulas

examples

definitions

procedures

comparisons

misconceptions or warnings

diagrams/images metadata

Stage 4: Alignment
Map each extracted unit onto the canonical knowledge graph:

which topic

which concept

which representation mode

which prerequisite zone

which possible proof gate support

Stage 5: Packaging
Turn aligned units into reusable content objects:

explanation units

worked examples

procedure steps

formula cards

glossary entries

misconception notes

Stage 6: Quality scoring
Rate each object for:

clarity

concept precision

curriculum alignment

ambiguity

representation richness

instructional usefulness

Stage 7: Artifact generation or supplementation
If the textbook has the concept but not the needed artifact type, generate or assemble missing variants:

simpler explanation

contrast explanation

timed practice set

delayed recall recap

misconception-targeted drill

8. Question-bank and past-question absorption pipeline
This is especially important because the coach needs a strong assessment brain.

Stage 1: Source registration
Register:

past questions

tests

quizzes

mocks

marking schemes

Stage 2: Question-answer reconciliation
If questions and answers are separate, map them reliably.

Stage 3: Question intelligence extraction
For each item, determine:

topic alignment

concept alignment

question family

representation mode

difficulty

timing sensitivity

proof-support type

likely misconception distractors

transfer level

mixed-context status

This strongly complements your existing ambition around question intelligence across all surfaces.

Stage 4: Explanation and marking scheme extraction
Attach:

correct answer

explanation

worked steps

rubrics where relevant

Stage 5: Question packaging
Store items as:

reusable question objects

grouped diagnostic sets

timed micro-check candidates

mixed-context proof candidates

misconception-targeting items

pressure probes

delayed recall checks

Stage 6: Diversity and sufficiency analysis
The system should know:

whether a topic has enough easy/medium/hard items

enough representation diversity

enough misconception-targeting items

enough speed-oriented items

enough transfer items

If not, it should raise content requests.

9. Glossary and formula intelligence constitution
This deserves its own sub-layer because definitions, terms, and formulas are not “extra content”; they are high-frequency cognitive anchors.

9.1 Glossary intelligence
For each glossary term, the system should maintain:

formal definition

simplified definition

related terms

commonly confused terms

misconception warnings

linked formulae

linked examples

linked concept graph neighbors

audio-friendly explanation if needed

9.2 Formula intelligence
For each formula, the system should maintain:

formula statement

meaning of each symbol

when to use

when not to use

derivation or justification where needed

common misuse patterns

nearby formulas likely to cause confusion

worked examples

transfer variants

9.3 Formula/glossary interplay
A formula should be explorable through:

definition mode

concept mode

worked example mode

error/misuse mode

compare-with-neighbor mode

That makes glossary/formula content actually useful to the diagnostic and intervention engines, not just available in a library.

10. Content quality and trust constitution
This is non-negotiable.

10.1 Quality dimensions
Every content artifact should be rated on:

concept correctness

clarity

curriculum alignment

ambiguity

representation appropriateness

proof-support usefulness

misconception usefulness

learner-facing readability

assessment fidelity

redundancy / novelty

10.2 Trust dimensions
Every source and artifact should carry:

source trust baseline

extraction confidence

transformation confidence

human-verified status if available

usage success feedback

contradiction/harm flags

10.3 Quality laws
Law A
No artifact should be high-trust only because it came from a textbook. It must also survive extraction and alignment quality checks.

Law B
No generated artifact should become first-class without quality scoring and, where needed, structural validation.

Law C
Repeated harmful or confusing usage should downgrade artifact trust.

Law D
High-stakes artifacts for proof, risk, or exam-facing decisions should require higher trust thresholds than low-stakes review artifacts.

11. Content gap detection constitution
This is where the content layer becomes self-aware.

The system should continuously ask:

do we have enough for this topic?

enough for this proof gate?

enough for this learner profile?

enough for this misconception?

enough for this representation?

enough for timed vs untimed?

enough for interference repair?

Canonical gap families
missing_topic_foundation

missing_definition_support

missing_formula_support

missing_worked_examples

missing_transfer_items

missing_mixed_context_items

missing_delayed_recall_assets

missing_pressure_probe_assets

missing_misconception_repair_assets

missing_interference_contrast_assets

weak_representation_diversity

weak_question_difficulty_span

weak_explanation_clarity

weak_exam_alignment

Coverage already reasons about proof-blocked, speed-gap, and false-mastery-suspect portfolio states. This content gap layer gives it richer material reality underneath. 


12. Content request doctrine
When a gap is detected, the system should create a formal request object, not just “wish it had more content.”

A content request should specify:

scope: topic/concept/subject

request family

why this is needed

which engine triggered it

urgency

required artifact type

required representation mode

required proof-support or strategy-support tags

minimum quality threshold

Examples:

“Need mixed-context items for osmosis vs diffusion interference repair”

“Need delayed-recall recap artifact for Fractions topic”

“Need transfer examples for algebraic substitution”

“Need misconception-targeted contrast artifact for photosynthesis vs respiration”

This is how the content brain becomes operationally intelligent.

13. Content lifecycle
Content also needs a lifecycle.

Content states
registered

parsed

aligned

packaged

quality_scored

trusted_active

limited_use

suspect

retired

revised

revalidated

Meaning
registered — source known
parsed — segmented/extracted
aligned — mapped to graph
packaged — turned into content objects/artifacts
quality_scored — evaluated
trusted_active — available for normal use
limited_use — only safe for low-stakes or narrow contexts
suspect — quality or trust problem detected
retired — no longer used
revised — artifact updated
revalidated — passed quality checks again

This matters because content should not remain permanently active if usage outcomes show it confuses learners or fails to support proof.

14. Content usage feedback loop
This is where the content layer joins the rest of the brain.

Every artifact used in:

a session

an intervention

a drill

an assessment

a recap
should feed back:

did it help?

did it confuse?

did it support the intended proof gate?

did it fit the learner state?

did it produce better outcomes than alternatives?

That means content can build memory too:

“this explanation variant works well for abstract topics with low abstraction tolerance”

“this drill is too ambiguous under timed conditions”

“this contrast artifact is excellent for one interference pair but weak for another”

15. Cross-engine relationships
This content layer must serve the rest of the coach brain directly.

To Hypothesis Competition
Content metadata helps decide whether evidence truly supports:

misconception

interference

retrieval weakness

representation mismatch

To Learner State
Content usage outcomes help learn:

preferred modality

successful session length

mode affinity

That is compatible with your adaptive profile, which already tracks preferred families, successful session duration, and teaching mode preferences by topic type. 


To Teaching Strategy
Strategy needs artifact families matched to diagnosis.

To Timing
Timing needs content suitable for:

delayed recall

retest

pressure probes

short reinforcement windows

To Risk
Poor content coverage or poor artifact quality should be risk drivers.

To Adaptation
Repeated artifact underperformance should contribute to refine/reroute/stop signals.

To Coverage
Coverage should not just know that a topic exists; it should know whether enough trusted content exists to truly work the topic. Coverage already reads proof contracts and evidence spines; it should also read content sufficiency signals. 


To Session Composer
Session composition depends on block-ready artifacts.

To Governance
Low-trust or suspect artifacts should be blocked from high-stakes use.

17. Final doctrine
The content brain should behave like this:

absorb curriculum as the canonical frame

absorb textbooks, notes, past questions, glossaries, formulas, and explanations as raw material

extract structure

align everything to the knowledge graph

package it into instructional and assessment objects

rate trust and quality

detect gaps

create requests for missing artifacts

learn from actual usage outcomes

and continuously improve the instructional inventory available to the coach

Your current audited architecture already has the beginnings of this through concept-node ingestion, contrast/misconception metadata use, evidence-point normalization, proof-contract outputs, coverage signal integration, adaptive profiling, and planner feedback.

Thought for 19s
Good. Now we define the Runtime Orchestration and Deployment Constitution.

This is the production law of the coach brain: what runs synchronously, what runs asynchronously, what is cached, what must be recomputed, how events flow, how failures are contained, and how the whole system stays fast, stable, and inspectable under real usage.

Your audited nucleus already gives the first production signals: Coverage has smart caching with staleness detection, Sequencing monitors churn and warns to lock a model for 3 days, Timing writes decisions consumed by the Study Planner and Delayed Recall Queue, Risk writes assessments consumed by the planner, and Adaptation writes protect/compress/reroute signals plus dwell-aware pacing. 

 


Runtime Orchestration and Deployment Constitution
1. Purpose
The full intelligence system is too large to run as one monolithic “analyze everything every time” call.

The runtime must answer:

what needs an immediate answer for the learner right now?

what can happen a few seconds later?

what should be recomputed in the background?

what should only run when triggered?

what should be cached?

what must be invalidated immediately?

how do we preserve consistency when many engines write related state?

how do we avoid thrash, stale plans, and contradictory outputs?

The core doctrine is:

the brain must feel instantaneous at the surface, but deep in the back it must be continuously reconciling, learning, and preparing better future decisions.

2. Foundational runtime laws
Law 1: not all intelligence is synchronous
Some decisions must happen inline with learner interaction.
Others should happen in short-latency async flows.
Others should run in batch or trigger-based background passes.

Law 2: every engine belongs to an execution tier
No engine should have an ambiguous runtime role.

Law 3: runtime must protect the learner experience first
If full-system recomputation is expensive, the learner should still get a safe, traceable, high-quality local decision.

Law 4: stale intelligence must be detectable
Cached outputs are fine.
Silent stale outputs are not.

Law 5: every consequential learner-facing release must pass governance
Even if parts of the brain run asynchronously, final learner-facing plans must respect release gates.

Law 6: recomputation should be event-driven before it is schedule-driven
The best trigger is usually meaningful state change, not a blind cron.

3. Runtime tier model
I would divide the full brain into 5 runtime tiers.

Tier 0 — Inline Interaction Tier
This is ultra-fast runtime.

Budget:

tens to low hundreds of milliseconds where possible

only local scope

no heavy portfolio recomputation

Purpose:

respond to learner interaction safely and immediately

Examples:

response recording

evidence normalization

local concept/topic evidence update

learner micro-state update

lightweight hypothesis rerank for the active topic

session-block success/failure evaluation

immediate protection checks

Tier 1 — Session Intelligence Tier
This is short-latency orchestration.

Budget:

sub-second to a few seconds

Purpose:

produce or revise the active session plan

adapt during or right after a session

Examples:

session composer

teaching decision refresh

timing refresh for the active topic

active-topic risk refresh

local adaptation review

content selection for current session blocks

governance for session release

Tier 2 — Planner Tier
This is medium-latency cross-topic planning.

Budget:

a few seconds

not for every click

triggered by meaningful portfolio changes

Purpose:

recompute portfolio shape and near-term study plan

Examples:

coverage classification refresh

sequencing refresh

planner priority rebuild

topic ranking updates

retest queue updates

delayed recall queue updates

This fits the current nucleus, where Study Planner reads ic_risk_assessments, ic_adaptation_log, and ic_timing_decisions to boost or suppress topic ranking, including risk > 0.6 boosts, protect boosts, reroute penalties, retest-due-today jumps, and compress-driven session reduction. 


Tier 3 — Deep Intelligence Tier
This is heavier triggered analysis.

Budget:

seconds to tens of seconds

often asynchronous

Purpose:

resolve ambiguity, mine patterns, reconcile cross-engine truth, build better future priors

Examples:

diagnostic experiment selection

systemic pattern mining

recurrence mining

learner profile rebuild

strategy memory update

contradiction analysis across scopes

forecast recomputation

Tier 4 — Background Knowledge Tier
This is the slow substrate and content brain.

Budget:

asynchronous

minutes or schedule-driven where needed

Purpose:

ingest sources

parse curriculum and textbooks

quality-score content

build new artifacts

revalidate content trust

mine long-range patterns

recalibrate thresholds

Examples:

content ingestion pipelines

question-bank absorption

artifact quality rescoring

curriculum graph rebuilds after new uploads

global analytics and calibration jobs

4. Engine-to-tier mapping
Tier 0 engines
Response Evidence Engine

Behavior Evidence Engine

Evidence Normalization Engine

local Concept State update

local Learner micro-state update

immediate Protection checks

immediate block-level evaluation

Tier 1 engines
Hypothesis Competition Engine for active topic

Misconception Detection Engine

Interference Detection Engine

Teaching Strategy Engine

Session Composer Engine

Content Selection Engine

Timing Engine for active topic/session

Confidence Gate / Policy Guardrail for session release

Tier 2 engines
Coverage Engine

Sequencing Engine

Risk Engine at portfolio scope

Study Planner assembly

Delayed Recall Queue builder

Retest Queue builder

Tier 3 engines
Adaptation Engine full pass

Diagnostic Experiment Engine

Forecast Engine

systemic pattern miners

learner adaptive profile rebuild

contradiction cluster analysis

coach self-evaluation

Tier 4 engines
Curriculum ingestion

textbook/question-bank parsing

artifact packaging

content gap detection

content request generation

quality rescoring

trust revalidation

5. Runtime object model
The runtime should operate around a few canonical envelopes.

5.1 Interaction Envelope
Created whenever the learner answers, requests help, or completes a block.

Contains:

learner id

session id

active topic/concept

raw event

immediate evidence packet refs

current session block ref

current learner micro-state ref

Purpose:

Tier 0 and part of Tier 1 execution

5.2 Session Decision Envelope
This is the live active-session brain context.

Contains:

learner state ref

topic state ref

dominant hypotheses

active misconceptions/interference

teaching decision

timing constraints

active protection rules

selected content refs

governance status

trace ref

This matches the constitutional idea we defined earlier and should be the core live session object.

5.3 Planning Envelope
This is for portfolio recomputation.

Contains:

learner-global state

subject states

topic states

risk assessments

adaptation log slice

timing decisions

coverage snapshots

exam horizon

current tensions/meta-signals

5.4 Deep Analysis Envelope
This is for Tier 3/4 jobs.

Contains:

historical windows

memory records

recurrence slices

contradiction history

strategy memory

forecast baselines

content sufficiency state

6. Event choreography in production
The full system should be event-driven with bounded recomputation.

6.1 Primary live events
response.recorded

hint.used

question.skipped

session.block_completed

session.completed

artifact.used

intervention.completed

6.2 Derived events
concept.state.updated

learner.state.updated

hypothesis.reranked

misconception.detected

interference.detected

protection.rule.created

timing.decision.emitted

session.plan.revised

risk.assessed

adaptation.event.emitted

contradiction.detected

6.3 Background events
recurrence.pattern.updated

learner.profile.rebuilt

systemic.pattern.detected

content.gap.detected

artifact.trust.changed

forecast.updated

6.4 Event routing doctrine
A single event should not wake the entire brain.

Examples:

response.recorded should wake Tier 0 immediately, then maybe Tier 1 if the active block or active topic state changed materially.

session.completed should wake Tier 1, Tier 2, and selected Tier 3 jobs.

curriculum.uploaded should wake Tier 4, then later emit graph/content-ready events that wake Tier 2/Tier 3 where relevant.

7. Sync vs async constitution
This boundary is vital.

7.1 Must run synchronously
These should usually run inline with active learner interaction:

raw event persistence

evidence packet creation

learner micro-state update

block-local success evaluation

active protection validation

current session block next-step decision

current session plan release validation

Reason:
the learner cannot wait for safe next-step logic.

7.2 Prefer near-async but fast
These can run just after the learner event, often within the same user-visible episode:

active-topic hypothesis rerank

active-topic misconception/interference scan

session composer revision

content selection refresh

local timing refresh

7.3 Async by default
These should usually run off the immediate interaction path:

full portfolio risk refresh

coverage snapshot rebuild

sequence recomputation

systemic pattern mining

forecast rebuild

content sufficiency refresh

learner profile rebuild

7.4 Trigger-only async
These should run only when signals justify them:

contradiction cluster analysis

diagnostic probe generation

deep curriculum re-alignment after source updates

threshold recalibration

8. Bounded recomputation doctrine
The full brain must not recompute everything all the time.

8.1 Recompute scopes
Local scope
Only the active concept/topic/session.

Use when:

response arrives

hint used

immediate block ends

Topic scope
All topic-local state and artifacts.

Use when:

topic diagnosis materially changed

intervention completed

proof gate changed

misconception/interference detected

Subject scope
All topics in a subject.

Use when:

sequence relevance changes

subject regression wave appears

exam blueprint or time tension changes materially

Portfolio scope
All learner planning state.

Use when:

risk shifts across several topics

major adaptation emitted

stop new learning activated

exam horizon materially changes

Deep memory scope
Historical and cross-scope mining.

Use when:

session completed

recurrence thresholds crossed

enough new evidence accumulated

9. Cache constitution
Caching is mandatory in a system this large, but it must be principled.

The audited Coverage engine already has smart caching with staleness detection against latest updates in topic profiles, academic events, and coaching episodes. That should become a system-wide standard, not just one-engine behavior. 


9.1 Cache classes
Hot cache
Used for live session data.
Examples:

active learner state

active topic state

current session envelope

active protection rules

latest content refs for current block

TTL:

very short

event-invalidated

Warm cache
Used for near-term planning.
Examples:

current coverage snapshot

latest sequence plan

latest risk summary

current planner ranking

delayed recall queue

TTL:

moderate

invalidated by topic/risk/adaptation/timing updates

Cold cache
Used for deep analysis outputs.
Examples:

forecast

systemic patterns

learner profile summary

strategy effectiveness summaries

TTL:

longer

invalidated by enough accumulated evidence or explicit triggers

9.2 Cache invalidation law
Never use pure TTL alone for intelligence truth.
Always combine:

TTL or freshness window

source-version checks

event-based invalidation

9.3 Cache lineage
Every cached object should know:

source refs

generation time

invalidation dependencies

producing engine version

10. Material-change doctrine
A big production problem is unnecessary recomputation because every small update is treated as meaningful.

The runtime needs a material_change standard.

10.1 Material changes include
dominant hypothesis changed

learner confidence state changed band

risk crossed band boundary

protection rule created/expired

proof gate status changed

contradiction opened

sequence churn above threshold

timing due-now status changed

adaptation mode changed

10.2 Non-material changes
tiny numeric drift within same band

repeated evidence that only strengthens already-stable local truth slightly

stale forecast refresh without new drivers

Only material changes should wake bigger tiers.

11. Release architecture
Learner-facing release should be treated as a formal product stage.

11.1 Draft phase
Engines produce candidate outputs.

11.2 Governance phase
Run:

confidence gate

contradiction check

policy guardrail

protection compliance

trace assembly

11.3 Release phase
Promote candidate session or plan to learner-visible status.

11.4 Revision phase
If new material evidence appears before execution, revoke or revise release.

This is especially important for queued sessions/plans.

12. Queue constitution
The system needs explicit queues.

12.1 Active Session Queue
For immediate current-session work:

block transitions

active content fetch

live micro-adaptation

post-block evaluation

12.2 Retest Queue
Built from mastery and timing logic.

Your audited Timing engine already produces retest scheduling and the planner already boosts retest-due-today topics.

12.3 Delayed Recall Queue
Built from timing decisions and reinforced by golden window logic.

The current nucleus already has a delayed recall queue consuming timing decisions.

12.4 Planner Refresh Queue
For coverage, risk, adaptation, and sequencing changes that affect ranking.

12.5 Deep Analysis Queue
For recurrence mining, profile rebuilds, contradiction clusters, forecasts.

12.6 Content Jobs Queue
For parsing, alignment, artifact generation, gap filling, revalidation.

13. Orchestrator design
The orchestrator should be split into several bounded coordinators, not one god-service.

13.1 Interaction Orchestrator
Handles:

live learner events

Tier 0 and Tier 1 chains

active session envelope maintenance

13.2 Planner Orchestrator
Handles:

Tier 2 recomputation

ranking rebuilds

retest and recall queue assembly

portfolio refreshes

13.3 Deep Analysis Orchestrator
Handles:

Tier 3 jobs

recurrence mining

adaptive profile rebuild

contradiction escalations

forecasts

13.4 Content Orchestrator
Handles:

Tier 4 ingestion

parsing

artifact packaging

trust/quality rescoring

content requests

13.5 Governance Orchestrator
Handles:

release gates

arbitration

policy checks

trace finalization

This keeps the runtime modular and failure-contained.

14. State persistence doctrine
This system needs a split between:

14.1 Source-of-truth state
Persistent, durable, versioned.
Examples:

evidence packets

hypotheses

topic states

mastery contracts

timing decisions

risk assessments

adaptation events

content artifacts

memory records

contradictions

traces

14.2 Derived read models
Fast, denormalized, easily rebuildable.
Examples:

current planner ranking

learner dashboard state

session summary view

topic current card

agenda for today

active warnings

Read models should be disposable.
Source-of-truth state should not be.

15. Consistency model
You do not need one giant transaction across the entire brain. That would be too heavy and brittle.

You do need bounded consistency.

15.1 Strong consistency zones
Use stronger consistency for:

event persistence

session release decision

protection-rule application to active session

mastery verdict writes

contradiction and arbitration writes

15.2 Eventual consistency zones
Use eventual consistency for:

planner refresh

forecasts

systemic patterns

strategy memory rebuild

content quality aggregates

15.3 Idempotency law
Every async job must be safely rerunnable.
This is essential for:

queue retries

out-of-order events

worker crashes

recompute after deployment

16. Failure containment
A production brain must degrade gracefully.

16.1 If Tier 3 fails
The learner should still get:

live safe session behavior

current protection rules

current local strategy

current active topic plan

What degrades:

deeper forecasts

systemic pattern freshness

profile refinement freshness

16.2 If Tier 2 fails
The learner should still get:

active session intelligence

existing last-good planner ranking

What degrades:

fresh portfolio prioritization

recall/retest queue freshness

new sequence changes

16.3 If content jobs fail
The learner should still use:

last trusted active artifacts

existing content inventory

What degrades:

new source absorption

newly requested artifact generation

16.4 If governance fails
Release should fail closed for high-stakes new plans.
For already active sessions, fall back to last-approved safe local envelope.

17. Observability constitution
A brain this large must be monitored like infrastructure.

17.1 Engine metrics
Track for every engine:

invocation count

success/failure rate

latency

stale-input rate

confidence distribution

output churn rate

17.2 Runtime metrics
Track:

queue lag by tier

cache hit/miss rate

invalidation frequency

recompute fan-out

release rejection rate

contradiction open duration

arbitration count

policy hit count

17.3 Educational outcome metrics
Track:

false mastery rate

session-to-improvement latency

intervention effectiveness by family

recall retention by interval

adaptation validation rate

planner precision

warning precision

17.4 Trace coverage
Track:

percentage of major decisions with complete trace

missing lineage incidents

stale-decision release incidents

18. Deployment topology
A clean deployment shape would separate concerns.

18.1 API surface
Serves:

learner app

parent/teacher portals

admin/curriculum portal

18.2 Interaction workers
For Tier 0 and Tier 1 low-latency tasks.

18.3 Planning workers
For Tier 2 jobs:

coverage

sequencing

risk

planner ranking

recall/retest queues

18.4 Deep-analysis workers
For Tier 3 jobs:

adaptation full passes

pattern miners

forecasts

contradiction clusters

self-evaluation

18.5 Content workers
For Tier 4 jobs:

ingestion

parsing

alignment

packaging

quality/trust scoring

18.6 Governance workers
For release gating, arbitration, and trace assembly when not inline.

18.7 Datastores
You will typically need:

primary relational store for durable intelligence state

queue/event bus

cache layer

object/blob storage for source materials and large artifacts

possibly search index/vector layer for content retrieval if you choose that route

19. Versioning and rollout doctrine
This system cannot be deployed with opaque logic drift.

19.1 Version all of these
engine implementations

threshold bundles

strategy taxonomies

content quality rules

proof gate templates

tension rules

planner scoring rules

19.2 Store producing version on major outputs
Example:

risk assessment should know which risk model version created it

sequence plan should know sequencing model version

mastery verdict should know proof template version

19.3 Safe rollout
When deploying new logic:

keep old outputs queryable

compare old vs new on shadow runs where feasible

log major divergences

only promote after quality review

20. Live-session adaptation doctrine
One of the most delicate runtime questions is whether the brain may change course during a session.

Answer: yes, but with discipline.

20.1 Allowed live adaptations
reorder remaining blocks

switch from independent to scaffolded

insert confidence-warmup

remove timed block if protection triggered

shorten session if overload rises

convert block into probe if ambiguity spikes

20.2 Disallowed live adaptations without explicit governance
certify mastery mid-chaos on thin evidence

change to a radically different long-horizon strategy without trace

ignore active hard protection rules

invalidate whole session plan without fallback safe block path

21. Runtime link to audited nucleus
A few parts of your current engine already act like the beginning of this runtime constitution:

Coverage already does smart caching with staleness detection against source-update timestamps. 


Sequencing already monitors churn and warns about locking a model. 


Timing already writes decisions consumed by the Study Planner and Delayed Recall Queue.

Risk already writes persisted assessments that the planner uses for risk-driven boosts, and Adaptation already writes protect/compress/reroute signals the planner consumes.

Adaptation already has dwell-aware pacing that suppresses over-adapting and only allows high-urgency changes.

So this production constitution is not theoretical. It is the disciplined expansion of patterns your current nucleus already contains.

22. Final doctrine
The full day-1 coach brain should run like this:

fast local intelligence at the point of interaction

short-latency session intelligence for live coaching

medium-latency portfolio planning for next-best actions

deeper asynchronous analysis for recurrence, forecasting, and systemic truth

slow background content and curriculum intelligence

governance across all release points

caches with explicit freshness and invalidation

queues with bounded recomputation

failure containment by tier

full observability and traceability

That is how the intelligence engine becomes not just deep, but deployable.

The next document should define the testing, evaluation, and benchmark constitution — the formal scenarios, adversarial cases, scorecards, regression suites, and educational success metrics that tell you whether this brain is actually getting smarter or merely getting bigger.


proceed

I’m defining the proof-of-intelligence layer now: how to test this brain, what counts as a pass, which failures matter most, and how to tell real improvement from added complexity.

Thought for 11s
Good. Now we define the Testing, Evaluation, and Benchmark Constitution.

This is the layer that answers the hardest practical question:

How will you know this brain is actually becoming better, rather than merely becoming larger, more complex, and more confident?

Your audited nucleus already gives the first hints of what must be tested: Topic Intelligence builds hypothesis rankings and a 6-checkpoint mastery proof contract, Sequencing compares multiple models and monitors churn, Timing controls review/retest/golden windows, Risk emits persisted assessments and early warnings, and Adaptation uses triggers, dwell logic, systemic patterns, and stop lists. 

 


Testing, Evaluation, and Benchmark Constitution
1. Purpose
This constitution defines how the academic coach proves four things:

that it is correct enough to be trusted,

that it is useful enough to improve learners,

that it is stable enough to operate over time,

and that it is self-improving rather than self-amplifying.

It must test not only code quality, but also:

reasoning quality,

diagnosis quality,

intervention quality,

proof quality,

timing quality,

protection quality,

adaptation quality,

governance quality,

and long-horizon educational outcomes.

Without this layer, the system will drift into one of three dangerous illusions:

size illusion — more code, same intelligence

confidence illusion — stronger outputs, weaker truth

activity illusion — lots of actions, little real learner progress

2. Foundational testing laws
Law 1: every major engine must have its own correctness tests
No engine should be trusted only through end-to-end behavior.

Law 2: the whole brain must also be tested as a system
A set of individually good engines can still produce bad global behavior.

Law 3: educational truth matters more than engineering neatness
A clean architecture that still produces false mastery or harmful pacing is not good enough.

Law 4: benchmark scenarios must include adversarial learner realities
Not just easy success cases.

Law 5: the most dangerous failures should be weighted most heavily
Especially:

false mastery,

harmful pressure escalation,

overconfident diagnosis,

unstable adaptation,

and silent contradiction.

Law 6: every major new model/rule/version should be benchmarked against the previous one
No silent logic drift.

3. What must be tested
I would divide testing into 8 permanent layers.

Layer A — Engine Correctness Testing
Does each engine behave correctly on well-defined inputs?

Layer B — Cross-Engine Integration Testing
Do related engines compose without contradiction or corruption?

Layer C — Scenario Simulation Testing
Does the brain behave correctly across realistic learner situations?

Layer D — Adversarial and Edge-Case Testing
Does it fail safely when reality is messy?

Layer E — Longitudinal Evaluation
Does it stay good across weeks, not just one session?

Layer F — Runtime and Reliability Evaluation
Does it remain stable in production?

Layer G — Educational Outcome Evaluation
Does it materially improve learning?

Layer H — Governance and Trust Evaluation
Does it remain inspectable, bounded, and honest under uncertainty?

4. Core benchmark families
4.1 Golden Path Benchmarks
These are canonical “healthy reasoning” cases.

Examples:

learner has real prerequisite weakness

learner is accurate but weak under pressure

learner has a specific misconception

learner is confused between two close concepts

learner repaired a topic and now needs delayed recall verification

learner is ready for advance after true proof

Purpose:
to verify the intended ideal behavior of the system.

4.2 Ambiguity Benchmarks
These test whether the system handles uncertainty properly.

Examples:

retrieval weakness vs pressure weakness

misconception vs careless repeated slip

interference vs general low understanding

fatigue-mediated error vs true knowledge weakness

Expected behavior:

maintain competing hypotheses

reduce hardness when ambiguity high

propose probes when necessary

avoid premature strong actions

4.3 False Mastery Benchmarks
These are among the most important.

Examples:

learner succeeds immediately after teaching but fails next day

learner solves correctly but cannot explain

learner works fine in isolation but fails mixed-context

learner works untimed but collapses under light timing

learner memorized pattern but cannot transfer

Expected behavior:

do not emit mastery_proven

keep proof incomplete or suspect

trigger retest/reinforcement/delayed recall logic

4.4 Protection Benchmarks
These test whether the coach restrains itself when needed.

Examples:

learner confidence fragile after repeated misses

fatigue high near session end

active no_timing protection exists

interference active and mixed-context block proposed

overload tension critical but high-density session proposed

Expected behavior:

emit or respect protection rules

downgrade or block unsafe actions

preserve safe session behavior

4.5 Adaptation Benchmarks
These test whether the system changes course correctly.

Examples:

strategy should refine, not reroute

reroute needed after repeated ineffective family

dwell should suppress excessive switching

contradiction should reopen diagnosis

plan tension should cause compression

confidence tension should cause protection

Your current engine already has these seeds through protect/compress/reroute and dwell-aware suppression. 


4.6 Systemic Pattern Benchmarks
These test whether the coach sees patterns bigger than one topic.

Examples:

same intervention family failing across 3 topics

multiple math topics regressing together

several topics repaired but all decaying in recall

stable topics all too slow under timed conditions

Expected behavior:

detect systemic pattern

elevate adaptation/risk

modify future strategy or timing globally

Your current engine already names several of these systemic patterns. 


4.7 Governance Benchmarks
These test whether the system remains honest.

Examples:

low-confidence diagnosis but hard action proposed

contradiction unresolved but plan tries to release

pressure exposure proposed under active no_timing block

mastery verdict without enough gates

sequence advance while blocked prerequisite risk still critical

Expected behavior:

confidence gate hit

policy hit

arbitration

release denial or downgrade

4.8 Runtime Benchmarks
These test the production behavior.

Examples:

many rapid learner responses

queue lag

stale cache invalidation

out-of-order events

async retry after worker crash

planner uses stale risk output

governance worker unavailable

Expected behavior:

safe degradation

idempotent reruns

release fail-closed where appropriate

session continues with last safe local envelope

5. Engine-by-engine scorecards
Every core engine should have a formal scorecard.

5.1 Hypothesis Competition Engine scorecard
Measure:

top-hypothesis accuracy

ambiguity calibration quality

false confirmation rate

useful probe recommendation rate

diagnosis reversal rate

time-to-stable-leading-hypothesis

5.2 Learner State Engine scorecard
Measure:

pressure vulnerability prediction quality

fatigue inference quality

recovery prediction quality

engagement drift lead time

confidence fragility detection quality

overreaction rate to single bad sessions

5.3 Misconception Detection Engine scorecard
Measure:

misconception precision

misconception recall

slip-vs-structure discrimination quality

recurrence capture quality

repair-status accuracy

5.4 Interference Detection Engine scorecard
Measure:

pairwise interference precision

repair benefit after detection

false adjacency alarms

early detection before visible collapse

5.5 Mastery Proof Engine scorecard
Measure:

false mastery rate

under-certification rate

gate bottleneck accuracy

delayed recall invalidation rate

proof confidence calibration

5.6 Teaching Strategy Engine scorecard
Measure:

strategy fit success

fallback usefulness

mismatch rate

family-level success by diagnosis class

harmful strategy deployment rate

5.7 Timing Engine scorecard
Measure:

review timing effectiveness

retest timing effectiveness

delayed recall yield

pressure introduction appropriateness

overload-causing schedule rate

5.8 Risk Engine scorecard
Measure:

warning precision

warning recall

lead time before meaningful failure

false critical alert rate

driver decomposition usefulness

5.9 Adaptation Engine scorecard
Measure:

validated adaptation rate

invalidated adaptation rate

churn rate

dwell override quality

reopen necessity accuracy

5.10 Session Composer Engine scorecard
Measure:

objective attainment rate

block-sequence effectiveness

overpacked session rate

poor-closure rate

session-to-outcome fit

5.11 Content Selection Engine scorecard
Measure:

artifact fit quality

content insufficiency detection precision

artifact usefulness

harmful/confusing artifact rate

representation fit quality

5.12 Governance cluster scorecard
Measure:

confidence gate precision

contradiction detection precision

policy hit correctness

arbitration quality

trace completeness

6. Benchmark scenario library
You need a permanent scenario library, not scattered examples.

I would define a Coach Intelligence Benchmark Corpus with fixed scenario families.

6.1 Scenario format
Each scenario should contain:

learner profile

curriculum/topic context

knowledge graph slice

prior memory slice

recent evidence history

active tensions/meta-signals

desired hidden truth

acceptable decisions

unacceptable decisions

expected lifecycle changes

expected governance outcomes

6.2 Scenario classes
Class A — Clean cases
Simple clear cases with one dominant cause.

Class B — Mixed cases
Multiple plausible causes.

Class C — Fragility cases
Looks good but unstable.

Class D — Pressure cases
Untimed okay, timed bad.

Class E — Interference cases
Two nearby ideas colliding.

Class F — Recurrence cases
Previously repaired issue returns.

Class G — Portfolio cases
Cross-topic and time-budget problems.

Class H — Governance cases
Where the right answer is restraint.

7. Adversarial benchmark families
This is extremely important because real learners are messy.

7.1 Noisy evidence benchmarks
Examples:

lucky guessing

noisy OCR-imported assessment

teacher note partially wrong

ambiguous question wording

unstable learner-state signal

Goal:
ensure the system does not become overconfident from noisy evidence.

7.2 Contradictory evidence benchmarks
Examples:

recent strong result, older broad weak history

topic appears stable, but same misconception recurs

learner says confident, performance says unstable

untimed success but mixed-context collapse

Goal:
ensure contradiction handling works.

7.3 Misleading trend benchmarks
Examples:

short-term improvement but long-term decay

confidence rising while actual retention falling

session completion looks good but progress flat

Goal:
ensure the system does not confuse activity for progress.

7.4 Harm-risk benchmarks
Examples:

fragile learner exposed to premature timing

overloaded learner given dense mixed session

high ambiguity case forced into hard reroute

topic advanced on false proof

Goal:
ensure protective behavior dominates when needed.

7.5 Runtime adversarial benchmarks
Examples:

duplicate events

delayed events

worker retry

stale cache usage attempt

partial write then crash

governance unavailable during release

Goal:
ensure runtime safety and idempotency.

8. Longitudinal evaluation constitution
One of the biggest mistakes would be to evaluate only session-local success.

This coach must be evaluated longitudinally.

8.1 Longitudinal windows
At minimum:

same-session

next-session

3-day window

1-week window

exam-horizon window

8.2 Longitudinal truths to test
repair durability

delayed recall stability

recurrence rate

adaptation validation over time

sequence effectiveness over time

pressure-readiness build-up

strategy-family diminishing returns

memory usefulness

8.3 Longitudinal metrics
relapse rate after repair

false mastery invalidation rate

delayed recall survival

time from diagnosis to stable proof

reopen rate by topic family

long-term intervention effectiveness

actual vs forecasted readiness

This is where the brain proves it can coach across time, not just in bursts.

9. Educational outcome metrics
This layer matters most in the end.

9.1 Learner-level outcomes
mastery-proven topic count

true retention rate

transfer success rate

pressure performance improvement

recovery after failure improvement

confidence stability improvement

reduced recurrence of same misconception/interference

9.2 Portfolio-level outcomes
coverage quality, not just breadth

review debt reduction

high-yield weak-topic reduction

false-readiness reduction

readiness projection accuracy

9.3 Session-level outcomes
session objective attainment

block efficiency

harmful-session rate

overfatigue session rate

session-to-delayed-gain rate

9.4 Coach-quality outcomes
diagnostic accuracy

adaptation usefulness

governance correctness

intervention family effectiveness

release safety quality

10. The most important failure metrics
Some failures should dominate dashboards and regression gates.

Tier-1 critical failures
False mastery rate
How often the system certifies too early.

Harmful pressure rate
How often the learner is exposed to timing/pressure when not ready.

Overconfident diagnosis rate
How often hard actions are taken from weak evidence.

Protection failure rate
How often blocking protections are bypassed or insufficient.

Contradiction leak rate
How often serious contradictions reach learner-facing release unresolved.

Adaptation thrash rate
How often the system oscillates unhelpfully.

Misconception miss rate
How often structural wrong models are missed and treated as generic weakness.

Interference miss rate
How often concept collision damage is not recognized.

If these are bad, the system is not safe enough, regardless of other improvements.

11. Calibration constitution
The coach must be calibrated, not just scored.

11.1 Confidence calibration
If the system says:

0.85 confidence mastery,
that should behave like 0.85 confidence mastery over many cases.

Test:

bucket outputs by confidence

compare expected vs actual correctness

11.2 Risk calibration
If a topic is marked high risk, it should actually fail more often without intervention than medium-risk topics.

11.3 Readiness calibration
If a learner is marked ready for timed micro-check, pass rates should justify that.

11.4 Ambiguity calibration
If ambiguity is low, hard actions should generally be safer than when ambiguity high.

Calibration is one of the clearest signs the brain is maturing.

12. Human-review constitution
Some benchmark layers require expert or semi-expert review.

Especially for:

misconception correctness

explanation quality

curriculum alignment

content artifact quality

strategy appropriateness in tricky pedagogical cases

I would define three review bands:

Band A — machine-checkable
Examples:

stale-cache invalidation

trace completeness

event idempotency

proof gate structure completeness

Band B — rubric-checkable
Examples:

strategy fit

session composition coherence

content artifact alignment

contradiction classification quality

Band C — expert pedagogical review
Examples:

whether the chosen intervention truly matches the nuanced educational need

whether explanation artifacts are pedagogically excellent, not just valid

13. Replay and shadow evaluation
These are crucial once the system runs live.

13.1 Replay evaluation
Take historical learner episodes and rerun newer engine versions on them.

Compare:

old diagnosis vs new diagnosis

old strategy vs new strategy

old release vs new release

old forecast vs actual outcome

old vs new false mastery behavior

13.2 Shadow evaluation
In production, a new engine version can run in parallel without controlling live release.

Compare:

divergence rate

safety difference

confidence difference

contradiction count

expected educational gain indicators

This is essential for safe upgrades.

14. Benchmark datasets you should maintain
I would maintain at least 6 benchmark repositories.

14.1 Structured learner episode corpus
Curated episodes with hidden-truth labels where possible.

14.2 Misconception corpus
Items and traces labeled by misconception class.

14.3 Interference corpus
Known confusion-pair and collision cases.

14.4 Proof corpus
Cases about:

immediate success

delayed collapse

transfer gaps

pressure gaps

mixed-context fragility

14.5 Portfolio/planner corpus
Multi-topic, exam-horizon, review-debt, time-budget cases.

14.6 Governance/red-team corpus
Cases designed to induce:

overconfidence

contradiction

false mastery

unsafe pressure use

invalid release

15. Synthetic learner simulator
This project would benefit hugely from synthetic learner simulation.

Not as a replacement for real learners, but as a stress lab.

15.1 Learner simulator families
prerequisite-weak learner

misconception-heavy learner

pressure-vulnerable learner

fragile-confidence learner

high-accuracy/low-transfer learner

interference-prone learner

fast-recovering learner

slow-recovering learner

strategic-guessing learner

noisy-behavior learner

15.2 Why this matters
Simulation lets you:

run thousands of adaptation loops

test recurrence behavior

test timing strategies

test proof logic

test harmful pressure deployment rates

test policy and governance robustness

16. Release-gate evaluation doctrine
No major engine or threshold update should ship without passing fixed constitutional gates.

16.1 Required release gates
no regression on false mastery

no regression on harmful pressure rate

no regression on contradiction leak rate

acceptable calibration change

acceptable runtime latency/cost change

acceptable trace completeness

acceptable protection precision

16.2 Soft gates
improved diagnostic accuracy

improved intervention effectiveness

improved planner precision

improved delayed recall survival

If soft gates improve but hard safety gates worsen, release should not proceed.

17. Score aggregation doctrine
Do not reduce the whole brain to one magic intelligence score.

Instead maintain a constitutional scorecard with categories:

A. Safety
false mastery

harmful pressure

contradiction leakage

protection failures

B. Diagnostic quality
hypothesis accuracy

misconception/interference detection

ambiguity handling

C. Decision quality
strategy fit

timing fit

adaptation quality

planner precision

D. Educational quality
proof durability

transfer improvement

recall survival

confidence stability

session objective attainment

E. Governance quality
confidence calibration

trace completeness

policy correctness

arbitration correctness

F. Runtime quality
latency

queue lag

stale-output incidents

retry/idempotency success

This gives you a constitutional dashboard rather than a vanity metric.
